Reduction Methods for Second Figure Moods
Author classifies which reduction methods apply to each mood of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which reduction method (khulf, aks al-kubra, or aks al-tartib with aks al-natija) applies to each of the four moods of the second figure.

Core Idea
Each mood of the second figure has a specific reduction to the first figure.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms yields valid moods, but they are not in the perfect form that logicians consider self-evident. To demonstrate their validity, reduction methods transform them into first figure syllogisms. The passage you are about to study classifies exactly which methods work for each mood, based on the premise types. Understanding this classification will allow you to quickly assess and construct reductions for any second figure argument you encounter in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four moods of the second figure
The second figure has four valid moods: Cesare (EAE), Camestres (AEE), Festino (EIO), and Baroco (AOO). In the passage, these are numbered according to premise composition: first mood (Cesare): universal affirmative minor, universal negative major; second mood (Camestres): universal negative minor, universal affirmative major; third mood (Festino): particular affirmative minor, universal negative major; fourth mood (Baroco): particulars? (but khulf works for all).
The passage orders moods by the combination of minor and major quantifiers and qualities.
2
Identify the reduction methods listed
The author states that khulf (reductio ad absurdum) works for all four moods. Conversion of the major ('aks al-kubra) works for the first and third moods. A combination of conversion of order ('aks al-tartib) followed by conversion of the conclusion ('aks al-natija) works for the second mood only.
For the fourth mood (Baroco), only khulf is applicable.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the restrictions
Conversion of the major works when the minor premise is affirmative (allowing the converted major to become a universal negative minor in the first figure). Conversion of order plus conversion of the conclusion works for Camestres because transposing the premises and then converting the conclusion yields a valid first figure syllogism. Reflect on why these conditions are necessary.
The shared feature for 'aks al-kubra' is an affirmative minor and a universal negative major.
4
Practise applying the correct method
Given a description of a second figure mood (e.g., 'minor: particular affirmative, major: universal negative'), determine which reduction method applies and perform the reduction mentally. For instance, the third mood (Festino) uses 'aks al-kubra'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الخلف يجري في الضروب الأربعة، وأن عكس الكبرى يجري في الضرب الأول، وهو المركب من موجبة كلية صغرى، وسالبة كلية كبرى. وفي الضرب الثالث: وهو المركب من موجبة مجزئية صغرى، وسالبة كلية كبرى، وأن عكس الترتيب، ثم عكس النتيجة يجري في الضرب الثاني فقط، وهو المركب من سالبة كلية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، فتدبر.
Know that the a contrario (khulf) proof applies to all four moods; and that conversion of the major applies to the first mood, which is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major; and to the third mood, which is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major; and that conversion of order (then conversion of the conclusion) applies only to the second mood, which is composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major. So reflect.
Source pages p. 804

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the valid reduction methods for the four moods of the second figure. The author states: first, the khulf proof (reductio ad absurdum) works for all four moods. Second, conversion of the major (categorical conversion of the major premise) works for the first and third moods. In the first mood, the minor is universal affirmative and the major is universal negative; in the third mood, the minor is particular affirmative and the major is universal negative. The shared feature is that the minor is affirmative and the major is universal negative, which after conversion becomes a universal negative (itself), suitable as major of the first figure. Third, conversion of order (transposition of premises) followed by conversion of the conclusion works only for the second mood, which has a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major. Here the minor after conversion becomes a universal negative (itself), suitable as major of the first figure, and the major is affirmative and becomes the minor. The passage ends by exhorting reflection, indicating that the reader should grasp the reasoning behind these restrictions.

Key Terms

عكس الكبرى
عكس الترتيب
عكس النتيجة

Key Takeaway
The second figure's four moods are reduced to the first figure by three methods: khulf for all, aks al-kubra for moods with affirmative minors, and aks al-tartib plus aks al-natija for the mood with universal negative minor and universal affirmative major.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Khulf Proof for the Second Figure
Author provides complete khulf proofs for all four moods of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a khulf (proof by contradiction) for any mood of the second figure and verify its validity.

Core Idea
Khulf proves a conclusion by showing its negation contradicts a premise.

In this passage, the author provides the step-by-step method for using a contrario proof (khulf) in the second figure. This technique is essential for establishing conclusions that are not directly deducible via a first-figure syllogism. We'll systematically work through the procedure and apply it to the second, third, and fourth moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Note the two premises and conclusion of the second-figure syllogism. For example, second mood: 'No inanimate is living' (major) and 'Every human is living' (minor) concludes 'No inanimate is human'.
2
Assume the conclusion false
Take the contradictory (naqīḍ) of the conclusion. For a universal negative (E), the contradictory is a particular affirmative (I): 'Some inanimate is human'.
The contradictory must be of the opposite truth value.
3
Form a new first-figure syllogism
Use the contradictory as the minor premise and the original major premise as the major premise. Ensure the new syllogism is in the first figure (subject of minor is subject of conclusion, predicate of minor is subject of major). Example: 'Some inanimate is human' (minor) and 'Every human is living' (major).
The new syllogism must be valid in the first figure.
4
Derive the new conclusion
From the new syllogism, deduce a conclusion. It will be a proposition that contradicts the original minor premise. Example: 'Some inanimate is living' contradicts 'No inanimate is living'.
Check that the new conclusion is indeed the contradictory of the original minor.
5
Conclude the original conclusion is true
Since assuming the conclusion false leads to a contradiction with the original minor (which is assumed true), the original conclusion must be true.
This is a valid reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ. في هذا الشكل؛ فهو أن يؤخذ نقيض النتيجة، ويجعل صغرى، فينتظم قياس على هيئة الشكل الأول منتج لما يناقض الصغرى. (قَوْلُهُ : صغرى القياس ... إلخ) أي: من الشكل الأول، وفي كلامه حذف تقديره ويجعل كبراه كبرى الشكل الثاني فينتظم ... إلخ، واعلم أن الشارح لم يقم دليل الخلف إلا على الضرب الأول من ضروب الشكل الثاني ونحن نقيمه على باقيها فنقول في الضرب الثاني وهو: لا شيء من الجماد بحيوان وكل إنسان حيوان ينتج: لا شيء من الجماد بإنسان لو لم يصدق هذا لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض الجماد إنسان، وتضمه إلى كبرى الأصل هكذا بعض الجماد إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان ينتج بعض الجماد حيوان وهو نقيض صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق، وفي الضرب الثالث وهو: بعض الحيوان إنسان ولا شيء من الجماد بإنسان ينتج بعض الحيوان ليس بجماد لو لم يصدق هذا لصدق نقيضه وهو كل حيوان جماد وتضمه إلى كبرى الأصل هكذا كل حيوان جماد ولا شيء من الجماد بإنسان ينتج: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان وهو: نقيض صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق، وفي الضرب الرابع وهو: بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان وكل ناطق إنسان ينتج: بعض الحيوان ليس بناطق لو لم يصدق هذا؛ لصدق نقيضه هو: كل حيوان ناطق، وتضمه إلى كبرى الأصل هكذا كل حيوان ناطق، وكل ناطق إنسان ينتج: كل حيوان إنسان وهو نقيض صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق.
Chapter: On syllogism. In this figure: it is to take the contradictory of the conclusion, make it the minor premise, then a syllogism in the form of the first figure is formed, producing what contradicts the minor premise. (His statement: 'the minor premise of the syllogism... etc.') i.e., of the first figure. In his statement there is an omission: the implied completion is 'and he makes its major premise the major of the second figure, then a syllogism is formed... etc.' Know that the commentator gave the proof of khulf only for the first mood of the second figure, and we give it for the remaining ones. For the second mood: 'No inanimate is living' and 'Every human is living' yields 'No inanimate is human.' If this were false, its contradictory would be true: 'Some inanimate is human.' Then combine it with the original major: 'Some inanimate is human' and 'Every human is living' yields 'Some inanimate is living,' which contradicts the original minor, assumed true. The fallacy arises from the contradictory of the conclusion, so the conclusion is true. For the third mood: 'Some animal is human' and 'No inanimate is human' yields 'Some animal is not inanimate.' If false, its contradictory 'Every animal is inanimate' is true. Combine with original major: 'Every animal is inanimate' and 'No inanimate is human' yields 'No animal is human,' which contradicts the original minor. So the conclusion is true. For the fourth mood: 'Some animal is not human' and 'Every rational is human' yields 'Some animal is not rational.' If false, its contradictory 'Every animal is rational' is true. Combine with original major: 'Every animal is rational' and 'Every rational is human' yields 'Every animal is human,' which contradicts the original minor. So the conclusion is true.
Source pages p. 805

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the khulf (a contrario) proof method for the second figure. The author begins with the general rule for the second figure: to prove a conclusion via khulf, take the contradictory (negation) of the conclusion, assume it as the minor premise of a new syllogism, and combine it with the unchanged major premise of the original syllogism. The new syllogism must be in the first figure and its conclusion must contradict the original minor premise, thereby proving the original conclusion. The commentator then proceeds to demonstrate the method for each mood because the original commentary (of al-Sharh) only did so for the first mood. For the second mood (Cesare?): original premises: No inanimate is living (major), Every human is living (minor). Conclusion: No inanimate is human. The contradictory is 'Some inanimate is human' (I). Combined with the original major (Every human is living) as major, we get in the first figure: Some inanimate is human, Every human is living, therefore Some inanimate is living, which contradicts the original minor (No inanimate is living). So the original conclusion is true. For the third mood (Camestres?): original premises: Some animal is human, No inanimate is human. Conclusion: Some animal is not inanimate. Contradictory: Every animal is inanimate. Combined with original major (No inanimate is human): Every animal is inanimate, No inanimate is human, therefore No animal is human, which contradicts the original minor (Some animal is human). Hence conclusion true. For the fourth mood (Festino?): original premises: Some animal is not human, Every rational is human. Conclusion: Some animal is not rational. Contradictory: Every animal is rational. Combined with original major (Every rational is human): Every animal is rational, Every rational is human, therefore Every animal is human, which contradicts the original minor (Some animal is not human). Thus conclusion true. In each case, the argument shows that assuming the conclusion false leads to a contradiction with the original minor premise, which is assumed true, proving the conclusion.

Khulf proof for any mood of the second figure works by assuming the contradictory of the conclusion, forming a first-figure syllogism, and deriving a contradiction with the original minor premise.

Proof by Contradiction in the Second Figure
Author explains burhan al-khalf for the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply burhan al-khalf to a second-figure categorical syllogism by constructing a first-figure syllogism that contradicts the original minor premise.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction turns a negative conclusion into a first-figure syllogism by making its contradictory the minor premise.

This passage introduces a key technique for verifying second-figure syllogisms: proof by contradiction (burhan al-khalf). Rather than directly proving the conclusion, we assume its opposite and derive a contradiction with the original premises. The commentators, especially al-'Attar, clarify the precise steps and rationale, showing how the process constructs a valid first-figure syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original second-figure syllogism
Locate the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion. Note that the conclusion in the second figure is always a universal negative (e.g., 'No S is P').
In the passage, the conclusion is universal negative, as al-'Attar states.
2
Form the contradictory of the conclusion
The contradictory of a universal negative (No S is P) is a particular affirmative (Some S is P). This will serve as the minor premise of the new syllogism.
Al-'Attar explains that the affirmative contradictory fits the first figure's minor premise.
3
Keep the original major premise as the new major premise
The original major premise (e.g., 'Every M is P') is universal and suitable as the major premise of the first figure.
Al-'Attar notes that the original major's universality makes it appropriate for the major premise.
4
Arrange the new syllogism in the first figure
The new syllogism has the contradictory as minor premise and the original major as major premise. In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major and predicate of the minor. Here, the middle term is the term common to both premises (e.g., P).
The resulting syllogism produces a conclusion that contradicts the original minor premise, establishing the validity of the original conclusion.
5
Verify the contradiction
The new syllogism's conclusion should contradict the original minor premise. If it does, the original conclusion is proven by reductio ad absurdum.
The passage states it produces 'what contradicts the minor premise,' i.e., the original minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
في هذا الشكل؛ فهو أنْ يُؤخَذَ نقيضُ النَّتيجة، ويُجعَل صغرى، فينتظم قياس على هيئةِ الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ منتجُ لِمَا يناقض الصُّغرى.

الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فِي هَذَا الشَّكْل أي : الشَّكل الثَّاني، وقيَّد بذلك؛ لأنَّ الخلف الشكل الثالث هو أن يُؤخذ نقيضُ النتيجة ويجعل كُبرى.
(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى هَيْئَةِ الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ الإضافة للبيان .
(قَوْلُهُ : لِمَا يُنَاقِضُ الصُّغْرَى) أي : صُغرى أصل القياس .
(قَوْلُهُ : الصُّغْرَى) أي : المفروضة الصِّدق؛ أي وَمَا ناقض مفروضةَ الصِّدقِ كانت .

العطار
(قَوْلُهُ: وَيُجْعَلَ صُغْرَى الْقِيَاسِ لأَنَّ نتائج هذا الشَّكْلِ سالبةٌ، فَنَقيضُهَا، وهو الموجبةُ ، تصلحُ لِصغرويَّةِ الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ، وتجعلُ كُبرى الأصل كُبرى؛ لأنَّها لِكُلِّيَّتِهَا تصلحُ لِكبرويَّةِ الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ، فينتظم منهما قياس آخَرُ .
Section: On the syllogism. In this figure: it is that the contradictory of the conclusion is taken and made the minor premise, then a syllogism is arranged in the form of the first figure producing what contradicts the minor premise. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'In this figure' i.e. the second figure, and it was restricted to that because in the third figure khalf is that the contradictory of the conclusion is taken and made the major premise. (His statement: 'In the form of the first figure' the addition is for clarification. (His statement: 'What contradicts the minor premise') i.e. the minor premise of the original syllogism. (His statement: 'The minor premise') i.e. the one assumed true; i.e. and what contradicts the assumed true one is false. Al-'Attar: (His statement: 'And is made the minor premise of the syllogism') because the conclusions of this figure are negative, so their contradictory, which is affirmative, is suitable for being the minor premise of the first figure, and the original major premise is made the major premise because it, being universal, is suitable for being the major premise of the first figure, so from them another syllogism is arranged.
Source pages p. 805

Commentary
This passage defines the method of proof by contradiction (burhan al-khalf) specifically for the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author states that the procedure is to take the contradictory (naqid) of the conclusion, make it the minor premise (sughra) of a new syllogism, and then arrange a syllogism in the first figure that produces a conclusion contradicting the original minor premise. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'this figure' refers to the second figure, and he notes a key difference from the third figure: in the third figure, the contradictory of the conclusion is taken as the major premise, not the minor. Dar al-Miqyasi also comments on the phrasing, noting that 'in the form of the first figure' is explanatory. Al-'Attar then explains the rationale: because the conclusions of the second figure are universal negative, their contradictory (affirmative) is suitable as the minor premise of the first figure, while the original major premise (already universal) serves as the major premise of the new syllogism. The result is a first-figure syllogism that leads to a contradiction with the original minor premise, thereby proving the original conclusion by reductio ad absurdum. For example, if we have a valid second-figure syllogism with a false conclusion, we assume its contradictory and derive something that contradicts the original true premise, showing the assumption is impossible.

Key Terms
خلف
شكل ثاني
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a second-figure syllogism, the conclusion is a universal negative. What is the contradictory of that conclusion, and how is it used in proof by contradiction?
A) A universal negative is taken as the minor premise of a first-figure syllogism.
B) A particular affirmative is taken as the minor premise of a first-figure syllogism.
C) A particular negative is taken as the major premise of a first-figure syllogism.
D) A universal affirmative is taken as the minor premise of a second-figure syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Proof by contradiction in the second figure transforms the syllogism into a first-figure argument by using the contradictory of the conclusion as the new minor premise and the original major premise as the new major premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the second-figure syllogism: Every accident is temporally originated (major); no substance is an accident (minor); therefore no substance is temporally originated. Apply burhan al-khalf: state the contradictory of the conclusion, form the new syllogism, and identify the contradiction that proves the original conclusion.
?
In a kalam debate, a second-figure syllogism is proposed: 'Every contingent is caused; no necessary being is contingent; therefore no necessary being is caused.' Perform proof by contradiction to check the syllogism's validity.

Khalf Method for the First Mood of the Second Figure
Demonstrates khalf for the first mood of the second figure using variables and word examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proof by contradiction for a categorical syllogism in the second figure's first mood.

Core Idea
Khalf transforms the second figure into a first-figure syllogism that contradicts one original premise.

In this lesson, we delve into the proof by contradiction (khalf) applied to the first mood of the second figure. Al-‘Attar introduces the method and al-Dasuqi's commentary clarifies each step. Understanding this foundational technique prepares you to handle all moods independently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism in the second figure
Locate the premises and conclusion. For the first mood: universal affirmative minor (All J are B) and universal negative major (No A is B), conclusion: No J is A.
Use variables J, A, B as in the text.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
Take the opposite of the conclusion. If the conclusion is 'No J is A', its contradictory is 'Some J is A'.
This is the starting point of the proof.
3
Combine the assumed contradictory with the original major premise
Form a new syllogism in the first figure: minor = 'Some J is A', major = 'No A is B', leading to 'Some J is not B'.
This new conclusion contradicts the original minor premise.
4
Verify the contradiction
The new conclusion 'Some J is not B' is the logical contradictory of 'All J are B', which is the original minor premise. Since the minor premise is assumed true, the assumption that led to the contradiction must be false, so the original conclusion is true.
The khalf is valid when the derived contradiction clearly opposes an accepted premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُ الشَّارح : صغرى القياس ... إلخ) أي: من الشكل الأول، وفي كلامه حذف تقديره ويجعل كبراه كبرى الشكل الثاني فينتظم ... إلخ ، واعلم أن الشارح لم يقم دليل الخلف الأعلى الضرب الأوّل من ضروب الشَّكل الثَّاني ونحن نقيمه على باقيها فنقول في الضرب الثَّاني وهو : لا شيء من الجماد بحيوان وكل إنسان حيوان ينتج: لا شيء من الجماد بإنسان لو لم يصدق هذا لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض الجماد إنسان، وتضمه إلى كبرى الأصل هكذا بعض الجماد إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان ينتج بعض الجماد حيوان وهو نقيض صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق، وفي الضرب الثالث وهو : بعض الحيوان إنسان ولا شيء من الجماد بإنسان ينتج بعض الحيوان ليس بجماد لو لم يصدق هذا لصدق نقيضه وهو كل حيوان جماد وتضمه إلى كبرى الأصل هكذا كل حيوان جماد ولا شيء من الجماد بإنسان ينتج: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان وهو : نقيض صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق، وفي الضرب الرابع وهو : بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان وكل ناطق إنسان ينتج : بعض الحيوان ليس بناطق لو لم يصدق هذا ؛ لصدق نقيضه هو : كلّ حيوان ليس بإنسان، وتضمه إلى كبرى الأصل هكذا كل حيوان ناطق، وكل ناطق إنسان ينتج : كل حيوان إنسان وهو نقيض صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق .

فيقالُ في الضَّرب الأوَّلِ من هذا الشَّكل مثلاً : «لو لم يصدق لا شيء من (ج) (أ) لصَدَقَ نقيضُهُ، وهو بعضُ (ج ) (أ)»، فتضمه إلى كبرى القياس، هكذا بعض (ج) (أ) ، ولا شيء من (أ) (ب)»، لينتج من الشكل الأوَّل: بعض (ج) ليس (ب) . وقد كانت الصُّغرى كلَّ (ج) (ب)» هذا خُلْفٌ

الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فِي الضَّرْبِ الْأَوَّلِ) أي: وهو المركَّبُ من موجبةٍ كلّيَّةٍ صُغرى، وسالبة كلِّيَّةٍ كُبرى، نحو قولنا : كلُّ ج ب ؛ أي : كلُّ إنسان حيوانٌ، وَلَا شيء مِن أ ب أي: وَلَا شيء مِنَ الجمادِ بحيوان.
(قَوْلُهُ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنْ ج أ ) أي : مِنَ الإنسان بحجر ، وهذا هو نتيجة الضَّرب الأوَّل . (قَوْلُهُ : نَقِيضُهُ) أي : وهو موجبةٌ جزئية .
(قَوْلُهُ : بَعْضُ ج أ) أي : بعضُ الإنسان حجر .
(قَوْلُهُ: إِلَى كُبْرَى الْقِيَاسِ) أي : فَفائدةُ الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ قوله : (بعضُ ج أ)؛ أي : بعضُ الإنسان حجر . (قَوْلُهُ : وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنْ أ ب) أي: مِنَ الحجرِ بحيوان .
(قَوْلُهُ : بَعْضُ ج ليسَ (ب) أي: بعضُ الإنسان ليس بحيوان. (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ كَانَتِ الصُّغْرَى) أي : والحالُ أنَّه قد كانت الصغرى من الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ المذكور .
(قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ ج (ب) أي : كلُّ إنسان حيوان .
(قَوْلُهُ : هَذَا خُلْفٌ) أي : ما ذكر من النتيجة الحاصلة من ضم نقيض نتيجة الشَّربِ الأَوَّلِ إلى كُبراهُ خلف؛ أي: كذب؛ لأنَّه مُنافٍ لِصُغرى الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّل الَّتي هي مفروضه الصدق، وكذبُ تلك النتيجة إنَّما نشأ من نقيض نتيجةِ الضَّرْبِ الأوَّل، فيكون ذلكَ النَّقيضُ كذباً، وحينئذ ; فتصدقُ نتيجةُ الشَّربِ الأَوَّل، فيكونُ الشَّربُ الأوَّلُ مُنتجاً ، فتأمَّلْ .
(The commentator's statement: 'the minor premise of the syllogism... etc.') i.e. from the first figure, and in his speech there is an omission, its estimation: and he makes its major premise the major premise of the second figure, so it is arranged... etc. Know that the commentator did not establish the proof by contradiction for the first mood of the second figure, and we establish it for the rest. So we say in the second mood: No inanimate is animal, and every human is animal, produces: No inanimate is human. If this were not true, its contradictory would be true, which is: Some inanimate is human. You join it to the original major premise as follows: Some inanimate is human, and no animal is inanimate? Actually the text says: Some inanimate is human, and every human is animal, produces: Some inanimate is animal, which is the contradictory of the original minor premise assumed true. The falsity came from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it (the conclusion) is true. And in the third mood: Some animal is human, and no inanimate is human, produces: Some animal is not inanimate. If this were not true, its contradictory would be true, which is: Every animal is inanimate. You join it to the original major premise as follows: Every animal is inanimate, and no inanimate is human, produces: No animal is human, which is the contradictory of the original minor premise assumed true. The falsity came from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true. And in the fourth mood: Some animal is not human, and every rational is human, produces: Some animal is not rational. If this were not true, its contradictory would be true, which is: Every animal is not human. You join it to the original major premise as follows: Every animal is rational, and every rational is human, produces: Every animal is human, which is the contradictory of the original minor premise assumed true. The falsity came from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true.

It is said in the first mood of this figure, for example: 'If it were not true that no J is A, then its contradictory would be true, which is some J is A.' You join it to the major premise of the syllogism, thus: Some J is A, and no A is B, so from the first figure it produces: Some J is not B. But the minor premise was: Every J is B. This is a contradiction.

Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'In the first mood') i.e. it is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, like our statement: Every J is B; i.e. every human is animal, and nothing from A is B; i.e. nothing from inanimate is animal. (His statement: 'Nothing from J is A') i.e. from human is stone, and this is the conclusion of the first mood. (His statement: 'its contradictory') i.e. it is a particular affirmative. (His statement: 'Some J is A') i.e. some human is stone. (His statement: 'to the major premise of the syllogism') i.e. the benefit of the first mood is his statement: (Some J is A); i.e. some human is stone. (His statement: 'and no A is B') i.e. from stone is animal. (His statement: 'Some J is not B') i.e. some human is not animal. (His statement: 'though the minor premise was') i.e. though it was the minor premise of the first mood mentioned. (His statement: 'Every J is B') i.e. every human is animal. (His statement: 'This is a contradiction') i.e. what is mentioned from the result obtained from joining the contradictory of the conclusion of the first mood to its major premise is a contradiction; i.e. a falsehood, because it conflicts with the minor premise of the first mood which was assumed true. The falsehood of that result arose from the contradictory of the conclusion of the first mood, so that contradictory is false. Then the conclusion of the first mood is true. So the first mood is productive. So consider.
Source pages p. 805

Commentary
This passage provides detailed applications of proof by contradiction (khalf) to each of the four moods (darb) of the second figure. Al-'Attar begins by noting that the original commentator (al-sharih) only demonstrated khalf for the first mood, and he will complete it for the remaining moods. For the second mood, which has premises 'No inanimate is animal' (major) and 'Every human is animal' (minor) producing 'No inanimate is human', the proof assumes the conclusion false, so its contradictory 'Some inanimate is human' is taken. Joining this with the major premise 'No inanimate is animal'? Wait, the text says: 'You join it to the original major premise as follows: Some inanimate is human, and every human is animal, produces: Some inanimate is animal.' Actually, there may be a misprint in the OCR: the major premise originally was 'No inanimate is animal'? But here he uses 'every human is animal' as the second premise? Let's read carefully: In the second mood, the original syllogism is: No inanimate (A) is animal (B) (major) and Every human (C) is animal (B) (minor) => No inanimate (A) is human (C). For khalf, assume the contradictory of the conclusion: Some inanimate (A) is human (C). Then join this to the original major premise? But he says: 'Some inanimate is human, and every human is animal'—that uses the original minor premise? Actually, the original major is 'No inanimate is animal', but here he uses 'Every human is animal' which is the original minor. So he is joining the assumed contradictory 'Some inanimate is human' with the original minor premise 'Every human is animal' to produce 'Some inanimate is animal', which contradicts the original major premise 'No inanimate is animal'. That is a contradiction with the original major, not the minor. But the text says 'contradictory of the original minor premise'. There is inconsistency: the text says 'nagid sughra al-asl' (contradictory of the original minor) but the example seems to produce contradiction with the major. However, al-'Attar's example for the second mood says: 'Some inanimate is human, and every human is animal, produces: Some inanimate is animal' which contradicts the major 'No inanimate is animal'. Possibly a textual error, but we should follow the text as given. For the third mood: premises 'Some animal is human' (major?) Actually, the second figure has specific arrangements: major is universal negative, minor is universal affirmative for first mood, etc. The third mood of the second figure is: major: No inanimate is human, minor: Some animal is human, conclusion: Some animal is not inanimate. Assuming conclusion false gives 'Every animal is inanimate'. Joining with original major 'No inanimate is human' gives 'No animal is human', contradicting the original minor 'Some animal is human'. For the fourth mood: premises: Some animal is not human (minor?), actually fourth mood of second figure: major: Every rational is human, minor: Some animal is not human, conclusion: Some animal is not rational. Assuming conclusion false gives 'Every animal is rational'. Joining with original major 'Every rational is human' gives 'Every animal is human', contradicting the original minor 'Some animal is not human'. Al-'Attar's examples use slightly different phrasing but illustrate the same pattern. On page 806, Dasuqi then explains the first mood in detail. The first mood of the second figure is: major: No A is B (e.g., No inanimate is animal), minor: Every C is B (e.g., Every human is animal), conclusion: No C is A (e.g., No human is stone). But in Dasuqi's example, he uses J, A, B as variables: original conclusion is 'No J is A' (No human is stone). Assuming conclusion false gives 'Some J is A' (Some human is stone). Join this with the original major premise 'No A is B' (No stone is animal) to get 'Some J is not B' (Some human is not animal), which contradicts the original minor premise 'Every J is B' (Every human is animal). That matches the general method: the new syllogism uses the assumed contradictory as minor and original major as major, producing a contradictory to the original minor. Dasuqi carefully explains each step, concluding that the contradiction proves the original conclusion true. Therefore, each mood of the second figure is valid.

In the first mood of the second figure, what is the contradictory of the conclusion?
A) Some J is A
B) No J is A
C) Some J is not A
D) Every J is B

Key Takeaway
Khalf for the first mood of the second figure works by assuming the opposite of the conclusion and deriving a contradiction with the original minor premise.

The Syllogism
intermediate
15 min
Applying Khalf to the Remaining Moods of the Second Figure
Extends khalf to moods 2-4 of the second figure, providing concrete examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a khalf proof for any of the four moods of the second figure.

Core Idea
Each mood of the second figure requires a unique combination of premises to generate the contradiction, but the method is uniform.

Having mastered the first mood, we now extend the khalf technique to moods 2-4 of the second figure. Al-‘Attar provides explicit examples for each mood, showing how assuming the contradictory of the conclusion leads to a contradiction with either the major or minor premise. This lesson guides you through each case step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and its premises
For each mood, note the quantity and quality of premises. Mood 2: minor universal negative, major universal negative? Actually text: 'No inanimate is animal' (major) and 'Every human is animal' (minor). Wait, the text order: Mood2: 'No inanimate is animal' (major) and 'Every human is animal' (minor) produces 'No inanimate is human'. Mood3: 'Some animal is human' (minor?) and 'No inanimate is human' (major?) produces 'Some animal is not inanimate'. Mood4: 'Some animal is not human' (minor?) and 'Every rational is human' (major?) produces 'Some animal is not rational'. Follow the text's examples.
The examples in the text use natural language; translate into variables.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
For each mood, write the negation. Example: for mood2, conclusion is 'No inanimate is human', so assume 'Some inanimate is human'.
Use standard logical negation: 'No S is P' contradicts 'Some S is P'.
3
Combine the assumed contradictory with one original premise
Join the assumed contradictory with the other premise to form a new syllogism. The text does this for each mood: for mood2, combine 'Some inanimate is human' with 'Every human is animal' (original minor) to get 'Some inanimate is animal', which contradicts the original major 'No inanimate is animal'.
Pay attention to which premise is used; it may vary by mood.
4
Verify which premise is contradicted
Check that the derived conclusion directly opposes a premise assumed true. In mood2, the contradiction is with the major; in mood3, with the minor; in mood4, with the minor. This confirms the original conclusion.
The contradiction always arises from the assumed contradictory, proving it false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قَوْلُ الشَّارح : صغرى القياس ... إلخ) أي: من الشكل الأول، وفي كلامه حذف تقديره ويجعل كبراه كبرى الشكل الثاني فينتظم ... إلخ ، واعلم أن الشارح لم يقم دليل الخلف الأعلى الضرب الأوّل من ضروب الشَّكل الثَّاني ونحن نقيمه على باقيها فنقول في الضرب الثَّاني وهو : لا شيء من الجماد بحيوان وكل إنسان حيوان ينتج: لا شيء من الجماد بإنسان لو لم يصدق هذا لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض الجماد إنسان، وتضمه إلى كبرى الأصل هكذا بعض الجماد إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان ينتج بعض الجماد حيوان وهو نقيض صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق، وفي الضرب الثالث وهو : بعض الحيوان إنسان ولا شيء من الجماد بإنسان ينتج بعض الحيوان ليس بجماد لو لم يصدق هذا لصدق نقيضه وهو كل حيوان جماد وتضمه إلى كبرى الأصل هكذا كل حيوان جماد ولا شيء من الجماد بإنسان ينتج: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان وهو : نقيض صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق، وفي الضرب الرابع وهو : بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان وكل ناطق إنسان ينتج : بعض الحيوان ليس بناطق لو لم يصدق هذا ؛ لصدق نقيضه هو : كلّ حيوان ليس بإنسان، وتضمه إلى كبرى الأصل هكذا كل حيوان ناطق، وكل ناطق إنسان ينتج : كل حيوان إنسان وهو نقيض صغرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق .

فيقالُ في الضَّرب الأوَّلِ من هذا الشَّكل مثلاً : «لو لم يصدق لا شيء من (ج) (أ) لصَدَقَ نقيضُهُ، وهو بعضُ (ج ) (أ)»، فتضمه إلى كبرى القياس، هكذا بعض (ج) (أ) ، ولا شيء من (أ) (ب)»، لينتج من الشكل الأوَّل: بعض (ج) ليس (ب) . وقد كانت الصُّغرى كلَّ (ج) (ب)» هذا خُلْفٌ

(The commentator's statement: 'the minor premise of the syllogism... etc.') i.e. from the first figure, and in his speech there is an omission, its estimation: and he makes its major premise the major premise of the second figure, so it is arranged... etc. Know that the commentator did not establish the proof by contradiction for the first mood of the second figure, and we establish it for the rest. So we say in the second mood: No inanimate is animal, and every human is animal, produces: No inanimate is human. If this were not true, its contradictory would be true, which is: Some inanimate is human. You join it to the original major premise as follows: Some inanimate is human, and no animal is inanimate? Actually the text says: Some inanimate is human, and every human is animal, produces: Some inanimate is animal, which is the contradictory of the original minor premise assumed true. The falsity came from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it (the conclusion) is true. And in the third mood: Some animal is human, and no inanimate is human, produces: Some animal is not inanimate. If this were not true, its contradictory would be true, which is: Every animal is inanimate. You join it to the original major premise as follows: Every animal is inanimate, and no inanimate is human, produces: No animal is human, which is the contradictory of the original minor premise assumed true. The falsity came from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true. And in the fourth mood: Some animal is not human, and every rational is human, produces: Some animal is not rational. If this were not true, its contradictory would be true, which is: Every animal is not human. You join it to the original major premise as follows: Every animal is rational, and every rational is human, produces: Every animal is human, which is the contradictory of the original minor premise assumed true. The falsity came from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true.

It is said in the first mood of this figure, for example: 'If it were not true that no J is A, then its contradictory would be true, which is some J is A.' You join it to the major premise of the syllogism, thus: Some J is A, and no A is B, so from the first figure it produces: Some J is not B. But the minor premise was: Every J is B. This is a contradiction.

Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'In the first mood') i.e. it is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, like our statement: Every J is B; i.e. every human is animal, and nothing from A is B; i.e. nothing from inanimate is animal. (His statement: 'Nothing from J is A') i.e. from human is stone, and this is the conclusion of the first mood. (His statement: 'its contradictory') i.e. it is a particular affirmative. (His statement: 'Some J is A') i.e. some human is stone. (His statement: 'to the major premise of the syllogism') i.e. the benefit of the first mood is his statement: (Some J is A); i.e. some human is stone. (His statement: 'and no A is B') i.e. from stone is animal. (His statement: 'Some J is not B') i.e. some human is not animal. (His statement: 'though the minor premise was') i.e. though it was the minor premise of the first mood mentioned. (His statement: 'Every J is B') i.e. every human is animal. (His statement: 'This is a contradiction') i.e. what is mentioned from the result obtained from joining the contradictory of the conclusion of the first mood to its major premise is a contradiction; i.e. a falsehood, because it conflicts with the minor premise of the first mood which was assumed true. The falsehood of that result arose from the contradictory of the conclusion of the first mood, so that contradictory is false. Then the conclusion of the first mood is true. So the first mood is productive. So consider.

Commentary
This passage provides detailed applications of proof by contradiction (khalf) to each of the four moods (darb) of the second figure. Al-'Attar begins by noting that the original commentator (al-sharih) only demonstrated khalf for the first mood, and he will complete it for the remaining moods. For khalf, assume the contradictory of the conclusion: Some inanimate (A) is human (C). Then join this to the original major premise? But he says: 'Some inanimate is human, and every human is animal'—that uses the original minor premise? That is a contradiction with the original major, not the minor. But the text says 'contradictory of the original minor premise'. There is inconsistency: the text says 'nagid sughra al-asl' (contradictory of the original minor) but the example seems to produce contradiction with the major. Assuming conclusion false gives 'Every animal is inanimate'. Joining with original major 'No inanimate is human' gives 'No animal is human', contradicting the original minor 'Some animal is human'. For the fourth mood: premises: Some animal is not human (minor?), actually fourth mood of second figure: major: Every rational is human, minor: Some animal is not human, conclusion: Some animal is not rational. The first mood of the second figure is: major: No A is B (e.g., No inanimate is animal), minor: Every C is B (e.g., Every human is animal), conclusion: No C is A (e.g., No human is stone). But in Dasuqi's example, he uses J, A, B as variables: original conclusion is 'No J is A' (No human is stone). Assuming conclusion false gives 'Some J is A' (Some human is stone). Join this with the original major premise 'No A is B' (No stone is animal) to get 'Some J is not B' (Some human is not animal), which contradicts the original minor premise 'Every J is B' (Every human is animal). That matches the general method: the new syllogism uses the assumed contradictory as minor and original major as major, producing a contradictory to the original minor. Dasuqi carefully explains each step, concluding that the contradiction proves the original conclusion true. Therefore, each mood of the second figure is valid.

Key Terms
ضرب ثاني
ضرب ثالث
ضرب رابع
دليل الخلف
نقيض
صغرى
كبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The original major premise 'No inanimate is human'
B) The original minor premise 'Some animal is human'
C) The original conclusion 'Some animal is not inanimate'
D) The assumed contradictory 'Every animal is inanimate'

Key Takeaway
For moods 2-4 of the second figure, assuming the contradictory of the conclusion and combining it with the original major premise yields a new conclusion that always contradicts the original major (mood2) or original minor (moods3-4).

Proof by Contradiction: The Khulf Principle
Author states the general principle of proof by contradiction for syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the proof-by-contradiction (khulf) method to verify the validity of a categorical syllogism in the first figure.

Core Idea
If assuming the opposite leads to absurdity, the conclusion must be true.

This lesson introduces the foundational principle of proof by contradiction (khulf) that underlies all demonstrative syllogisms. The author compactly states that from the contradictory of the conclusion, a contradiction follows, making it impossible, thus confirming the conclusion. The commentaries unpack this into a more intuitive principle of incompatible entailments, which avoids technical conversions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the khulf argument
In the opening sentence, locate the conclusion (natija), its contradictory (naqīḍ al-natīja), and the resulting impossibility (khulf). The author claims that khulf follows from the naqīḍ and that the impossibility renders the conclusion true.
The term 'khulf' here refers to a logical contradiction, not just absurdity.
2
Trace the logical flow
Assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion. Combine it with the given premises to derive a contradiction. Since a contradiction is impossible, the original assumption must be false, so the conclusion is true. This is the reduction ad absurdum pattern.
This works only if the syllogism is valid and premises are true.
3
Understand the justification from the commentator
Al-Attar quotes Sharḥ al-Muṭāliʿ, which reframes the proof: the entailments of the two sides (affirmation and negation of the middle term for the subject) are incompatible. Since entailments are incompatible, the entaileds (the original positions) must be incompatible as well. This removes the need for contrived conversions.
This insight shows the khulf principle is more fundamental than figure-specific rules.
4
Recognize the generality of the principle
Although the passage focuses on the first figure, the khulf method applies to any valid syllogism across all figures and moods. The assurance of a conclusion's truth depends on the impossibility of its contradictory, not on the figure.
The author later provides tables for other figures, confirming the universality of the approach.

(قَوْلُهُ : يَلْزَمُ الْخُلْفُ) أي : البطلان. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : فَالنَّتِيْجَةُ حَقٌّ ضرورة امتناع كذب النَّقيضَيْنِ، قالَ شارحُ المطالعِ : والحقُّ أنَّ إنتاج هذا الشَّكل لا يحتاج إلى التَّكلفاتِ المذكورة؛ لأنَّ حاصله يرجعُ إلى الاستدلال بتنافي اللوازم على تنافي الملزومات، فيكفي أن يُقال: من لوازم أحدِ الطَّرفَينِ ثبوتُ الوسط لهُ، وَمِن لوازم الآخَرِ سَلْبُهُ عنهُ، وَهُمَا مُتنافيانِ، فَيتنافَى الملزومان، وإلا ; اجتمع المتنافيان. Chapter on Syllogism: It follows from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it becomes impossible, thus the conclusion is true. al-Dasuqi (His statement: 'It follows') i.e., the contradiction follows from the contradictory of the conclusion, i.e., the conclusion of the first mode. (His statement: 'becomes') i.e., that contradictory becomes impossible. (His statement: 'the conclusion is true') i.e., the conclusion of the first mode is true. (His statement: 'the contradiction follows') i.e., the falsehood. al-Attar (His statement: 'the conclusion is true') necessarily because it is impossible for both contradictories to be false. The commentator on al-Mutali' said: The truth is that the production of this figure does not need these contrivances because its outcome reduces to inference through the mutual incompatibility of the entailments to the mutual incompatibility of the entaileds. It suffices to say: from the entailments of one of the two sides is the affirmation of the middle term for it, and from the entailments of the other is its negation from it, and they are mutually incompatible, so the entaileds are mutually incompatible; otherwise the two incompatibles would be united. (His statement: 'since it') i.e., the form of the syllogism. (1) (Statement of the commentator: 'the matter') i.e., the minor premise which is the contradictory of the conclusion. And know that I found Ibn Sa'id in his gloss on this book placed tables in which he drew the productive moods other than the first figure and explained the proofs of production in an obscure, unedited, and unordered manner despite his praise of them such as no one before had done. So in light of that and in light of what is in the commentary and glosses of omission and summarization, I was compelled to draw them in four tables in an easy manner ordered according to the text and commentary, not leaving what they left nor summarizing what they summarized, until it came out from between dung and blood as pure milk palatable to drinkers. And I do not declare myself innocent, for I am sick, and above every possessor of knowledge is the All-Knowing. And God is the one hoped to grant it acceptance. And here it is calling you, so answer it with your full mouth.
Source pages p. 807

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the syllogism, stating the general principle that the truth of a syllogism's conclusion is proven by contradiction: the contradictory of the conclusion, when assumed, leads to an impossibility, so the conclusion must be true. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that the contradiction follows from the contradictory of the conclusion, not from the premises themselves. Then al-Attar adds a quote from Sharh al-Mutali' which criticizes the complicated proofs given earlier and simplifies the rationale: the proof works by showing that the entailments of the two sides are incompatible, implying the entaileds are also incompatible. Finally, a lengthy note explains that the author created tables for the productive moods of figures other than the first, improving on Ibn Sa'id's work. This note is a textual interpolation.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyās): The syllogism; here defined as that which produces a conclusion by contradiction from the contradictory of the conclusion. [Note: In al-Abhari's Isaghuji, qiyas refers mainly to categorical syllogism; in Fakhr al-Din al-Razi it bleeds into analogical reasoning.]
خلف (khulf): The absurdity or contradiction that follows from assuming the contradictory of the conclusion. [Note: No significant shift — this term is stable.]
نقيض النتيجة (naqīḍ al-natīja): The contradictory of the conclusion, assumed as a premise in the proof by contradiction. [Note: No significant shift — this term is stable.]
مادة (mādda): The matter or content of a syllogism; here specifically the minor premise which is the contradictory of the conclusion. [Note: In logic, mādda often refers to the premises; here it is a specific usage.]

Key Takeaway
The proof by contradiction (khulf) is the fundamental method for establishing the truth of a syllogism's conclusion by showing the impossibility of its opposite.

Reducing Second Figure Syllogisms to the First Figure
Author explains two reduction methods for second figure moods: conversion of major and reversal of order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two reduction methods ('aks al-kubra and 'aks al-tartib) to convert any valid second figure syllogism into the first figure, using the first mood as a case study.

Core Idea
Two techniques convert second figure syllogisms into the intuitive first figure.

Reducing syllogisms to the first figure makes their validity immediately obvious. In this lesson, you will learn two methods from the Islamic logical tradition: conversion of the major premise and reversal of the order of premises after converting the minor. The passage from the Taṣdīr commentary illustrates these methods using the first mood of the second figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure mood
Locate the original syllogism: minor premise (universal affirmative) and major premise (universal negative). In the text, this is 'Every J is B' and 'No B is A'. The middle term B is the predicate in both premises, confirming the second figure.
The first mood of the second figure has a universal affirmative minor and universal negative major.
2
Apply 'aks al-kubra (conversion of major)
Convert the major premise 'No B is A' to its simple converse 'No A is B'. The syllogism now becomes: Every J is B, No A is B. To make it first figure, swap the premises so that the converted major comes first: No A is B, Every J is B. This is the second mood of the first figure (Cesare), producing the conclusion 'No J is A' intuitively.
The conversion of an E-type proposition preserves truth.
3
Apply 'aks al-tartib (reversal of order after converting minor)
First, convert the minor premise 'Every J is B' to its subaltern (no change needed, as it is already A-type) but the text specifies converting the minor. In this mood, the minor is already affirmative, so conversion yields the same form? Actually, the passage says to convert the minor first, then swap. For the first mood, the minor is universal affirmative which converts to a particular affirmative (I-type). After conversion, we have: Some B is J (from minor) and No B is A (major). Then swap: No B is A (major first), Some B is J (minor). This is first figure (Ferio if minor is particular). The conclusion 'No J is A' follows.
In the second figure, 'aks al-tartib involves converting the minor first; in the third figure, it involves converting the major first.
4
Confirm the conclusion remains the same
Both methods produce the same conclusion, 'No J is A'. The reduction does not change the logical validity; it only rearranges the syllogism into the first figure where validity is immediately evident.
5
Distinguish from third figure reduction
Note that in the third figure, the reversal of order is different: you first convert the major, then swap. The commentator al-Dasuqi explicitly restricts this method to the second figure.
This distinction prevents confusion across figures.

Source Text

Section: On Syllogism. And it necessarily follows from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it becomes impossible, so the conclusion is true. His statement 'it produces intuitively' means: it produces by intuition, or it produces an intuitive result, i.e., apparent, and the first is more apparent. His statement 'also' means as was said concerning the first mood by reductio. His statement 'Every J is B' means: every human is an animal. His statement 'and no B is A' means: no animal is a stone, and this is the conversion of the major of the first mood. His statement 'No J is A' means: no human is a stone. His statement 'and it is the desired' means: and thereby the first mood is productive. His statement 'and as for reversal of order' means that which occurs after conversion of the minor. His statement 'in this figure' means the second figure, and he restricted it because reversal of order in the third figure is to convert the major first, then make it minor. His statement 'it is that the minor is converted' therein: reversal of order is to make the major minor and vice versa. Al-'Attar said: His statement 'so that it returns to the first figure' means: it is reduced to the second mood of it, which is that the minor is universal affirmative and the major universal negative. His statement 'in the first mood' means of the second figure. His statement 'that the minor is converted' means the minor of the original, and it is here universal negative, so it converts to universal negative.
Source pages p. 807

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on reducing moods of the second figure to the first figure. It opens with a statement about proof by contradiction: if the contradictory of a conclusion leads to an impossibility, the conclusion must be true. Then it presents two methods for reducing second figure moods. The first method is 'aks al-kubra' (conversion of the major premise). The idea is to convert the major premise so that the syllogism takes the form of the first figure, which produces the conclusion intuitively. An example is given: if the original mood has 'Every J is B' (minor) and 'No B is A' (major), converting the major gives 'No A is B', but the text shows a different arrangement. Actually, the example shows that after conversion, the syllogism becomes 'Every J is B, No B is A' which is already first figure? Wait, the passage says: 'as is also said concerning the first mood: Every J is B, No B is A, produces from the first figure No J is A.' This seems to indicate that the first figure can also produce this conclusion directly. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that the conversion of the major makes the syllogism revert to the first figure, specifically the second mood of the first figure (where minor is universal affirmative and major universal negative). He gives concrete examples: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No animal is a stone' to illustrate the terms. The second method is 'aks al-tartib' (reversal of order), which involves converting the minor premise. Al-Dasuqi explains that reversal of order means swapping the premises: making the major minor and vice versa. He distinguishes this from reversal in the third figure, where one first converts the major then swaps. Al-'Attar adds that reducing to the first figure means going to the second mood of it (universal affirmative minor and universal negative major). He specifies that the minor to be converted is the original minor, which here is universal negative, so its conversion remains universal negative. The passage thus provides a clear, example-driven explanation of these two reduction techniques.

عكس الكبرى
عكس الترتيب
الشكل الثاني
الضرب الأول

The Syllogism
intermediate

Second Figure Syllogism: Moods and Reduction to First Figure
Enumerates the four second figure moods and their reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four valid moods of the second figure and reduce any of them to the first figure using the method of contradiction (al-khalf).

Core Idea
Second figure moods all have negative majors and reduce to first figure by converting the major and swapping premises.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism has its own set of valid moods, each producing a necessary conclusion when its premises are true. In this passage, the author lists the four productive moods of the second figure and explains how they can be reduced to the first figure using a two-step process: conversion of the major premise and reversal of the premise order, followed by contradiction of the minor. This reduction is essential for proving the validity of second-figure arguments within the first-figure framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure's structure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises, and the major premise (the one containing the predicate of the conclusion) must be negative. Examine each mood's conclusion and premises to confirm this pattern.
For example, mood 1: 'Every human is animal; Nothing of human is stone' – middle term 'human' is predicate in both premises, and the major 'Nothing of human is stone' is negative.
2
List the four productive moods
The passage gives four moods: (1) Every human is animal, no human is stone → no stone is animal? Actually conclusion is 'every human is animal and nothing of human is stone', but the standard second figure moods are Cesare, Camestres, Festino, Baroco. Recognize which mood corresponds to each by the quantity and quality of premises.
Mood 1 corresponds to Cesare (E, A, E), mood 2 to Camestres (A, E, E), mood 3 to Festino (E, I, O), mood 4 to Baroco (A, O, O).
3
Perform reduction: convert the major and reverse order
For each mood, convert the major premise (swap subject and predicate, preserving truth) and then swap the two premises so that the converted major becomes the minor of the first figure. This yields a first-figure syllogism.
For Cesare: convert major 'No human is stone' to 'No stone is human'; then reverse order to get 'No stone is human; every human is animal; therefore no stone is animal' – which is Celarent in first figure.
4
Use contradiction (al-khalf) to complete reduction
The author specifies that after converting and reversing, you contradict the minor premise. This refers to the method of proof by contradiction: assume the opposite of the desired conclusion, derive a contradiction, thus proving the original conclusion. Practically, from the first-figure mood, you can derive the conclusion directly; the contradiction step is an alternative validation.
In the passage, 'then contradict the minor' means that in the reduced first-figure syllogism, you take the minor premise's contradictory as an assumption for reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text

جدول رقم 1 - الشكل الثاني. ضروبه المنتجة أربعة. الأول: نتيجته: كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر. الخلف: عكس الكبرى عكس الترتيب ثم يناقض الصغرى. الثاني: نتيجته: لا شيء من الحجر حيوان وكل إنسان حيوان. الخلف: عكس الكبرى عكس الترتيب ثم يناقض الصغرى. الثالث: نتيجته: بعض الحيوان إنسان ولا شيء من الحجر إنسان. الخلف: عكس الكبرى عكس الترتيب ثم يناقض الصغرى. الرابع: نتيجته: بعض الحيوان ليس إنسان وكل ناطق إنسان. الخلف: عكس الكبرى عكس الترتيب ثم يناقض الصغرى. وهذه الضروب لا تصلح كبرى للأول إلا بعد عكس الكبرى.
Table No. 1 - Second figure. Its productive moods are four. The first: its conclusion: Every human is an animal and nothing of human is stone. The contradiction: convert the major, reverse the order, then contradict the minor. The second: its conclusion: Nothing of stone is an animal and every human is an animal. Commentary
This passage presents the second figure of the categorical syllogism, listing its four valid moods. The second figure is defined by having a negative major premise and the middle term being the predicate in both premises. The author first gives the conclusion for each mood, then describes the method of reduction to the first figure via argument by contradiction (الخلف). The reduction involves two steps: converting the major premise ('aks al-kubra) and reversing the order of the premises ('aks al-tartib), then contradicting the minor premise. For example, Mood 1: 'Every human is an animal; Nothing of human is stone' reduces by converting the major ('Nothing of stone is human') and swapping to get 'Nothing of stone is human; Every human is animal' then contradicting the minor. The author notes that these moods' majors are not suitable for the first figure until after conversion.

Overview of the Third Figure and Its Six Moods
Author enumerates the six valid moods of the third figure and their reduction to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six valid moods of the third categorical syllogistic figure and state the conclusion for each.

Core Idea
The third figure yields only particular conclusions, but all six moods are valid and reducible to first figure.

The third figure is defined by having the middle term as subject in both premises, which forces the conclusion to be particular. The author lists all six valid moods, each with its pair of premises and conclusion. Understanding these moods is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, especially Kalam and Fiqh, where such syllogisms often appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In each mood, the middle term (here 'human') is the subject of both premises. Notice that the middle term does not appear in the conclusion.
The middle term is the term that connects the other two.
2
Examine the quantifiers of premises
The premises mix universal (كل) and particular (بعض) and negative (لا شيء) quantifiers. For example, mood 1 has two universal affirmatives; mood 4 has a universal affirmative and a universal negative.
The quantifiers determine the type of conclusion.
3
Note the conclusion
Every conclusion in the third figure is particular: either 'some animal is rational' (جزئية موجبة) or 'some animal is not stone' (جزئية سالبة). Never a universal conclusion.
This is a rule of the third figure.
4
List the six moods
Go through each mood from the passage: mood 1 (كل إنسان ناطق وكل إنسان حيوان → بعض الحيوان ناطق), mood 2 (بعض الإنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق → بعض الحيوان ناطق), etc. Write down the premises and conclusion for each.
Memorize the patterns to quickly recognize valid third figure arguments.

Commentary
This passage presents the third figure of the categorical syllogism, listing all six valid moods. The third figure is characterized by having the middle term as the subject in both premises, and the conclusion is always particular. Each mood is given with its conclusion and the reduction method using argument by contradiction (الخلف). The reduction steps are: convert the minor premise ('aks al-sughra), reverse the order of premises ('aks al-tartib), then contradict the original major premise. For example, Mood 1: 'Every human is rational; Every human is animal' gives conclusion 'Some animal is rational'. To reduce, convert the minor 'Every human is animal' to 'Some animal is human', then reverse order to get 'Some animal is human; Every human is rational' which is a valid first figure mood (Ferio), then contradict the major of the original (which is 'Every human is rational') to get the conclusion. The author notes that some moods require special handling when premises are particular or negative.

The Syllogism

The Reduction Method (al-Khalf) in the Third Figure
Author enumerates the six valid moods of the third figure and their reduction to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three-step reduction method (convert minor, reverse order, contradict major) to transform any valid third figure syllogism into a first figure syllogism using argument by contradiction.

Core Idea
The third figure is proven valid by reducing each mood to the first figure via conversion and contradiction.

The author provides a uniform reduction method for all six moods of the third figure: convert the minor premise, reverse the order of premises, then contradict the original major premise. This method uses the principle of argument by contradiction (al-khalf). By mastering this reduction, you will deepen your understanding of syllogistic validity and be able to check any third figure argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
In a third figure syllogism, the minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion (e.g., 'animal' in 'every human is animal'), and the major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion (e.g., 'rational' in 'every human is rational'). The middle term 'human' is subject of both.
The minor is usually the first premise in the list, but verify by the conclusion's subject.
2
Convert the minor premise ('aks al-sughra)
Apply conversion to the minor premise: swap subject and predicate while adjusting quantity. If the minor is universal affirmative (كل إنسان حيوان), convert to particular affirmative (بعض الحيوان إنسان). If it is particular affirmative (بعض الإنسان حيوان), convert to particular affirmative (بعض الحيوان إنسان). Universal negative (لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر) converts to universal negative (لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان). Particular negative does not convert, but the third figure's minor is never particular negative in valid moods.
Conversion preserves truth only with the appropriate quantity change.
3
Reverse the order of premises ('aks al-tartib)
After conversion, swap the two premises so that the new major (originally the converted minor) becomes the first premise, and the new minor (originally the major) becomes the second. This order matches the first figure where the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor.
This step arranges the premises correctly for the first figure.
4
Contradict the original major premise (yunaqid kubra al-asl)
Take the contradictory opposite of the original major premise. For example, if the original major was 'كل إنسان ناطق' (universal affirmative), its contradictory is 'بعض الإنسان ليس بناطق' (particular negative). Set this as the new minor premise of the reduced syllogism. This step ensures the reduction uses the principle of contradiction (al-khalf): assuming the conclusion false leads to a contradiction with the premises.
The contradiction is used to prove the conclusion indirectly. The reduced syllogism will have the same conclusion as the original.
5
Verify the reduced syllogism is valid first figure
Check that the resulting syllogism fits a valid first figure mood (e.g., Ferio or Festino). If the reduction steps are correct, the conclusion will follow necessarily, confirming the original third figure argument is valid.
All six third figure moods reduce to valid first figure moods.

Six Productive Moods of the Third Figure
Author lists the six moods of the third figure with conclusions and reduction methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six productive moods of the third figure and reduce any of them to the first figure using conversion of the minor premise and reversal of premise order.

Core Idea
All third figure moods reduce to the first figure by converting the minor premise and swapping premises.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has exactly six valid moods, each yielding a particular or negative conclusion. To confirm their validity, logicians reduce them to the first figure by a standard two-step method: first convert the minor premise, then reverse the order of premises. This lesson walks through the moods and the reduction technique as presented in the classical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure by middle term position
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. For example, if the premises are 'Every human is animal' and 'Every human is rational', the middle term 'human' is the subject in each.
This contrasts with the first figure (middle term subject of major, predicate of minor) and second figure (middle term predicate of both).
2
List the six moods and their conclusions
The passage enumerates six moods. Although the exact premises are not given, the conclusions are provided. For instance, the first mood's conclusion is 'Every human is rational' (universal affirmative), but the standard third figure yields particular conclusions; this may be a special case or an error in the reconstructed text.
Due to OCR reconstruction, the specific conclusions may not match standard manuals, but the reduction method is reliable.
3
Learn the standard reduction method
The method is 'aks al-sughra (conversion of the minor premise) followed by 'aks al-tartib (reversal of the order of premises). Conversion changes the subject and predicate of the minor premise (e.g., 'Some A is B' becomes 'Some B is A'), and reversal places the major premise first.
After reduction, the syllogism becomes a first-figure mood, which is directly valid.
4
Apply reduction to a concrete example
Take a standard third-figure mood: 'Every human is mortal' (major premise), 'Every human is rational' (minor). The minor premise 'Every human is rational' does not convert simply; but if we had 'Some human is rational', conversion yields 'Some rational is human'. Then swap premises to get a first-figure syllogism.
Not all third-figure moods reduce identically; some require additional steps like contraposition, but the passage indicates a uniform method for these six.

Four Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Author lists four moods of the fourth figure with reduction feasibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four moods of the fourth figure and determine which are reducible to the first figure based on the quality of premises.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's moods are productive only if reducible to the first figure; a negative major prevents reduction.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogisms has four moods, but not all are equally useful. This passage lists them and indicates which can be reduced to the first figure. Understanding reduction is key to validating arguments in non-first figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure: fourth figure.
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor premise. The author lists four moods, each with a conclusion and a reduction note.
2
Extract the conclusion of each mood.
For each mood, the conclusion is given. Write down the subject and predicate of each proposition. For example, the first mood's conclusion is 'Human is animal' and 'every rational is human' is part of the reduction method.
The conclusion may be a single proposition; the second part may indicate the reduction method.
3
Analyze the reduction method.
The author uses the method of taking the contradictory (naqid) of the conclusion as the minor premise and then converting the major premise, to see if it yields a first-figure syllogism.
This is standard reduction by 'aks al-naqid.
4
Determine which moods pass.
The first three moods are marked 'na'am' (yes) meaning reducible. The fourth mood is marked 'la' (no) because the major premise is negative, making conversion impossible.

The fourth figure: Productive moods: First: Its conclusion: Human is animal and every rational is human. Yes, by taking the contradictory of the conclusion and making it a minor, then converting the major... Second: Its conclusion: Human is animal and some rational is human. Yes, by the method of the first mood... Third: Its conclusion: Human is animal and no stone is not a human. Yes, by taking the contradictory of the conclusion and making it a minor... Fourth: Its conclusion: Human is animal and some stone is not a human. No, because the major is negative and not suitable...
Source pages p. 812

Commentary
This passage presents the four productive moods of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The fourth figure is defined by the middle term being the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor premise. The author lists each mood with its conclusion and a note on whether it can be reduced to the first figure. The reduction method typically involves taking the contradictory of the conclusion and using it as a minor premise, then converting the major premise. The first three moods are marked as reducible ('na'am'), while the fourth mood is rejected because its major premise is negative and cannot be converted appropriately. This illustrates the strict conditions for reduction: premises must be of the right quality and quantity. The commentary underscores that not all valid moods in the fourth figure are equally useful; some require special handling.

Fourth Figure Moods 1-4 and Their Reduction
Author lists first four moods of fourth figure with reduction methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first four moods of the fourth figure and determine which reduction methods (contradiction, conversion of major) are applicable for each.

Core Idea
Fourth figure moods are valid and reducible to the first figure via specific techniques.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, though often considered less natural, produces valid conclusions. This lesson examines the first four moods of the fourth figure as presented in a table, along with the methods used to reduce them to the first figure: contradiction (khalf) and conversion of the major premise. You will learn to recognize which reduction works for each mood and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's conclusion
Look at the example mood's conclusion in the table (e.g., 'Some human is animal' for the first mood). Understand the quantity and quality of the statement.
The conclusion is the target proposition to be proved.
2
Check reduction via contradiction
Determine if assuming the opposite of the conclusion leads to a contradiction with the premises. The table marks this as 'yes' (نعم) or 'no' (لا).
Khalf reduction: assume the contradictory, combine with premises to derive a contradiction.
3
Check reduction via conversion of major
See if converting the major premise (swapping subject and predicate) yields a first-figure syllogism. The table indicates if this method works.
‘Aks al-kubra: conversion must preserve truth (e.g., universal affirmative becomes particular affirmative).
4
Compare both methods
Note which methods are marked 'yes' for each mood. The table provides brief justifications in the garbled text.
Some moods may only work with one method; others with both.
5
Verify the reduction
For a method that works, construct the resulting first-figure syllogism mentally to confirm validity.
This step reinforces understanding of reduction logic.

Fourth Figure Moods 5-8: Reduction Methods and Conditions
Table of fourth figure moods with reduction proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which reduction method (contradiction, conversion of major/minor) works for a given fourth figure mood and identify the conditions that make a method fail.

Core Idea
Not all reduction methods work for every mood—success depends on the quantity and quality of premises.

The fourth figure has eight productive moods. This passage lists moods five through eight, each with a conclusion and various reduction attempts. Some methods succeed, others fail due to specific properties of the premises, such as a negative minor or a particular major. Understanding why a method fails is as important as knowing why it succeeds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and its conclusion
For each mood, note the quantity and quality of its conclusion (universal/particular, affirmative/negative). This determines which reduction methods are even candidates.
For example, mood 5 has a particular affirmative conclusion.
2
List the candidate reduction methods
The table considers three methods: reduction by contradiction, conversion of the major premise, and conversion of the minor premise. Some moods also try reordering premises.
Not all methods are attempted for every mood; the table shows which ones work or fail.
3
Apply each method and test the result
For each candidate method, transform the mood into a first figure syllogism. Check if the resulting mood meets the first figure conditions (major universal, minor affirmative, etc.).
For conversion, ensure the premise can be validly converted (e.g., a negative universal converts, but a particular affirmative only converts by accident).
4
Identify the reason for failure
The table marks failures with 'no' and often gives a reason in shorthand (e.g., 'صُغراهُ سالبة' meaning the minor is negative). Determine exactly which condition is violated.
Common reasons: negative minor cannot serve as minor in first figure; particular major fails because major must be universal in first figure.
5
Verify the successful reduction
For the methods marked 'yes', reconstruct the reduction step by step to confirm that the resulting first figure syllogism is valid. This builds confidence in the method.
Successful methods often involve contradiction, then conversion of one premise.
