Why Order Conversion Fails for First and Third Moods
Author explains impossibility of reduction via order conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the first and third moods of the first figure cannot be reduced by converting premise order, and distinguish valid reduction methods (khalf or aks al-kubra).

Core Idea
Order conversion breaks first-figure rules: minor must be affirmative.

In this passage, the commentator explains why certain moods of the first figure cannot be proven by simply swapping the premises. Understanding these constraints is essential for correctly reducing syllogisms. We will walk through the reasoning and see how figure-specific conditions determine permissible reduction methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the moods in question
The first and third moods are defined: first mood = universal affirmative minor + universal negative major; third mood = particular affirmative minor + universal negative major. Note their components.
These moods are from the standard first-figure list.
2
Examine the proposed reduction: order conversion
Order conversion means swapping the minor and major premises. The passage argues this fails for these moods. Ask: what would happen to the quality and quantity of each premise after swapping?
3
Check the figure rules after swap
In the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative. After order conversion, the originally negative major becomes the minor. Since it is negative, it violates this rule. Also, for the third mood, the minor after conversion becomes particular (due to conversion of the particular affirmative), and the figure requires the major to be universal; but after swap the major would be particular, violating another rule.
The passage mentions two violations: negative minor and particular major.
4
Compare valid reduction methods
The passage states that khalf (reductio) and aks al-kubra (conversion of major) are valid. Understand how these preserve the figure conditions. Khalf assumes the contradictory of the conclusion, avoiding premise swap. Aks al-kubra converts the major premise (universal negative to universal negative after conversion) without violating the minor's affirmativeness.
This reinforces the importance of the minor premise's quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا معنى قوله : ثمَّ عكسُ النَّتيجة». واعلَمْ أنَّ الضَّربَ الأَوَّلَ والثَّالث يمكن بيان إنتاجهما بالخُلف وبعكس الكبرى، ولا يمكن بعكس الترتيب؛ لأنَّه إِذا عُكِسَ التَّرتيب ؛ وقعت السالبة صغرى، والسالبة لا تصلح لِصُغُرَوِيَّةِ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّل . وأيضاً يلزمُ وقوع الجزئية في الضَّرْبِ الثَّالث كبرى.
This is the meaning of his statement: 'Then the conversion of the conclusion.' And know that the first and third moods can have their productivity demonstrated by reductio ad absurdum and by conversion of the major, but cannot by conversion of order, because if the order is converted, the negative falls as minor, and the negative is not suitable for the minorship of the first figure. Also, in the third mood, particularity necessarily occurs in the major.
Source pages p. 814

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on why certain moods of the first figure cannot be proven by converting the order of premises (عكس الترتيب). The author explains that the first and third moods (defined by Dasuqi as: first = universal affirmative minor + universal negative major; third = particular affirmative minor + universal negative major) can be proven by reductio ad absurdum (الخلف) or by converting the major premise, but not by swapping the premises. The reason is that if you swap them, the negative premise becomes the minor premise. In the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative; a negative minor is invalid. Additionally, for the third mood, swapping would make the major premise particular (due to conversion of the minor), which again breaks the rule. This demonstrates the strict conditions of the first figure.

Why 'Aks al-Tartib Fails for the First and Third Moods
Author explains why 'aks al-tartib fails

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which reduction methods are applicable to the first and third moods of the first figure and explain why conversion of order is invalid.

Core Idea
The first figure requires an affirmative minor and a universal major; swapping premises violates these constraints.

This passage outlines the structural rules of the first figure and how they restrict which reduction methods work. Understanding why 'aks al-tartib fails is key to mastering syllogistic validity tests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first and third moods
Recall that the first mood (Barbara) has universal affirmative minor and universal negative major; the third mood (Darii) has particular affirmative minor and universal negative major.
2
Understand 'aks al-tartib
Conversion of order means swapping the minor and major premises. After swapping, the original minor becomes the major and vice versa.
3
Check the new minor against first figure conditions
After swapping, the new minor (originally the major) is a universal negative. But the first figure requires the minor to be affirmative. Thus the swapped premises violate the rule.
For the third mood, the new major becomes particular, which also violates the universal major condition.
4
Conclude why 'aks al-tartib is invalid
Because the result violates the first figure's necessary conditions, this reduction method cannot be used for these moods.

Source Text

This is the meaning of his statement: 'then the conversion of the conclusion.' And know that the first and third moods can have their productiveness shown by the indirect proof and by conversion of the major premise, but cannot by conversion of the order; because if the order is converted, the negative premise becomes the minor, and the negative is not suitable for being the minor of the first figure. Also, the occurrence of the particular in the third mood as major is necessary.
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'And this' means his statement: 'and it converts... etc.') (His statement: 'the first mood' means it is composed of an affirmative universal minor and a negative major. (His statement: 'and the third' means it is composed of an affirmative particular minor and a negative universal major. (His statement: 'It is possible to show their productiveness by the indirect proof... etc.') Know that it is possible to show the third mood by hypothesis: by supposing the subject of the minor as D; then two premises are obtained, one is 'Every D' and the other is 'Every D is J'. Then you make the first the minor for the original major, as: 'Every D is B' and 'Nothing of A is B' produces from the first figure 'Nothing of D is A'. Then you convert the second premise to 'Some J is D' and combine it with the result of this syllogism, as: 'Some J is D' and 'Nothing of D is A' produces from the first figure 'Some J is not A', which is what was sought. (His statement: 'for the minorship of the figure' means it is not suitable to be the minor of the first figure because it is required that its minor be affirmative. (His statement: 'And also it is necessary... etc.') means as it is necessary for the occurrence of the negative in it as minor of the first figure. (His statement: 'in the third mood' rather, and in the first; since the affirmative universal minor must be converted, and it converts as particular.) (Al-Attar)
Source pages p. 814

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the reduction methods for the first and third moods of the first figure. The commentator begins by clarifying that the meaning of the earlier statement 'then the conversion of the conclusion' is what follows. He then states a general principle: the first and third moods (Barbara and Darii) can have their validity demonstrated by two methods: (1) indirect proof (khalaf) and (2) conversion of the major premise ('aks al-kubra). However, they cannot be demonstrated by conversion of the order ('aks al-tartib, i.e., swapping the minor and major premises). The reason given is that if you swap the premises, the minor becomes a negative proposition, but the first figure requires the minor to be affirmative. Additionally, for the third mood, swapping would make the major a particular proposition, which is also invalid for the first figure (the major must be universal). Al-Dasuqi then provides detailed definitions: the first mood has an affirmative universal minor and a negative universal major; the third mood has an affirmative particular minor and a negative universal major. He then explains a hypothetical demonstration (bi al-iftiradh) for the third mood: suppose D is the subject of the minor premise, then from 'Every D is B' (from the original minor) and 'No A is B' (original major), we infer 'No D is A'. Then convert the original minor 'Every D is J' to 'Some J is D', and combine with 'No D is A' to get 'Some J is not A' via the first figure. This shows the validity of the third mood indirectly. The commentary also notes that even for the first mood, converting the affirmative universal minor results in a particular, which is still permissible as a minor in the first figure? Actually al-Attar adds that the universal affirmative minor converts to a particular, and that particular can serve as minor again; but the point is that the conversion of the order leads to an invalid configuration. The passage thus reinforces the structural constraints of the first figure: minor must be affirmative, major must be universal.

ضرب أول
ضرب ثالث
سالبة
صغرى

C) Because both premises become particular.
D) Because the conclusion becomes universal.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's structural rules—affirmative minor and universal major—determine which reduction methods are permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a kalam argument: 'All temporally originated things are contingent; the world is temporally originated; therefore the world is contingent.' Identify the mood and check if 'aks al-tartib would be valid. Explain why or why not.
?

Showing Productiveness by Iftirad (Hypothesis)
Author explains the iftirad method for the third mood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the hypothetical demonstration (iftirad) for the third mood of the first figure.

Core Idea
By introducing a hypothetical term, the third mood's validity is shown through two first-figure syllogisms.

The passage introduces a detailed method called 'iftirad' (hypothetical demonstration) to prove the third mood. This method uses a new term D to break the mood into simpler steps that conform to the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Set up the original third mood
The third mood (Darii) has premises: Some J is D (minor) and No D is A (major). Conclusion: Some J is not A.
We use J for the subject of the minor, D for the middle, A for the major.
2
Introduce a hypothetical term
Take D as the subject of the minor premise. Then from the two premises 'Every D is B' (from the original: actually the minor is 'Some J is D', wait: the commentary uses D as the subject of the minor, saying 'suppose the subject of the minor is D'. Let's rephrase: The original minor is 'Some J is D'. We introduce D as a term representing the subject of the minor. Actually the commentary says: 'by supposing the subject of the minor as D' meaning we take an arbitrary D that falls under the minor subject. Then we use the original major 'No A is B'? The commentary says: 'Every D is B' from the original minor? Let's follow the commentary: 'from 'Every D is B' (from original minor) and 'No A is B' (original major) we infer 'No D is A'. Then convert the original minor to 'Some J is D'. Then from 'Some J is D' and 'No D is A' we infer 'Some J is not A'.
This uses two first-figure syllogisms: first to get 'No D is A', then the second to get the final conclusion.
3
First syllogism: from the original premises
From 'Every D is B' (the original minor converted to universal? Actually the minor 'Some J is D' is not universal. The commentary says: 'Then you make the first the minor for the original major, as: 'Every D is B' and 'Nothing of A is B' produces from the first figure 'Nothing of D is A'.' It seems they assume that the minor premise can be made universal by hypothesizing that every D is J? This is complicated. For pedagogical clarity, we simplify: The iftirad method introduces a new term that is a subset of the minor subject. Let's follow the text: 'by supposing the subject of the minor as D; then two premises are obtained, one is 'Every D' and the other is 'Every D is J'.' Actually the text says: 'كلُّ د' and 'كلُّ دج' meaning 'Every D' and 'Every D is J'. Then they make the first the minor for the original major, as: 'Every D is B' and 'Nothing of A is B' produces 'Nothing of D is A'.
This step is the core of the iftirad.
4
Second syllogism: using the converted minor and the result
Then convert the second premise 'Every D is J' to 'Some J is D' (by conversion of affirmative universal to particular). Then combine 'Some J is D' with 'Nothing of D is A' to get 'Some J is not A' via first figure (Celarent? Actually it's Darii-like: particular affirmative minor, universal negative major yields particular negative).
This completes the demonstration.

Reduction of the Second Mood
Author explains reduction possibilities for second mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which reduction methods apply to the second mood of the first figure and explain why conversion of the major fails.

Core Idea
The second mood (Celarent) reduces by khalaf and order swap, but not by major conversion.

In this lesson, we examine the reduction methods for the second mood of the first figure (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major). The text explains that while indirect proof and premise swapping work, converting the major premise is impossible because it would produce a particular proposition, which is not allowed as a major in the first figure. Understanding these constraints is crucial for mastering syllogistic reduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood composition
The second mood consists of a universal negative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise.
This is the Celarent mood (EAE in first figure).
2
Test each reduction method
Check khalaf (indirect proof) and aks al-tartib (conversion of order); both are valid. Then check aks al-kubra (conversion of major); it fails.
The author explicitly states which methods work.
3
Explain the failure of aks al-kubra
The major premise is universal affirmative; its conversion yields a particular affirmative proposition. But the first figure requires the major premise to be universal. Hence the converted proposition cannot serve as a valid major.
This is the core logical reason given in the text.

And the particular is not suitable for being the major of the first figure. And the second mood can have its productiveness shown by indirect proof and by conversion of the order, but not by conversion of the major premise; because due to its affirmation, it converts only to a particular, and the particular is not suitable for being the major of the first figure. As for the fourth mood, its productiveness cannot be shown by conversion of the major premise; because due to its affirmation, it converts only to a particular, and it is not suitable for being the major of the first figure, nor by conversion of the order; because the minor is particular negative, and it does not convert, ......
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'is not suitable for being the major of the first figure' means it is not suitable to be the major of the first figure because the first figure requires its major to be universal. (His statement: 'the second mood' means it is composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major. (His statement: 'as for the fourth mood' means it is composed of a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major. (His statement: 'does not occur as major of the first figure' means because if it were to convert, it would convert as a particular negative like itself, and the particular does not occur as major of the first figure, as has passed.
Al-Attar
·
(His statement: 'because due to its affirmation' means because the major, being universal affirmative in the second mood, converts only to a particular affirmative, and what is required here is a universal negative. (His statement: 'and it does not convert' according to the chosen view, and conversion is necessary in the conversion of the major, and his statement: 'and if it were to convert, it would be like itself' according to the weak view, thus said Rajab Efendi, and the truth is that there is no disagreement that the particular negative does not convert; yes, if it has one of the two properties, it converts like itself; that is possible according to modality, and the discussion here is according to quantity. And the statement of the commentator: 'and if it were to convert' means as a hypothetical and concession, not that it is a narrative; it is a weak opinion; reflect.)
Source pages p. 815

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the syllogism, continuing the discussion of reduction methods for the moods of the first figure. It addresses the second and fourth moods. The chapter begins by reiterating that a particular proposition cannot serve as the major premise in the first figure (the major must be universal). Then it states that the second mood (Celarent: universal negative minor, universal affirmative major) can be reduced by indirect proof (khalaf) and by conversion of the order (swap premises), but not by conversion of the major premise ('aks al-kubra). The reason: the major is universal affirmative; converting it yields a particular affirmative, which is not allowed as a major in the first figure. For the fourth mood (Ferio: particular negative minor, universal affirmative major), neither method works: conversion of the major fails for the same reason (affirmative converts to particular), and conversion of the order fails because the minor is particular negative, and a particular negative proposition does not convert (it cannot be swapped to become a valid premise). Al-Dasuqi clarifies the definitions: second mood = universal negative minor + universal affirmative major; fourth mood = particular negative minor + universal affirmative major. Al-Attar adds technical details about the conversion of the particular negative: according to the chosen view (al-qawl al-mukhtar), particular negatives do not convert at all; on a weak view, they might convert as themselves (i.e., remain particular negative), but that would still be invalid because the major must be universal. The discussion underscores that reduction methods are constrained by the quantity and quality of premises, and that only certain transformations preserve the validity conditions of the first figure.

الضرب الثاني
كُبرى الشكل الأول
لا تنعكس

Reduction of the Fourth Mood
Author explains why fourth mood cannot be reduced by either method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the fourth mood of the first figure cannot be reduced by either aks al-kubra or aks al-tartib.

Core Idea
The fourth mood (Ferio) is irreducible because its particular negative minor cannot convert, and the major converts to an invalid particular.

The fourth mood of the first figure presents a special challenge: neither standard reduction method works. The text explains why conversion of the major fails (same reason as second mood) and why conversion of the order fails (the minor is particular negative and does not convert). This demonstrates the strict limitations of reduction to the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood composition
The fourth mood consists of a particular negative minor premise and a universal affirmative major premise.
This is the Ferio mood (EIO in first figure).
2
Test aks al-kubra
The major is universal affirmative; it converts to a particular affirmative. Since the first figure major must be universal, the converted proposition is invalid.
Same reason as in the second mood.
3
Test aks al-tartib
Swapping the premises would require converting the minor (particular negative). But a particular negative proposition does not convert (according to the chosen view). Hence no valid reduction.
The text explicitly states 'wa hiya la tan'akis' (it does not convert).

Methods of Reducing Third and Fourth Figure Syllogisms
Author enumerates the methods by which moods of third and fourth figures are reduced to first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four methods of reducing third and fourth figure moods to the first figure and identify which moods admit multiple methods.

Core Idea
Reduction to the first figure validates syllogisms in later figures.

The author of the Tadhheeb explains that the productive moods of the third and fourth figures can be reduced to the first figure using four methods: per impossible (al-khalf), conversion of the major premise, conversion of the minor premise, or reversal of the order of premises. This passage provides a high-level overview, noting that not every method works for every mood. Understanding these methods is essential for constructing sound theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four reduction methods
From the passage, extract the four methods: (1) al-khalf (proof by contradiction), (2) aks al-kubra (conversion of major), (3) aks al-sughra (conversion of minor), and (4) aks al-tartib (reversal of premise order). Note the Arabic terms.
2
Understand how each method works
For each method, recall its operation: khalf assumes the opposite of the conclusion and derives a contradiction; conversion swaps subject and predicate of a premise; reversal exchanges the order of premises to match the first figure.
Conversion rules vary by quantity and quality; review if needed.
3
Analyze which methods apply to a given mood
Not every method works for every mood. For a concrete third figure mood, e.g., 'Some believers are righteous; all believers are truthful; therefore some righteous are truthful', test which methods would produce a valid first figure syllogism.
4
Generalize the principle
Conclude that some moods can be reduced by two or more methods, while others require a specific one. The passage says this becomes clear by reflection (ta'ammul), so practice on various moods.

Commentary
This passage provides a general statement about how the productive moods of the third and fourth figures can be reduced to the first figure. The author lists four methods: (1) reduction per impossible (khalf), (2) conversion of the major premise, (3) conversion of the minor premise, and (4) reversal of the order of premises (transposition). He notes that not every method works for every mood; some moods can be demonstrated by two or more methods, while others can only be demonstrated by one. The details are promised to come later ('as will come'). The author emphasizes that all of this becomes clear through careful reflection (ta'ammul). This sets the stage for the detailed discussion of each mood in the later sections. The reader should note that the methods listed are all ways to convert a mood of the third or fourth figure into a mood of the first figure, which is the standard of demonstration (burhan).

Conditions of the Third Figure: Affirmative and Actual Minor
Author states the two conditions for the third figure and commentators explain their necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a valid third-figure syllogism and test syllogisms for their satisfaction.

Core Idea
A valid third-figure syllogism requires an affirmative and actual minor premise.

In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. The logicians laid down two conditions for this figure to produce a certain conclusion: the minor premise must be affirmative and actual. The commentators al-Dasuqi and al-Attar illustrate why these conditions are necessary through specific examples from everyday life. Understanding these conditions will help you evaluate the validity of syllogisms in Islamic theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Confirm that the middle term is the subject in both premises. In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both the major and minor premises.
2
Check the minor's quality
The minor premise must be affirmative. If it is negative, the syllogism will not produce a unique conclusion (as al-Attar's examples show).
3
Check the minor's modality
The minor premise must be actual (fi'liyya), not merely possible. If it is possible, the conclusion may be false even when premises are true, as al-Dasuqi's example demonstrates.
4
Verify the major
No condition on the major's quality or modality; it can be affirmative or negative, and its modality is unrestricted.
5
Test with an example
Apply these conditions to a real syllogism from Islamic fiqh or kalam to see if it satisfies the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْقِيَاسِ
[ شروط الشَّكلِ الثَّالِثِ وضروبه المنتجة] (وَ) يُشترَطُ (فِي) الشَّكل (الثَّالِثِ): بحسب الكيفِ إِيْجَابُ الصُّغْرَى).
(وَ) بحسب الجهةِ (فِعْلِيَّتُهَا).
قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : فِعْلِيَّتُهَا) أي : الصُّغرى، وأمَّا الكُبرى ؛ فَلَا يُشترط فعليّتُها . (قَوْلُهُ: وَفِعْلِيَّتُهَا) إِذْ لو كانت الصغرى ممكنةً؛ لم تنتج، أَلا ترى أنَّ زيداً إذا ركب الفرس فقط، وعمراً ركب الحمار فقط ؛ صدق (۱) : كلُّ ما هو مَركوب زيدٍ مركوبُ عَمْرو بالإمكان، وكلُّ ما هو مَركوب زيدٍ فرس بالضرورة، وكذبَ : بعضُ ما هو مركوب عمرو فرس بالإمكان ؛ لأنَّ مَركوبه بالفعل حمار بالضرورة.
قال العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : إِيْجَابُ الصُّغْرَى) إذْ لَو كانَتْ سالبةٌ ؛ فَالكُبرى إمَّا موجبة أو سالبةٌ، وأيا مَا كانَ؛ يحصلُ الاختلافُ في النتيجة الموجب للعقم، فَالكُبرى الموجبة كَقَولِنَا : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بفرس وَكُلُّ إنسان ناطق، والحقُّ السَّلْبُ، وهو قولنا : لا شيء مِنَ الفرس بِنَاطق، ولو بدَّلْنَا الكُبرى بِقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان؛ كانَ الحَقُّ الإيجاب، وهو قولنا : كُلَّ فرسٍ حيوان، وأمَّا السَّالبةُ فَكَقَولِنَا : لَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الإِنسانِ بفرس وَلَا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بحمارٍ ، والحقُّ السَّلْبُ، وَهُوَ قولُنَا : لا الفرس بحمارٍ ، وَلَو بدَّلْنَا الكُبرى بِقَولِنَا : لا شيء مِنَ الإِنسانِ بِصَهَالٍ؛ كَانَ الحَقُّ الإيجاب، وهو قولنا : كُلُّ صَيَّالٌ. فرس.
Chapter: On the Syllogism. [Conditions of the third figure and its productive moods] (And) it is required (in) the (third) figure: in terms of quality: the affirmation of the minor premise. (And) in terms of modality: its actuality. al-Dasuqi said: (His saying: 'its actuality') i.e., the minor premise; as for the major, its actuality is not required. (His saying: 'and its actuality') Since if the minor were contingent, it would not produce a conclusion. Do you not see that if Zayd only rides a horse, and Amr only rides a donkey, then it is true: 'Everything ridden by Zayd is ridden by Amr possibly', and 'Everything ridden by Zayd is a horse necessarily', and it is false: 'Something ridden by Amr is a horse possibly' because what he actually rides is a donkey necessarily. al-Attar said: (His saying: 'affirmation of the minor') Since if it were negative, then the major is either affirmative or negative, and in either case, there occurs a difference in the conclusion that causes sterility. So the affirmative major is like our saying: 'Nothing of the human is a horse' and 'Every human is rational', and the truth is the negative, which is our saying: 'Nothing of the horse is rational'. And if we change the major to our saying: 'Every human is an animal', then the truth is the affirmative, which is our saying: 'Every horse is an animal'. As for the negative major, it is like our saying: 'Nothing of the human is a horse' and 'Nothing of the human is a donkey', and the truth is the negative, which is our saying: 'No horse is a donkey'. And if we change the major to our saying: 'Nothing of the human neighs', then the truth is the affirmative, which is our saying: 'Everything that neighs is a horse.'
Source pages p. 816

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the conditions for the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The matn (core text) states two conditions: (1) in terms of quality (kayf), the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) in terms of modality (jiha), the minor premise must be actual (fi'liyya). The commentators expand on why these conditions are necessary. al-Dasuqi explains that the condition of 'actuality' applies only to the minor premise, not the major. If the minor is merely possible (mumkina), the syllogism fails to produce a certain conclusion. He illustrates with an example: suppose Zayd only rides a horse (actual), and Amr only rides a donkey (actual). Then we can truly say 'Everything ridden by Zayd is possibly ridden by Amr' (since there is no contradiction) and 'Everything ridden by Zayd is necessarily a horse'. But the conclusion 'Something ridden by Amr is possibly a horse' is false because Amr actually rides a donkey, not a horse. The falsity of the conclusion despite true premises shows that when the minor is possible, the syllogism is invalid. al-Attar explains the condition of affirmation. If the minor is negative, then regardless of whether the major is affirmative or negative, the conclusion becomes indeterminate or contradictory, leading to sterility (no necessary conclusion). He gives examples: if we say 'Nothing human is a horse' (minor negative) and 'Every human is rational' (major affirmative), we cannot conclude that horses are rational; rather the truth is that no horse is rational. Changing the major to 'Every human is an animal' forces an affirmative conclusion (every horse is an animal), but this is not derived from the premises themselves; it is a separate inference. Similarly with a negative major, the conclusion flips depending on the terms. Thus, to avoid such confusion, the minor must be affirmative.

Key Terms
صُغْرَى
كُبْرَى
شكل ثالث
إيجاب
فعلية

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Conditions of the Third Figure: Quality, Modality, Quantity
Conditions of the third figure and its productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions necessary for a valid third-figure syllogism and explain why each condition is required.

Core Idea
Three conditions — affirmative minor, actual minor, at least one universal — unlock the productive moods of the third figure.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has its own set of strict conditions. In this passage, the author enumerates them precisely: quality, modality, and quantity. Mastering these conditions will allow you to check any third-figure argument for validity and to construct your own sound syllogisms in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality condition
The first condition is that the minor premise must be affirmative (positive). If it were negative, the premises would differ in quality, leading to sterility (no conclusion). Look for the phrase 'إيجاب الصغرى' in the Arabic.
This applies only to the minor premise; the major may be affirmative or negative as needed.
2
Identify the modality condition
The minor premise must also be actual (fi'liyya), not merely possible. This means it asserts actual existence. Without this condition, the middle term might not connect the terms in reality. The Arabic uses 'فعلية الصغرى'.
If the minor is possible (mumkin), the conclusion may not follow even if premises are true.
3
Identify the quantity condition
At least one of the two premises must be universal in quantity (kulliyya). If both were particular, the middle term could apply to different subsets, preventing a necessary connection. The phrase 'كلية إحداهما' indicates this condition.
It does not matter which premise is universal; either the minor or the major suffices.
4
Apply the conditions to derive productive moods
When all three conditions are met, the third figure yields valid moods. The author specifies that the two minors (one universal affirmative, one particular affirmative) with the major affirmative universal, or the minor universal and major particular, or the minor particular and major universal, produce a conclusion. This gives six valid moods (e.g., Darapti, Disamis, Datisi, Felapton, Ferison, Bocardo).
The conditions ensure the middle term is properly distributed and the terms connect without fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
[شروط الشَّكلِ الثَّالث وضروبه المنتجة]
(وَ) يُشترَطُ (فِي) الشَّكل (الثَّالِثِ):
بحسب الكيفِ إِيْجَابُ الصُّغْرَى).
۲. (وَ) بحسب الجهةِ (فِعْلِيَّتُهَا).
۳. (و) بحسب الكم، أن يكونَ مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ إِحْدَاهُمَا؛ أي: إحدى المقدمتين من الصُّغرى والكبرى.
(لِيُنْتِجَ الصُّغريان (الْمُوجِبَتَانِ)؛ أي: الكلية والجزئية (مَع) الكبرى الْمُوْجِبَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، أَوْ بِالْعَكْسِ)؛
Chapter: On Syllogism. [Conditions of the Third Figure and its Productive Moods] It is stipulated in the third figure: (1) according to quality, the affirmation of the minor (premise). (2) And according to modality, its actuality. (3) And according to quantity, that there be with universality of one of them; i.e., one of the two premises, the minor or the major. (So that) the two minors (affirmatives) (i.e., the universal and the particular) with the major affirmative universal, or vice versa, produce (a conclusion).
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage enumerating the three conditions necessary for a syllogism in the third figure to produce a valid conclusion. The author (or commentator) first lists the conditions in a numbered format. The first condition is qualitative: the minor premise must be affirmative (positive). If it were negative, the conclusion would be problematic due to difference in quality between premises leading to sterility. The second condition concerns modality: the minor premise must be actual (fi'liyya), meaning it must assert actual existence, not possibility. If it were possible, the conclusion would not follow, as illustrated by the example of Zayd and Amr riding different animals. The third condition is quantitative: at least one of the two premises must be universal (kulliyya). If both were particular, the middle term might be applied to different subsets, preventing the transfer of judgment from the middle term to the minor term. After stating these conditions, the passage indicates that when these conditions are met, the productive moods of the third figure are obtained: the two affirmative minors (one universal and one particular) with the major affirmative universal, or vice versa (i.e., the minor universal and major particular or the minor particular and major universal). This yields six valid moods in the third figure. The author's enumeration is precise and serves as a foundation for understanding the third figure's validity requirements.

إيجاب الصغرى
فعلية الصغرى
كلية إحداهما

Why One Premise Must Be Universal in the Third Figure
Commentary explains the condition that one premise must be universal in third figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a third figure categorical syllogism requires at least one universal premise and identify the valid premise combinations.

Core Idea
Two particular premises break the link between terms in the third figure.

In the third figure syllogism, the middle term is the subject in both premises. The commentator explains that if both premises were particular, there would be no guarantee that the same part of the middle term is being referred to. Therefore, at least one premise must be universal to ensure overlap and allow a conclusion. This lesson unpacks that condition and the valid combinations.

Guided Path
1
Recall the third figure structure
In the third figure, the middle term is subject in both premises. For example, 'All B are C' and 'Some B are A' have middle term B as subject.
2
Identify the condition
The passage states: 'one of the two premises must be universal' (كلية إحداهما). This applies to both possible arrangements of minor and major.
3
List valid premise combinations
The valid combinations are: (1) universal affirmative minor with particular affirmative major, or (2) particular affirmative minor with universal affirmative major. Both yield a particular affirmative conclusion.
The phrase 'or vice versa' only refers to the second combination, not any other permutation.
4
Understand the reasoning behind the condition
If both premises were particular, the middle term might refer to different subsets in each premise. For instance, 'Some B are C' and 'Some B are A' could involve two different groups of B. Then the judgment linking C to one group of B might not apply to the group connected to A, so no conclusion follows.
5
Apply the rule to a concrete example
Consider: 'Some animals are horses' (minor) and 'Some horses are mammals' (major). Here both premises are particular. The middle term 'horses' might refer to different horses in each premise, so you cannot conclude anything about animals and mammals. This violates the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ. (و) بحسب الكم، أن يكونَ مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ إِحْدَاهُمَا)؛ أي: إحدى المقدمتين من الصُّغرى والكبرى. (لِيُنْتِجَ الصُّغريان (الْمُوجِبَتَانِ)؛ أي: الكلية والجزئية (مَع) الكبرى الْمُوْجِبَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، أَوْ بِالْعَكْسِ)؛ أي: الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلِّيَّةِ مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية (مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ). وفي العبارة تسامح; لأنَّ قولَهُ بِالعَكس يُفهَمُ منه أن يكونَ... ووجه اشتراطِ كُلِّيَّةِ إحداهما : أَنَّهُما لَو كانتا جزئيتين ; لاحتمل أن يكون البعضُ مِنَ الأوسط المحكوم عليه بالأكبر غير البعض من الأوسط المحكوم عليه بالأصغرِ، فَلَا يتعدَّى الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر.
Chapter on Syllogism. (And) with respect to quantity, that one of them be universal; i.e., one of the two premises, the minor and the major. (So that the two minors produce) the two affirmatives; i.e., the universal and the particular (with) the universal affirmative major, or vice versa; i.e., the universal affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major (results in) a particular affirmative. And in the expression there is looseness, because his saying 'or vice versa' would be understood to mean... And the reason for the condition that one of them be universal: if both were particular, it would be possible that the part of the middle term judged by the major is other than the part of the middle term judged by the minor, so the judgment does not transfer from the middle to the minor.
Source pages p. 818

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on syllogism, specifically discussing the third figure (where the middle term is subject in both premises). The author states a condition regarding quantity: one of the two premises must be universal. The minor premise must be affirmative (either universal or particular), and the major must be universal affirmative, or vice versa — meaning the minor is universal affirmative and the major is particular affirmative. The result is a particular affirmative conclusion. There is noted looseness in the expression 'or vice versa' because it could imply the opposite arrangement, but the intended meaning is only the second case (minor universal, major particular). The reason given for the condition that one premise be universal is that if both were particular, the middle term might refer to different subsets, preventing a connection between the minor and major terms. This is a standard justification: with two particular premises, there is no guarantee that the instances overlap, so no conclusion follows.

Resolving Ambiguity in Syllogistic Mood Descriptions
Commentator explains tasamuh in 'or by conversion' and lists three valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify tasamuh (looseness) in a logical text and use contextual redundancy to determine the intended specific syllogistic mood.

Core Idea
Context resolves tasamuh: a general expression may refer to only one specific mood when another is redundant.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a potential ambiguity in the phrase 'or by conversion' within a rule for syllogistic moods. By recognising that one possible interpretation is already covered elsewhere, he shows how to hone in on the intended meaning. This skill—disambiguating logical shorthand—is crucial for accurately applying syllogistic rules in Islamic theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
Locate the expression 'or by conversion' in the text. Notice that it could mean that the order of premises is reversed, implying two possible mood combinations.
2
List the two possible interpretations
The phrase could produce: (1) universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major, or (2) universal affirmative minor with particular affirmative major.
3
Check for redundancy with earlier statements
The first interpretation is already covered by the earlier phrase 'the two affirmatives with the universal affirmative'. Therefore it would be redundant to repeat it.
This redundancy signals that only the second interpretation is intended.
4
Confirm the intended specific meaning
The commentator concludes that the phrase actually means only the second mood (universal affirmative minor with particular affirmative major), and he notes that this is a looseness (tasamuh) in expression.
5
Recognise the three resulting moods
After disambiguation, the rule yields three moods producing a particular affirmative conclusion. The first is explicitly given: every B is C, every B is A → some C is A. The other two follow similarly.
This step reinforces how disambiguating one phrase clarifies the entire list.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ أي : الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلِّيَّةِ مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية (مُوجَبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ) مفعول لينتج . وفي العبارة تسامح ؛ لأنَّ قولَهُ بِالعَكس» يُفهَمُ منه أن يكونَ الكبريانِ الموجبتانِ مع الصُّغرى الموجبةِ الكلَّيَّة، وحينئذ يحصل ضربان: الأول : الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلّيّة مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية. والثَّاني : الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلّيّة مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية . لكنَّ الضَّربَ الأَوَّلَ داخل في قولِهِ : لينتج الموجبتانِ مع الموجبة الكلِّيَّة، فتعيَّن أن يُراد به الضَّربُ الثَّاني فقط ؛ أي: الصغرى الموجبة الكلِّيَّة مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية، على ما فشرناه بذلك. ولا يخفى أنَّ قولَه : بِالعَكس» يُفهَمُ منه الضربان، فإطلاقه وإرادة ضرب واحدٍ يكون تسامحاً . فالمفهوم من قولِهِ : لينتج الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية، أو بالعكس ثلاثة أضرب منتجة للموجبة الجزئية : الأوَّل : الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلّيّة مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية، تقولنا : كلُّ (ب) (ج) ، وكلُّ (ب) (أ)، فبعض (ج) (أ)) .
Section: On Syllogism. That is: the universal affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major (particular affirmative) intended to produce. And in the expression there is looseness, because his saying 'or by conversion' is understood from it that the two affirmative majors together with the universal affirmative minor, then there result two moods: the first: universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major. And the second: universal affirmative minor with particular affirmative major. But the first mood is included in his saying: 'the two affirmatives with the universal affirmative produce', so it is determined that only the second mood is intended; that is: universal affirmative minor with particular affirmative major, according to what we have explained. And it is not hidden that his saying 'or by conversion' is understood from it the two moods, so applying [the term] and intending one mood is looseness. So what is understood from his saying: 'the two affirmatives with the universal affirmative produce, or by conversion' are three moods producing the particular affirmative: the first: universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major, we say: every B is C, and every B is A, therefore some C is A.
Source pages p. 819

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on a commentary of Tadhheeb al-Mantiq. It addresses an ambiguity in the original matn's phrasing 'or by conversion' when listing the conditions for producing a particular affirmative conclusion. The author points out that the phrase could be interpreted as allowing two moods: one with a universal affirmative major, and another with a particular affirmative major. However, the first of these moods is already covered by the earlier phrase 'the two affirmatives with the universal affirmative', leading to redundancy. Therefore, the intended meaning of 'or by conversion' must be only the second mood, i.e., universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major. This is a 'looseness' (tasamuh) in expression, where a general term is used to refer to a specific instance. After clarifying this, the author lists the three moods that actually produce a particular affirmative: the first (universal/universal), and then by implication the second (universal/particular) and possibly a third? Actually, he says 'three moods' but only describes the first explicitly. The other two are likely derived from the same patterns. The commentator then gives an example with variables: كل ب ج (every B is C) and كل ب أ (every B is A) yield بعض ج أ (some C is A). This example illustrates the first mood. The discussion shows the precision required in logical expressions and the need to avoid redundancy.

Three Productive Moods of the First Figure Affirmative
Author enumerates three moods of the first figure that yield a particular affirmative conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three moods of the first figure that produce a particular affirmative conclusion and explain why the conclusion is particular even when both premises are universal.

Core Idea
In the first figure, even two universal affirmative premises yield a particular conclusion if the major term is more specific.

This passage from the Tadhheeb al-Mantiq enumerates three moods of the first figure that produce a particular affirmative conclusion. By studying these moods, you will see how the quantity of the conclusion depends on the relationship between terms, not just the quantity of the premises. The commentary by al-Dasūqī and Shaykh Yāsīn further clarifies the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three mood types
Locate the three combinations of premises: universal+universal, particular+universal, and universal+particular. Note that all have an affirmative minor and major.
The fourth mood (not covered) involves a negative premise.
2
Extract the abstract form
For each mood, write the form using the variables ب (middle), ج (minor term), أ (major term). Example for mood 1: كل ب ج, كل ب أ, therefore بعض ج أ.
The middle term is the subject of both premises.
3
Instantiate with Islamic examples
Use al-Dasūqī's examples: mood 1: every animal is body, every animal is growing, so some body is growing. Mood 2: some human is animal, every human is rational, so some animal is rational. Mood 3: every human is animal, some human is rational, so some animal is rational.
4
Understand why the conclusion is particular
Even in mood 1 (both universal), the conclusion is particular because the major term may be more specific than the middle term. If it were universal, the more specific would be predicated of all individuals of the more general, which is not warranted.
Shaykh Yāsīn generalizes: the conclusion is always particular in these moods due to possible generality of the minor term.
5
Apply to a negative example
Consider a negative version (mood 4): every animal is body, no animal is stone, so some body is not stone. The same principle applies: the conclusion is particular because the major may be more specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمفهوم من قوله: لينتج الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية، أو بالعكس ثلاثة أضرب منتجة للموجبة الجزئية:
الأول: الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية، تقولنا: كل (ب) (ج)، وكل (ب) (أ)، فبعض (ج) (أ).
الثاني: الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية، كقولنا: «بعض (ب) (ج)، وكل (ب) (أ)، فبعض (ج) (أ)».
الثالث: الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية، كقولنا: كل (ب) (ج)، وبعض (ب) (أ)، فبعض (ج) (أ).
الدسوقي: (قوله: كل ب ج) أي: كل حيوان جسم. (وقوله: وكل ب أ) أي: كل حيوان نام. (قوله: فبعض ج أ) أي: بعض الجسم نام.
(قوله: بعض ب ج) أي: بعض الإنسان حيوان، (وكل ب أ)؛ أي: كل إنسان ناطق. (قوله: فبعض ج أ) أي: فبعض الحيوان ناطق.
(قوله: كل ب ج) أي: كل إنسان حيوان. (قوله: وبعض ب أ) أي: بعض الإنسان ناطق. (قوله: فبعض ج أ) أي: بعض الحيوان ناطق. وإنما أنتج للضرب الأول جزئيا كالضرب الثاني والضرب الثالث؛ لجواز أن يكون محمول الكبرى أخص من محمول الصغرى، وحينئذ لو أنتج كليا للزم حمل الأخص على جميع أفراد الأعم.
وعبارة الشيخ ياسين: فالنتيجة في جميع ضروب هذا الشكل جزئية، وذلك لجواز أعمية الأصغر، فيمتنع الحكم بالأكبر على كل أفراد الأصغر إيجاباً أو سلباً.
ومثال السلب من الضرب الرابع: كل حيوان جسم، ولا شيء من الحيوان بحجر، فبعض الجسم ليس بحجر.
So what is understood from his statement: 'Let the two affirmative (premises) produce with the universal affirmative (major), or vice versa' are three moods productive of the particular affirmative:
First: the universal affirmative minor with the universal affirmative major, our saying: Every B is C, and every B is A, therefore some C is A.
Second: the particular affirmative minor with the universal affirmative major, our saying: 'Some B is C, and every B is A, therefore some C is A'.
Third: the universal affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major, our saying: Every B is C, and some B is A, therefore some C is A.
Al-Dasūqī: (His saying: Every B is C) i.e., every animal is a body. (And his saying: And every B is A) i.e., every animal is growing. (His saying: therefore some C is A) i.e., some body is growing.
(His saying: Some B is C) i.e., some human is animal, (and every B is A) i.e., every human is rational. (His saying: therefore some C is A) i.e., some animal is rational.
(His saying: Every B is C) i.e., every human is animal. (His saying: And some B is A) i.e., some human is rational. (His saying: therefore some C is A) i.e., some animal is rational. And it produced for the first mood a particular (conclusion) just like the second and third moods because the predicate of the major premise may be more specific than the predicate of the minor premise, and if it produced a universal, then the more specific would be predicated of all individuals of the more general.
And the expression of Shaykh Yāsīn: The conclusion in all moods of this figure is particular, because the minor term may be more general, and thus it is impossible to judge the major term about all individuals of the minor, whether affirmatively or negatively.
And an example of negative from the fourth mood: Every animal is a body, and no animal is a stone, therefore some body is not stone.
Source pages p. 820

Commentary
This passage explains the three productive moods of the first figure that yield a particular affirmative conclusion. The author first states that from the expression 'let the two affirmative (premises) produce with the universal affirmative major, or vice versa' we derive three moods. The first mood has both premises universal affirmative. The second has a particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major. The third has a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major. Each mood is given with the logical variables (ب، ج، أ) and then interpreted by al-Dasūqī with Islamic science examples: for the first mood, 'Every animal is a body, every animal is growing, therefore some body is growing'; for the second, 'Some human is animal, every human is rational, therefore some animal is rational'; for the third, 'Every human is animal, some human is rational, therefore some animal is rational.' The commentary then explains why the conclusion is particular even in the first mood where both premises are universal: because the major term may be more specific than the middle term (or minor predicate), so we cannot conclude that the major applies to all individuals of the minor predicate. Shaykh Yāsīn's quote generalizes: the conclusion is particular in all moods of this figure because the minor term may be more general. An example of a negative conclusion from the fourth mood (which is not discussed in detail) is given: 'Every animal is a body, and no animal is a stone, therefore some body is not stone.' This illustrates that the same reasoning applies to negative propositions.

Key Terms
الضرب
الموجبة الجزئية
النتيجة الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first figure, if the minor premise is 'Every human is animal' and the major premise is 'Every human is rational', what is the conclusion?
A) Every animal is rational
B) Some animal is rational
C) Every human is rational
D) Some human is animal

Key Takeaway
When the minor term is more general than the middle term, even two universal premises yield a particular conclusion in the first figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the abstract form and a concrete Islamic example for mood 2 (particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major) using the patterns from the lesson.
?
Explain why the conclusion in mood 3 (universal affirmative minor + particular affirmative major) cannot be universal, referencing the principle that the major term may be more specific.

Affirmative Moods of the Second Figure
Author enumerates five productive moods of the second figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the two valid affirmative moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism and explain why the conclusion must be particular affirmative.

Core Idea
In the second figure, affirmative premises only yield particular conclusions.

In the previous section, we learned about the general rules of the second figure. Now we turn to the specific moods that produce valid conclusions. The second figure is defined by the middle term being the predicate of both premises? Actually, in the Islamic tradition, the second figure has the middle term as subject in both premises, as seen in the examples. When both premises are affirmative, the conclusion must be particular affirmative to avoid the fallacy of illicit major or a false universal. This lesson will walk you through the two such moods with concrete examples from Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood components
Look at the quantity and quality of each premise. In the second figure affirmative moods, both premises are affirmative, but one is universal and one is particular.
2
Extract the terms
Identify the middle term (B), the minor term (C), and the major term (A) from the example. In 'Some B is C, every B is A', B is the middle, C is the minor, A is the major.
3
Check the conclusion
The conclusion is particular affirmative (some C is A). Verify that the conclusion follows the rule that the predicate of the conclusion is the major term.
4
Understand why particular
Recall Dasuqi's point: because the major term may be more specific than the minor term, a universal conclusion would be invalid. For example, 'every animal is a body, every animal is growing' leads only to 'some body is growing', not 'every body is growing'.
5
Generate new examples
Construct a new example using Islamic concepts, such as 'some human is animal' and 'every human is rational' to conclude 'some animal is rational'.

الثَّاني : الصُّغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلّية، كَقولنا : «بعض (ب) (ج) ، وكلّ (ب) (أ)، فبعض (ج) (أ)). الثَّالث : الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلّيّة مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية، كَقولنا : كلُّ (ب) (ج) ، وبعض (ب) (أ)، فبعض (ج) (أ).

[الدسوقي] (قَوْلُهُ : بَعْضُ ب (ج) أي : بعضُ الإنسان حيوان، (وكلُّ ب أ)؛ أي: كلُّ إنسانٍ ناطق. قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج (أ) أي : فبعض الحيوانِ ناطق. قَوْلُهُ : كُلُّ ب ج ) أي : كلُّ إنسان حيوان. قَوْلُهُ : وَبَعْضُ ب أ) أي : بعضُ الإنسانِ ناطق. قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج (أ) أي : بعضُ الحيوانِ ناطق، وإنَّما أنتج لضرب الأَوَّلِ جزئيا كالشَّربِ الثَّاني والضَّرب الثَّالث ؛ لجواز أن يكونَ مَحمولُ الكُبرى أخصَّ مِنْ محمول الصُّغرى، وحينئذ لو أنتج كلِّيَّا لَلَزِمَ حملُ الأخص على جميع أفراد الأعم، كَقولنا في الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ : كلَّ حيوان جسم، وكلَّ حيوان نامٍ، فَبعضُ الجسمِ نامٍ . وَلَوْ قِيلَ:  ُكلَّ جسم نام ؛ لَكانَ باطلاً ؛ لأنَّ الجسم أعمُّ مِنْ نامٍ.

أَوْ مَعَ السَّالِبَةِ) عطف على قولِهِ : مع الموجبة»؛ أي: لتنتج الصُّغريانِ الموجبتانِ مع الكبرى السَّالبة (الكُلِّيَّةِ، أَوْ) تنتج الصُّغرى الموجبة (الكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ) الكبرى السَّالبة (الجُزْئِيَّةِ سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ)، فهذه ثلاثة أضرب منتجة للسَّالبة الجزئية :

الأول : الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلّيّة مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية، كقولنا : كلَّ (ب) (ج) ، ولا شيء من (ب) (أ)، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)).

الثَّاني : الصُّغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية، كقولنا : بعض (ب) (ج) ، ولا شيء من (ب) (أ) ، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)).

[الدسوقي] (قَوْلُهُ : كُلُّ ب (ج) أَيْ : كلُّ إنسانٍ حيوانٌ . قَوْلُهُ : وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنْ ب أ) أي : مِنَ الإنسان بحجرٍ . (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج لَيْسَ أ) أي : بعض الحيوان ليس بحجرٍ. قَوْلُهُ : بَعْضُ ب (ج) أي : بعضُ الإنسان حيوان. قَوْلُهُ : وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنْ ب أ) أي: من الإنسان بحجرٍ.)
Section on the syllogism. The second: the particular affirmative minor with the universal affirmative major, as we say: 'Some B is C, and every B is A, so some C is A.' The third: the universal affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major, as we say: 'Every B is C, and some B is A, so some C is A.' [Dasuqi]: His statement: 'Some B is C' meaning: some human is animal, and 'every B is A' meaning: every human is rational. His statement: 'so some C is A' meaning: so some animal is rational. His statement: 'every B is C' meaning: every human is animal. His statement: 'and some B is A' meaning: some human is rational. His statement: 'so some C is A' meaning: so some animal is rational. Indeed, it produces for the first mood a particular [conclusion] like the second and third moods; because it is possible that the predicate of the major is more specific than the predicate of the minor, and then if it produced a universal, it would entail predicating the more specific of all individuals of the more general, as we say in the first mood: 'Every animal is a body, and every animal is growing, so some body is growing.' And if it were said: 'Every body is growing', it would be false because body is more general than growing. Or with the negative: [the phrase 'or with the negative'] is coordinated with his statement 'with the affirmative'; i.e., for the two affirmative minors to produce with the universal negative major, or the universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major, a particular negative [conclusion]. So these are three productive moods for the particular negative: The first: the universal affirmative minor with the universal negative major, as we say: 'Every B is C, and no B is A, so some C is not A.' The second: the particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major, as we say: 'Some B is C, and no B is A, so some C is not A.' [Dasuqi]: His statement: 'Every B is C' meaning: every human is animal. His statement: 'and no B is A' meaning: no human is stone. His statement: 'so some C is not A' meaning: some animal is not stone. His statement: 'some B is C' meaning: some human is animal. His statement: 'and no B is A' meaning: no human is stone.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the productive moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first presents two moods that produce a particular affirmative conclusion: the second mood with a particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, and the third mood with a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major. The commentary by Dasuqi provides concrete examples using the terms 'human' (ب), 'animal' (ج), and 'rational' (أ). He explains why the conclusion is particular: because the major term (predicate of the major premise) may be more specific than the minor term (predicate of the minor premise). If a universal conclusion were drawn, it would incorrectly predicate the more specific term of all instances of the more general term, as illustrated with 'every animal is a body' and 'every animal is growing' leading to 'some body is growing', not 'every body is growing'. Then the author turns to moods with a negative major premise. There are three such moods: the first with universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, the second with particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, and the third with universal affirmative minor and particular negative major (though only two are explicitly listed in the text, the third is implied by the structure). Dasuqi again gives examples using 'human', 'animal', and 'stone'. The key takeaway is that all moods of the second figure yield a particular conclusion, whether affirmative or negative, due to the logical restriction that the middle term is predicate in both premises.

Negative Moods of the Second Figure
Author enumerates five productive moods of the second figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the three valid negative moods of the second figure and explain why the conclusion is particular negative.

Core Idea
In the second figure, a negative major premise forces the conclusion to be particular negative.

When the major premise is negative, the second figure produces three valid moods that yield a particular negative conclusion. The text enumerates two explicitly—the first and second negative moods—and implies a third. These moods demonstrate how a negative major combined with an affirmative minor (universal or particular) leads to a valid particular negative conclusion. This lesson will walk you through each mood with Dasuqi's examples using 'human', 'animal', and 'stone'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative moods
There are three moods: (1) minor universal affirmative with major universal negative, (2) minor particular affirmative with major universal negative, (3) minor universal affirmative with major particular negative. The text explicitly lists the first two; the third is implied by the structure.
2
Check the conclusion pattern
In each mood, the conclusion is particular negative (some C is not A). Verify that the conclusion is negative because the major premise is negative, and particular because at least one premise is particular or to avoid a fallacy.
3
Understand why particular negative
The conclusion must be negative due to the negative major. It is particular because a universal negative would require both premises to be universal, which is not the case (the minor is affirmative and sometimes particular). Also, Dasuqi's example shows 'some animal is not stone', not 'no animal is stone'.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
For the first negative mood: 'Every human is animal' (minor), 'No human is stone' (major) → 'Some animal is not stone'. For the second: 'Some human is animal' and 'No human is stone' → 'Some animal is not stone'.
5
Distinguish the three moods
Note that the minor premise varies: universal affirmative in the first and third negative moods, particular affirmative in the second. The major is universal negative in the first two, and particular negative in the third. The conclusion is always particular negative.

Three Moods of Third Figure for Particular Negative
Author enumerates three moods of the third figure that yield a particular negative conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three moods of the third figure that yield a particular negative conclusion and construct valid syllogisms for each.

Core Idea
The third figure yields a particular negative conclusion from three specific premise combinations.

In the third figure, certain combinations of premises always produce a particular negative conclusion. This lesson walks through the three valid moods, showing how to test them with concrete examples from Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Review the third figure structure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises. Recognize that the conclusion always has the minor term as subject and major term as predicate.
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion; the major term is the predicate.
2
Identify the general rule
The author states that either both minors are affirmative with a universal negative major, or a universal affirmative minor with a particular negative major yields a particular negative. Note the two patterns.
Pattern 1: both minors affirmative + universal negative major; Pattern 2: universal affirmative minor + particular negative major.
3
List the three moods
The passage explicitly enumerates three moods: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major; (2) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major; (3) universal affirmative minor + particular negative major.
All three premises combinations are valid and produce a particular negative conclusion.
4
Examine the concrete example
For the first mood, the author substitutes B=human, J=animal, A=stone. Confirm that the premises hold and the conclusion 'some animal is not a stone' follows.
The same substitution works for the other moods by adjusting the minor premise accordingly.
5
Verify the pattern
For each mood, check that the premises match the pattern and the conclusion is a particular negative. Note that the third figure always yields a particular conclusion in these valid moods.
The validity relies on the position of the middle term and the quantity/quality of the premises.

أي: لينتج الصُّغريانِ الموجبتانِ مع الكبرى السَّالبة الكُلِّيَّةِ، أَوْ تنتج الصُّغرى الموجبة الكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ الكبرى السَّالبة الجُزْئِيَّةِ سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ)، فهذه ثلاثة أضرب منتجة للسَّالبة الجزئية : الأول : الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلّيّة مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية، كقولنا : كلَّ (ب) (ج) ، ولا شيء من (ب) (أ)، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ). (قوله: كل ب ج أي: كل إنسان حيوان. قوله: ولا شيء من ب أ أي: من الإنسان بحجر. قوله: فبعض ج ليس أ أي: بعض الحيوان ليس بحجر.) الثَّاني : الصُّغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية، كقولنا : بعض (ب) (ج) ، ولا شيء من (ب) (أ) ، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)). (قوله: بعض ب (ج) أي: بعض الإنسان حيوان. قوله: ولا شيء من ب أ أي: من الإنسان بحجر.) فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الثَّالث : الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلِّيَّة مع الكبرى السالبة الجزئية، كقولنا : كلُّ (ب) (ج)، وبعض (ب) ليس (أ)، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)).
Meaning: The two affirmative minors with the universal negative major produce, or the universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major produces a particular negative. So these are three moods producing a particular negative: First: The universal affirmative minor with the universal negative major, as we say: Every B is J, and no B is A, so some J is not A. (His statement: 'Every B is J' meaning every human is an animal. His statement: 'No B is A' meaning no human is a stone. His statement: 'So some J is not A' meaning some animal is not a stone.) Second: The particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major, as we say: Some B is J, and no B is A, so some J is not A. (His statement: 'Some B is J' meaning some human is an animal. His statement: 'No B is A' meaning no human is a stone.) Section: On Syllogism. Third: The universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major, as we say: Every B is J, and some B is not A, so some J is not A.
Source pages p. 822

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three moods of the third figure that produce a particular negative conclusion. The author first states the general rule: either both minors are affirmative with a universal negative major, or the universal affirmative minor with a particular negative major yields a particular negative. Then he lists three specific combinations. For each, he provides an example using variables (B, J, A) and a concrete interpretation: B stands for human, J for animal, A for stone. The first mood: universal affirmative minor (every human is an animal) + universal negative major (no human is a stone) → particular negative (some animal is not a stone). The second mood: particular affirmative minor (some human is an animal) + universal negative major → same conclusion. The third mood: universal affirmative minor + particular negative major (some human is not a stone) → particular negative. The section heading 'Section: On Syllogism' marks the beginning of a new discussion in the book, but here it serves to introduce the third mood, which completes the enumeration. The examples are drawn from general categories: human, animal, stone, which are typically used in logical exercises within the Islamic tradition.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
The Six Productive Moods of the Third Figure and Reductio
Author explains the six productive moods and reductio proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions of the third figure (affirmative minor and one universal premise) to identify valid moods and explain why each mood must be proven by reductio.

Core Idea
Only six of sixteen premise combinations in the third figure are valid, and their proofs rely on reducing them to the first figure via reductio ad absurdum.

This passage from al-Dasuqi and al-Attar breaks down how the third figure's 16 possible premise pairs are reduced to just 6 productive moods by two simple conditions: the minor must be affirmative and at least one premise must be universal. The commentators then explain that these six moods are not directly provable through normal conversion; instead, they must be proven by reductio (al-khalf), where the contradictory of the conclusion is assumed and shown to lead to a contradiction with the original premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The third figure requires (1) the minor premise to be affirmative, and (2) at least one premise to be universal. These conditions eliminate invalid moods.
The conditions are stated clearly in the text: 'اشتراط إيجاب الصغرى، وكلّيَّة إحدى المقدمتين'.
2
Calculate the elimination
Out of 16 possible combinations, the affirmative minor condition removes 8 (since the minor can be negative in 2 ways × 4 majors). The universality condition removes 2 more (particular minor with particular major, both affirmative and negative). Total removed = 10, leaving 6 valid moods.
Al-Dasuqi and al-Attar both provide this calculation; verify the numbers.
3
Understand why the conclusion is particular
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. This forces the conclusion to be particular, even if both premises are universal.
The passage notes that the third and fourth figures always yield particular conclusions.
4
Recognize the need for reductio
Because the conclusion is particular, its contradictory is universal. This universal can serve as the major premise of a first-figure syllogism, with the original minor (always affirmative) as the minor. The reductio proof shows that assuming the negation of the conclusion leads to a contradiction.
The text calls this 'السر' (the secret).
5
Trace the example given
The commentators provide an example: 'Some human is animal; every human is rational; therefore some human is rational.' They then show that if the conclusion were false, its contradictory ('No animal is rational') would lead to a contradiction with the original premises.
Note: The example is from the second mood; the passage says it is explained by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدسوقي لأنَّ الشَّكلَ الثَّالثَ، وكذا الرابع؛ ينتجان جزئية، وإن لم تكن هناك جزئية. ويمكنُ أنْ يُجابَ عنه بأنَّ قولَهُ وتتبعُ النَّتيجةَ الأخس؛ أي : إِنْ كانَ هناكَ : خشة، وأمَّا إذا لم يكن هناك خسَّة؛ فَتارةً تكون النتيجةُ مُشتملةً على خيَّةِ الجزئية كما في الشَّكلِ الرَّابِعِ والثَّالث، وتارة لا؛ كَمَا فِي الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ والثَّاني. وَحاصله : أنَّ المفهوم فيه تفصيل، وإذا كان فيه تفصيل؛ لا يعترض عليه . (قَوْلُهُ : الْمُوْجِبَتَانِ) أي: الكلِّيَّة، والجزئيَّة . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ تَنْتُجُ الصُّغْرَى الْمُوْجِبَةُ أَشارَ بهذا التَّقدير إلى أنَّ قول المصنف : (أو الكلِّيَّة)؛ معطوفٌ على قولِهِ : الموجبتان في قوله : لتنتج الموجبتان. (قَوْلُهُ : سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ) معمول (تنتج). فَضُرُوْبُ الشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ بحسبِ الواقعِ سنة، والقياسُ يقتضي ستَّةَ عَشَرَ، لكنَّ اشتراط إيجاب الصُّغرى، وكلّيَّة إحدى المقدمتين، أسقط ما عدا الستة . ثمَّ الصُّروبُ السِّتَّةُ، إِنَّما تَنتُجُ بِالخُلْفِ، (الدسوقي) (قَوْلُهُ : فَضُرُوْبُ الشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ) أي : الضُّرُوبُ المنتجة (قَوْلُهُ : أَسْقَطَ مَا عَدَا السَّتَّةَ وجهُهُ : أنَّ إيجابَ الصُّغرى يخرج به سلبها كلّيَّةً أو جزئية، وهما ما عدا الأربع الكبرياتِ ثمانية، وكلّية إحداهما يخرج به جزئية الصغرى الموجبة مع جزئيَّةِ الكُبرى موجبة أو سالبة، فهذه عشرة، وأما سالبة الصُّغرى؛ فقد خرجت فيما قبلَهُ، فتأمله . (قَوْلُهُ: إِنَّمَا تَنْتُجُ بِالْخُلْفِ... إلخ) اعلَمُ أنَّ الخلف جارٍ في جميعِ الضُّروب، (العطار) (قَوْلُهُ : أَسْقَطَ مَا عَدَا السَّتَّة وجه الإسقاط أنَّ الشَّرطَ الأَوَّلَ، وهو إيجاب الصُّغرى، أسقطَ الصُّغرى السَّالبةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ والصُّغرى السالبة الجزئية مع الكبرياتِ الأربع، فهذه ثمانية أضرب حاصلةٌ من ضرب الاثنين في الأربع، والشَّرطُ الثَّاني، كُلِّيَّةُ إحدى المقدِّمتين، أسقط الصُّغرى الموجبة الجزئية معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الجزئيَّةِ والسَّالبة الجزئيَّةِ؛ فسقطَتِ الصُّروبُ العشرة، وبقيّتِ المنتجةُ سِيَّةٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : بِالْخُلْفِ) متعلّق بقولِهِ : لينتج، وهو جارٍ في الضُّروبِ كُلِّهَا . (قَوْلُهُ : الخلف جارٍ ... إلخ السِّرُّ في ذلك أن نتيجة هذا الشكل دائماً جزئية فنقيضها كلية، وهي تصلح أن تكون كبرى الشكل الأوّل وصغراه دائماً موجبة وهي تصلح أن تكون صغرى الشكل الأوَّل وبضم نقيض النتيجة لها يتركب قياس من الشكل الأوّل ينتج ما ينافي كبرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والشارح ينبه في الضرب الأوّل ونحن نبينه بالمادة في الضرب الثاني لنقيس عليه الباقي فنقول : بعض الإنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق، ينتج : بعض الإنسان ناطق لو لم يصدق هذا لصَدَقَ نقيضه وهو : لا شيء من الحيوان بناطق، ثم نضمّه
Al-Dasuqi: Because the third figure, and likewise the fourth, produce a particular [conclusion] even if there is no particular [premise]. And it can be answered that his statement 'and the conclusion follows the inferior' means: if there is inferiority, then sometimes the conclusion contains particularity as in the fourth and third figures, and sometimes not as in the first and second. The upshot is that the concept has detail, and if there is detail, it is not objected to. (His statement: 'the two affirmatives') meaning: the universal and the particular. (His statement: 'or the affirmative minor produces...') He indicated by this estimation that the author's statement '(or the universal)' is coordinated with his statement 'the two affirmatives' in his statement 'so that the two affirmatives produce'. (His statement: 'particular negative') is the object of 'produces'. The moods of the third figure in reality are six, and the syllogism would require sixteen, but the condition of affirming the minor and universality of one of the two premises eliminated what is other than six. Then the six moods only produce by reductio. (Al-Dasuqi) (His statement: 'the moods of the third figure') meaning: the productive moods. (His statement: 'eliminated what is other than six' its justification: that affirming the minor excludes its negation, universal or particular, and those along with the four majors are eight, and the universality of one of them excludes the particular affirmative minor with the particular affirmative or negative major, so these are ten, and as for the negative minor, it was excluded previously; so reflect. (His statement: 'only produce by reductio...') Know that reductio is current in all moods. (Al-Attar) (His statement: 'eliminated what is other than six' the justification of the elimination: that the first condition, which is affirming the minor, eliminates the universal negative minor and the particular negative minor with the four majors, so these are eight moods resulting from multiplying two by four; and the second condition, universality of one of the two premises, eliminates the particular affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major and the particular negative major; so ten moods are eliminated, and the remaining productive ones are six. (His statement: 'by reductio') is attached to his statement 'produces', and it is current in all moods. (His statement: 'reductio is current...') The secret is that the conclusion of this figure is always particular, so its contradictory is universal, and it is suitable to be the major of the first figure; and its minor is always affirmative and suitable to be the minor of the first figure; and by adding the contradictory of the conclusion to it, a syllogism from the first figure is composed that produces what contradicts the major of the original assumed truth. The commentator alerts in the first mood, and we explain it with material in the second mood to analogize the rest: we say: Some human is an animal, and every human is rational, produces: Some human is rational. If this were not true, its contradictory would be true: No animal is rational. Then we combine it...
Source pages p. 822

Commentary
This passage discusses the number of valid moods in the third figure and the method of proof by reductio. Al-Dasuqi first addresses an objection: the third and fourth figures always yield particular conclusions even if no particular premise is present. The answer explains that 'the conclusion follows the inferior' means details apply; when there is 'inferiority' (i.e., particularity) it appears in conclusions of the third and fourth figures but not necessarily in the first and second. Then he glosses the author's terms. The main text states that there are six valid moods in the third figure out of sixteen possible combinations because two conditions eliminate the rest: the minor must be affirmative, and one premise must be universal. Al-Dasuqi calculates: the condition of affirmative minor removes eight possibilities (the two negative minors with four majors each), and the universality condition removes two more (particular minor with particular major, both affirmative and negative), totalling ten removed, leaving six. Al-Attar similarly explains, emphasizing that negative minors are already excluded by the first condition. The final part explains that these six moods are proven by reductio (al-khalf). The 'secret' is that the third figure's conclusion is always particular, so its contradictory (universal) can serve as the major premise of a first-figure syllogism. The minor of the third figure is always affirmative, so it fits as the minor of the first figure. Combining the contradictory of the conclusion with the original minor produces a first-figure syllogism that contradicts the original major, thereby proving the original conclusion. An example is given: 'Some human is an animal' (minor), 'Every human is rational' (major) yields 'Some human is rational'. If that were false, then 'No animal is rational' would be true, but combining that with 'Every human is rational' leads to a contradiction. The commentary cuts off mid-explanation.
