The Six Productive Moods of the Third Figure
Author lists conditions for six productive moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the conditions that reduce the third figure moods from sixteen to six and identify a valid third figure mood from its premises.

Core Idea
Only six of sixteen possible third figure moods are productive because the minor must be affirmative and one premise universal.

The third figure has a distinctive structure: the middle term is subject in both premises. This imposes constraints on which premise combinations yield valid conclusions. In this lesson, you will learn the two conditions that produce exactly six productive moods, starting from an example mood.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the third figure structure
The middle term is subject in both premises. The conclusion is always particular.
2
Analyze the example mood
The author gives a concrete mood: universal affirmative minor, particular negative major yields particular negative conclusion. Note the premises are both about B.
3
Count possible premise combinations
With four types (A, E, I, O) for each premise, there are 16. But not all are valid in the third figure.
4
Apply the two conditions
Minor must be affirmative (so no E or O minor) and at least one premise must be universal. This eliminates 10 combinations, leaving 6.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الثَّالث : الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلِّيَّة مع الكبرى السالبة الجزئية، كقولنا : كلُّ (ب) (ج)، وبعض (ب) ليس (أ)، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)) . فضروبُ الشَّكلِ الثَّالِثِ بحسبِ الواقعِ سنة، والقياسُ يقتضي ستَّةَ عَشَرَ، لكنَّ اشتراط إيجاب الصُّغرى، وكلّيَّة إحدى المقدمتين، أسقط ما عدا الستة . ثمَّ الصُّروبُ السِّتَّةُ، إِنَّما تَنتُجُ بِالخُلْفِ، أَوْ عَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى، أَوْ عَكْسِ التَّرْتِيبِ ، ثُمَّ عكس النَّتيجةِ.
Chapter: On the third figure. The minor affirmative universal with the major negative particular, like our saying: Every B is C, and some B is not A, so some C is not A. The moods of the third figure in reality are six, and the syllogism requires sixteen, but the condition of the affirmative minor and the universality of one of the two premises eliminates what is other than the six. Then the six moods only produce by reductio ad absurdum, or by conversion of the minor, or by conversion of the order then conversion of the conclusion.
Source pages p. 823

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by providing an example of the third figure: 'Every B is C, and some B is not A, so some C is not A.' This mood is one of the six productive ones. The author then states that in reality there are six productive moods in the third figure, although the number of possible moods based on the four premises (combinations of quantity and quality) is sixteen. The reduction to six is due to two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative, and at least one of the two premises must be universal. These conditions eliminate ten out of sixteen, leaving six moods. The passage then teaches that these six moods produce their conclusions not directly but through three techniques: reductio ad absurdum (khulf), conversion of the minor premise, or conversion of the order (i.e., swapping the premises) followed by conversion of the conclusion. The third figure has a middle term that is subject in both premises, so the conclusion is always particular. The methods are needed to convert the indirect reasoning into a direct first-figure syllogism.

الضروب المنتجة
إيجاب الصغرى
كلية إحدى المقدمتين

The Syllogism

The Three Methods of Proof in the Third Figure
Author lists three methods of proof for third figure moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three methods of proving third figure moods: reductio ad absurdum, conversion of the minor, and conversion of order followed by conversion of the conclusion.

Core Idea
Each third figure mood is proven indirectly through one of three methods.

The six productive moods of the third figure do not yield their conclusions directly. Instead, they require a proof technique to convert the reasoning into a first figure syllogism. The author lists three methods: khulf, aks al-sughra, and aks al-tartib followed by aks al-natija.

Guided Path
1
Understand why proofs are needed
Third figure moods are not self-evident; they need to be reduced to the first figure to be accepted as valid.
2
Learn method 1: reductio ad absurdum (khulf)
Assume the negation of the conclusion, combine with one premise to derive a contradiction, thus proving the original conclusion.
3
Learn method 2: conversion of the minor (aks al-sughra)
Convert the minor premise (e.g., from A to I) to create a first figure mood that directly yields the conclusion.
4
Learn method 3: conversion of order then conversion of conclusion (aks al-tartib then aks al-natija)
Swap the premises to form a different figure, then convert the new conclusion to get the original conclusion.
5
Memorize the three methods
These are the standard techniques for third figure proofs. Practice applying each to the six moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The author then states that in reality there are six productive moods in the third figure, although the number of possible moods based on the four premises (combinations of quantity and quality) is sixteen. The reduction to six is due to two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative, and at least one of the two premises must be universal. These conditions eliminate ten out of sixteen, leaving six moods. The passage then teaches that these six moods produce their conclusions not directly but through three techniques: reductio ad absurdum (khulf), conversion of the minor premise, or conversion of the order (i.e., swapping the premises) followed by conversion of the conclusion. The third figure has a middle term that is subject in both premises, so the conclusion is always particular. The methods are needed to convert the indirect reasoning into a direct first-figure syllogism.

Key Terms
الخلف
عكس الصغرى
عكس الترتيب
عكس النتيجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three methods of proof for the third figure mentioned in the passage?
A) Khulf
B) Aks al-sughra
C) Aks al-tartib alone
D) Aks al-tartib then aks al-natija

Key Takeaway
Third figure moods are proven by reductio, conversion of minor, or conversion of order followed by conversion of conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a third figure mood 'Every B is C, some B is not A, therefore some C is not A', outline how you would prove it using conversion of the minor.

The Syllogism

Elimination of Moods in the Third Figure
Commentators explain which moods are eliminated and why.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions (affirmative minor, universal premise) to determine which moods of the third figure are productive.

Core Idea
Only six out of sixteen possible moods survive the elimination conditions.

The third figure of categorical syllogism initially seems to have many possible combinations of premises. However, by applying two simple conditions — the minor must be affirmative and one premise must be universal — the number of productive moods reduces to just six. This lesson will guide you through that elimination process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions
The commentary states two conditions: the minor must be affirmative, and one premise must be universal. Note these.
2
Count eliminated moods from condition one
With 4 major types (A, E, I, O) and 2 negative minor types (universal and particular), condition 1 eliminates 4×2=8 moods.
3
Apply condition two
After condition one, condition two eliminates moods where both premises are particular: the mood with minor particular affirmative and major particular affirmative, and the mood with minor particular affirmative and major particular negative. That's 2 more.
4
Verify remaining are six
16 initial moods minus 8 minus 2 equals 6 moods. These are the productive moods of the third figure.

الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَضُرُوْبُ الشَّكْلِ الثَّالِثِ) أي : الضُّروبُ المنتجة (قَوْلُهُ : أَسْقَطَ مَا عَدَا السَّتَّةَ وجهُهُ : أنَّ إيجابَ الصُّغرى يخرج به سلبها كلّيَّةً أو جزئية، وهما ما عدا الأربع الكبرياتِ ثمانية، وكلّية إحداهما يخرج به جزئية الصغرى الموجبة مع جزئيَّةِ الكُبرى موجبة أو سالبة، فهذه عشرة، وأما سالبة الصُّغرى ؛ فقد خرجت فيما قبلَهُ، فتأمله . (قَوْلُهُ: إِنَّمَا تَنْتُجُ بِالْخُلْفِ... إلخ) اعلَمُ أنَّ الخلف(۱) جارٍ في جميعِ الضُّروب، العطار (قَوْلُهُ : أَسْقَطَ مَا عَدَا السَّتَّة وجه الإسقاط أنَّ الشَّرطَ الأَوَّلَ، وهو إيجاب الصُّغرى، أسقطَ الصُّغرى السَّالبةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ والصُّغرى السالبة الجزئية مع الكبرياتِ الأربع، فهذه ثمانية أضرب حاصلةٌ من ضرب الاثنين في الأربع، والشَّرطُ الثَّاني، كُلِّيَّةُ إحدى المقدِّمتين، أسقط الصُّغرى الموجبة الجزئية معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الجزئيَّةِ والسَّالبة الجزئيَّةِ ؛ فسقطَتِ الصُّروبُ العشرة، وبقيّتِ المنتجةُ سِيَّةٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : بِالْخُلْفِ) متعلّق بقولِهِ : لينتج، وهو جارٍ في الضُّروبِ كُلِّهَا . (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : الخلف جارٍ ... إلخ السِّرُّ في ذلك أن نتيجة هذا الشكل دائماً جزئية فنقيضها كلية، وهي تصلح أن تكون كبرى الشكل الأوّل وصغراه دائماً موجبة وهي تصلح أن تكون صغرى الشكل الأوَّل وبضم نقيض النتيجة لها يتركب قياس من الشكل الأوّل ينتج ما ينافي كبرى الأصل المفروضة الصدق والشارح ينبه في الضرب الأوّل ونحن نبينه بالمادة في الضرب الثاني لنقيس عليه الباقي فنقول : بعض الإنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق، ينتج : بعض الإنسان ناطق لو لم يصدق هذا لصَدَقَ نقيضه وهو : لا شيء من الحيوان بناطق، ثم نضمّه إلى صغرى الأصل هكذا بعض الإنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الحيوان بناطق ينتج : بعض الإنسان ليس بناطق وهو نقيض كبرى الأصل المفروضة ،الصدق، والفساد إنما جاء من نقيض النتيجة فهي حق وإلا لزم رفع النقيضين. Dassuqi: His saying: 'The moods of the third figure' means the productive moods. His saying: 'Eliminates what is other than six' explanation: Affirmative minor eliminates its negative, universal and particular, which number eight (four majors times two), and the universality of one of them eliminates the particular affirmative minor with a particular major, affirmative or negative, so these are ten. As for the negative minor, it was eliminated before, so consider it. His saying: 'Only produce by khulf...' Know that khulf is applicable in all moods. al-Attar: His saying: 'Eliminates what is other than six' explanation: The first condition, affirmative minor, eliminates the universal negative minor and the particular negative minor with the four majors, so these are eight moods resulting from multiplying two by four. The second condition, universality of one of the premises, eliminates the particular affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major and the particular negative major; so ten moods are eliminated, leaving six productive ones. His saying: 'By khulf' is attached to his saying 'to produce', and it is applicable in all moods. (Footnote 1) The secret is that the conclusion of this figure is always particular, so its contradictory is universal, which is suitable to be the major of the first figure, and its minor is always affirmative and suitable to be the minor of the first figure. By adding the contradictory of the conclusion to it, a syllogism is composed from the first figure which produces what contradicts the original major assumed true. The commentator draws attention to the first mood, and we explain it by matter in the second mood to analogize the rest. We say: Some human is animal, and every human is rational, produces: Some human is rational. If this were not true, then its contradictory would be true: No animal is rational. Then we join it to the original minor: Some human is animal, and no animal is rational, produces: Some human is not rational, which is the contradictory of the original major assumed true. The falsity came from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true, otherwise lifting the two contradictories would be necessary. (Footnote 1 cont.) His saying: 'And that conversion of the minor...' The secret is that the major in these four (moods) is universal and suitable to be the major of the first figure. So if the minor after its conversion is joined to it, and it is affirmative, a syllogism from the first figure is composed which necessarily produces the required, unlike the other two moods for the reason mentioned. (Footnote 2) His saying: 'It applies in two moods' the secret is that each of them has an affirmative major, so it converts to affirmative, and after its conversion it is suitable to be the minor of the first figure. By joining the original minor to it, a syllogism from the first figure is composed which produces what if converted would be the original conclusion. We explain it by matter in the first: Every human is animal and every human is rational produces: Some animal is rational. We say: We convert the major, then convert the order, so a syllogism from the first figure is composed thus: Some rational is human, and every human is animal produces: Some rational is animal, then we convert it to Some animal is rational, which is exactly the original conclusion. As for the remaining four, conversion of order is not possible for the reason mentioned.
Source pages p. 823

Commentary
This is a commentary on the matn concerning the third figure. Dassuqi and al-Attar explain in detail the elimination of moods. They clarify that the condition of affirmative minor eliminates all negative minors (universal and particular) when combined with any major (four types), giving eight moods. Then the condition that one premise is universal eliminates two more: the particular affirmative minor with particular affirmative major and with particular negative major, because both premises are particular. Thus ten moods are eliminated, leaving six. They then discuss the methods of proof. Both agree that khulf (reductio ad absurdum) applies to all six moods. The explanation: Since the conclusion is particular, its contradictory is universal and can serve as a major in the first figure; the minor is always affirmative and can serve as a minor in the first figure. Combining the contradictory with the original minor yields a first-figure syllogism that contradicts the original major. Then Dassuqi specifies that conversion of the minor applies to four moods: those where the major is universal. Al-Attar adds that conversion of order then conversion of conclusion applies to two moods: those where both premises are affirmative and the major is universal. He provides an example with 'Every human is animal, every human is rational' to demonstrate the process.

Key Terms
إيجاب الصغرى
كلّية إحدى المقدمتين
الضروب المنتجة

C) 10
D) 2

Key Takeaway
Only six premise combinations in the third figure satisfy both the affirmative minor and universal premise conditions, and these are the productive moods.

Proof by Khulf (Reductio) in the Third Figure
Commentators explain why khulf works for all third figure moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio ad absurdum (khulf) proof for any productive mood of the third figure.

Core Idea
Khulf works for all six moods because the particular conclusion's contradictory gives a universal major for the first figure.

The method of khulf (reductio ad absurdum) is a powerful proof technique applicable to all moods of the third figure. The commentary explains that since the conclusion of the third figure is always particular, its contradictory is universal, which can serve as the major premise in a first-figure syllogism. By combining this with the original minor, we obtain a contradiction that proves the original conclusion. Let's work through an example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original third figure syllogism
For example, minor: Some human is animal; major: Every human is rational; conclusion: Some human is rational.
2
Assume the opposite of the conclusion
Assume the conclusion is false, so its contradictory 'No animal is rational' is true.
3
Form a first-figure syllogism using the contradictory and original minor
Take the contradictory as major: 'No animal is rational', and the original minor: 'Some human is animal'. Combine to get 'Some human is not rational' (Ferio).
4
Observe the contradiction
The derived conclusion 'Some human is not rational' contradicts the original major 'Every human is rational'.
5
Conclude that the assumption was false
Therefore the assumption that the original conclusion is false leads to a contradiction, so the original conclusion must be true.

Conversion Methods in the Third Figure
Commentators specify which moods each conversion method proves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two conversion methods and identify which moods they apply to.

Core Idea
Conversion of the minor works for four moods with universal major; conversion of order then conclusion works for two moods with affirmative universal major.

Besides khulf, the third figure has two other proof methods: conversion of the minor premise (aks al-sughra) and conversion of order followed by conversion of the conclusion (aks al-tartib thumma aks al-natija). The commentary specifies that the first applies when the major is universal (four moods), and the second applies when the major is affirmative and universal (two moods). Let's explore why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proof methods
The methods are aks al-sughra (conversion of minor) and aks al-tartib + aks al-natija (conversion of order then conclusion).
2
Determine applicability of aks al-sughra
Aks al-sughra works when the major premise is universal (A or E). This applies to four moods of the third figure.
3
Determine applicability of aks al-tartib
Aks al-tartib works when the major is affirmative and universal (A only). This applies to two moods.
4
Apply aks al-sughra to an example
Given a third figure syllogism with universal major 'Every B is C' and minor 'Some A is B', convert the minor to 'Some B is A', then combine: 'Every B is C, Some B is A → Some A is C' (first figure, Darii).
5
Apply aks al-tartib to the example 'Every human is animal, every human is rational'
Convert the major 'Every human is animal' to 'Some animal is human'. Swap order: 'Some animal is human, every human is rational' yields 'Some animal is rational'. Then convert to 'Some rational is animal'? Wait, the final conversion gives the original conclusion 'Some animal is rational'. Follow the text: convert major, swap, produce conclusion, then convert again.
The original conclusion is 'Some animal is rational'. After conversion of order, we get 'Some rational is animal', which then converts back to 'Some animal is rational'.

Dassuqi: His saying: 'The moods of the third figure' means the productive moods. His saying: 'Eliminates what is other than six' explanation: Affirmative minor eliminates its negative, universal and particular, which number eight (four majors times two), and the universality of one of them eliminates the particular affirmative minor with a particular major, affirmative or negative, so these are ten. As for the negative minor, it was eliminated before, so consider it. His saying: 'Only produce by khulf...' Know that khulf is applicable in all moods. al-Attar: His saying: 'Eliminates what is other than six' explanation: The first condition, affirmative minor, eliminates the universal negative minor and the particular negative minor with the four majors, so these are eight moods resulting from multiplying two by four. The second condition, universality of one of the premises, eliminates the particular affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major and the particular negative major; so ten moods are eliminated, leaving six productive ones. His saying: 'By khulf' is attached to his saying 'to produce', and it is applicable in all moods. (Footnote 1) The secret is that the conclusion of this figure is always particular, so its contradictory is universal, which is suitable to be the major of the first figure, and its minor is always affirmative and suitable to be the minor of the first figure. By adding the contradictory of the conclusion to it, a syllogism is composed from the first figure which produces what contradicts the original major assumed true. The commentator draws attention to the first mood, and we explain it by matter in the second mood to analogize the rest. We say: Some human is animal, and every human is rational, produces: Some human is rational. If this were not true, then its contradictory would be true: No animal is rational. Then we join it to the original minor: Some human is animal, and no animal is rational, produces: Some human is not rational, which is the contradictory of the original major assumed true. The falsity came from the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is true, otherwise lifting the two contradictories would be necessary. (Footnote 1 cont.) His saying: 'And that conversion of the minor...' The secret is that the major in these four (moods) is universal and suitable to be the major of the first figure. So if the minor after its conversion is joined to it, and it is affirmative, a syllogism from the first figure is composed which necessarily produces the required, unlike the other two moods for the reason mentioned. (Footnote 2) His saying: 'It applies in two moods' the secret is that each of them has an affirmative major, so it converts to affirmative, and after its conversion it is suitable to be the minor of the first figure. By joining the original minor to it, a syllogism from the first figure is composed which produces what if converted would be the original conclusion. We explain it by matter in the first: Every human is animal and every human is rational produces: Some animal is rational. We say: We convert the major, then convert the order, so a syllogism from the first figure is composed thus: Some rational is human, and every human is animal produces: Some rational is animal, then we convert it to Some animal is rational, which is exactly the original conclusion. As for the remaining four, conversion of order is not possible for the reason mentioned.

Commentary
This is a commentary on the matn concerning the third figure. Dassuqi and al-Attar explain in detail the elimination of moods. They clarify that the condition of affirmative minor eliminates all negative minors (universal and particular) when combined with any major (four types), giving eight moods. Then the condition that one premise is universal eliminates two more: the particular affirmative minor with particular affirmative major and with particular negative major, because both premises are particular. Thus ten moods are eliminated, leaving six. They then discuss the methods of proof. Both agree that khulf (reductio ad absurdum) applies to all six moods. The explanation: Since the conclusion is particular, its contradictory is universal and can serve as a major in the first figure; the minor is always affirmative and can serve as a minor in the first figure. Combining the contradictory with the original minor yields a first-figure syllogism that contradicts the original major. Then Dassuqi specifies that conversion of the minor applies to four moods: those where the major is universal. Al-Attar adds that conversion of order then conversion of conclusion applies to two moods: those where both premises are affirmative and the major is universal. He provides an example with 'Every human is animal, every human is rational' to demonstrate the process.

عكس النتيجة

Reduction Methods to the First Figure
Author explains when conversion of minor and conversion of order produce valid reductions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which reduction method (aks al-sughra or aks al-tartib thumma aks al-natija) applies to a given first-figure syllogism mood and justify your choice based on the conditions on the premises.

Core Idea
Reduction to the first figure requires careful selection of the valid conversion method for each mood.

This passage from al-Dasuqi details two ways to reduce other moods to the perfect first figure: conversion of the minor premise and conversion of order followed by conversion of the conclusion. The author specifies which moods admit each method, with conditions on the quantity and quality of the premises. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing valid syllogistic proofs in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reduction methods
The text mentions 'aks al-sughra' (conversion of the minor premise) and 'aks al-tartib thumma aks al-natija' (conversion of the order then conversion of the conclusion). Note their names and the order of operations for each.
2
List the moods valid for aks al-sughra
The author states it runs in four moods: two universal affirmatives (AAA), universal affirmative + universal negative (EAE), particular affirmative + universal affirmative (AII), particular affirmative + universal negative (EIO). Write these down.
These correspond to the first four moods of the first figure in the standard list.
3
Understand why the last two moods are excluded
The last two moods (AII? Actually the excluded ones are 'composite of universal and particular affirmative' and 'universal affirmative and particular negative') have a major premise that is particular or particular negative, which cannot serve as the major in the first figure because the first figure requires a universal major.
The excluded moods are the fifth and sixth moods of the first figure (e.g., AII? No, the standard first figure has 4 moods: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. The author mentions four are valid for aks al-sughra, so the last two of the six original moods? Actually, the passage says 'دون الآخرين' meaning excluding the last two of the six moods of the first figure. So the six moods are: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO, AII? This is confusing. Focus on the condition: the major must be universal.
4
List the moods valid for aks al-tartib thumma aks al-natija
Only the first (AAA) and fifth (EAE) moods are valid. The other four are excluded because in those, the major premise is negative (converts to negative, unsuitable as minor) or particular (unsuitable as major after conversion).
The numbering of moods likely follows a specific order. The key is to recognize the condition on the major premise.
5
Apply the conditions to a new mood
Given a mood like AOO (if it were in the first figure), check if either reduction method works. AOO has a particular major (O), so aks al-sughra fails; the major is not universal. Also, the major is not negative? Actually O is particular negative; it converts to particular negative, unsuitable as minor. So aks al-tartib would also fail. This step reinforces the conditions.

وأن عكس الصغرى جار في أربعة أضرب، أعني: المركب من موجبتين كليتين، ومن موجبة كلية وسالبة كلية، ومن موجبة جزئية وموجبة كلية، ومن موجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية دون الأخيرين؛ أعني: المركب من كلية وموجبة جزئية، والمركب من موجبة كلية وسالبة جزئية؛ لأن كبراهما لا تصلح لكبروية الشكل الأول. وإن عكس الترتيب ثم عكس النتيجة يجري في ضربين، وهما: الأول والخامس دون الأربعة الباقية؛ لأن بعضها كبراه سالبة تنعكس سالبة أيضاً، فلا يصلح عكسها لصغروية الشكل الأول.
And that conversion of the minor premise runs in four moods: namely, the composite of two universal affirmatives, and of a universal affirmative and a universal negative, and of a particular affirmative and a universal affirmative, and of a particular affirmative and a universal negative, excluding the last two: namely, the composite of a universal and a particular affirmative, and the composite of a universal affirmative and a particular negative; because their major premise is not suitable for being the major of the first figure. And as for conversion of the order then conversion of the conclusion, it runs in two moods: the first and the fifth, excluding the remaining four; because some of them have a major premise that is negative, converting as negative also, so its conversion is not suitable for being the minor of the first figure.
Source pages p. 824

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the methods of reducing syllogisms to the first figure. The author, al-Dasuqi, explains two reduction techniques: (1) conversion of the minor premise (عكس الصغرى) and (2) conversion of the order followed by conversion of the conclusion (عكس الترتيب ثم عكس النتيجة). For the first technique, he lists four valid moods in the first figure where the minor premise can be converted to yield a first-figure syllogism that directly produces the desired conclusion. The condition is that the major premise must be universal, suitable for serving as the major of the first figure. The two excluded moods have a particular major or a particular negative major, which cannot be majors in the first figure. For the second technique, only two moods work: the first (AAA) and the fifth (EAE). In the other four moods, the major is either negative (and its conversion remains negative, unsuitable as a minor of the first figure) or particular (unsuitable as a major after conversion). An example from fiqh: Suppose we have 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram)' and 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' — this is a first-figure AAA syllogism. The conversion of the minor would require taking 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' and converting it to 'Some intoxicant is nabidh', which is not needed here but illustrates the process. The author's focus is on the formal conditions for these reductions.

Key Terms
عكس الصغرى (aks al-sughra): Conversion of the minor premise of a first-figure syllogism to produce a new minor premise for a first-figure reduction. [Note: Stable term across logic works; the conditions for validity are consistently discussed.]
عكس الترتيب ثم عكس النتيجة (aks al-tartib thumma aks al-natija): A two-step reduction: first swap the premises (convert order), then convert the resulting conclusion to get the original conclusion. [Note: Also known as 'aks al-muqaddimatayn' in some texts; the technique is standard in post-Avicennan logic.]
شكل أول (shakl awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism where the middle term is subject in the major and predicate in the minor, producing all valid moods with direct deduction. [Note: No significant shift; universally defined as the perfect figure in Aristotelian logic.]

Key Takeaway
The validity of reduction methods depends on the quantity and quality of the major premise; only certain moods admit each technique.

Understanding Khalf in the Third Figure
Author defines khalf in the third figure and distinguishes it from khalf in the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to execute the khalf procedure for the third figure of the categorical syllogism and distinguish it from the second figure.

Core Idea
Khalf by contradiction: assume the conclusion's opposite in the major to derive a contradiction.

In categorical logic, reductio ad absurdum (khalf) is a powerful proof technique. This passage teaches you exactly how to apply khalf when the original syllogism is in the third figure. The author also distinguishes it from the second figure, emphasizing a key structural difference. Understanding this will let you prove conclusions that may not be directly evident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original third-figure syllogism
Locate the two premises and their terms. In the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises. Ensure the syllogism is valid.
2
Take the contradictory of the conclusion
Form the opposite (naqid) of the original conclusion. This will become a premise in the new first-figure syllogism.
3
Make the contradictory the major premise
Set the contradictory as the major premise (kubra) of the new syllogism. The original minor premise remains the minor.
The original minor must be affirmative for first figure validity.
4
Verify the new syllogism is first figure
Check that the middle term is the subject in the major and predicate in the minor (first figure structure). The minor must be affirmative.
5
Derive the contradiction
The new syllogism should produce a conclusion that contradicts the original major premise (the one not used in the khalf). This proves the original conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في القياس. أما الخلف في هذا الشكل؛ فهو أن يؤخذ نقيض النتيجة، ويجعل كبرى، وصغرى القياس لإيجابها صغرى فينتظم منها قياس على هيئة الشكل الأول منتج لما ينافي الكبرى. في هذا الشكل أي الثالث، وقيد بذلك؛ لأن الخلف في الشكل الثاني أن يوجد نقيض النتيجة ويجعل صغرى القياس.
Chapter on syllogism. As for reductio ad absurdum in this figure: it is that the contradictory of the conclusion is taken and made the major premise, and the minor premise of the syllogism is made the minor (because it is affirmative), so that a syllogism in the form of the first figure is composed, producing something that contradicts the major premise. 'In this figure' means the third, and it is restricted to this because reductio in the second figure is that the contradictory of the conclusion is taken and made the minor premise of the syllogism.
Source pages p. 825

Commentary
This passage introduces the method of khalf (reductio ad absurdum) specifically for the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author, likely al-Dasuqi, explains the procedure: take the contradictory (نقيض) of the conclusion, and set it as the major premise of a new first-figure syllogism. The original minor premise remains the minor (since it is affirmative, a condition for the first figure). Together they produce a conclusion that contradicts the original major premise, showing that the original conclusion must be true. He then clarifies that in the second figure, the procedure differs: there the contradictory of the conclusion is made the minor premise, not the major. An example from kalam: Suppose we have the third-figure syllogism 'Some body is created, every body is substance, therefore some substance is created'. To prove this by khalf, we assume the contradictory 'No substance is created' as major, keep 'Some body is created' as minor, which in the first figure yields 'Some body is not substance', contradicting the true premise 'Every body is substance'. Thus the original conclusion is established. The restriction to the third figure is crucial because the role of the contradictory in the reduction differs per figure.

Key Takeaway
In third-figure khalf, the contradictory of the conclusion becomes the major premise in a new first-figure syllogism, while in the second figure it becomes the minor.

Khulf and Conversion in the Third Figure
Commentary explains khulf and conversion for the third figure with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a khulf argument and perform conversion of the minor for third-figure syllogisms, identifying which moods each method applies to.

Core Idea
The third figure is validated by khulf (reductio) or conversion of the minor, each with distinct logical conditions.

In this commentary passage from the Tadhheeb, the author explains two key methods for proving the validity of third-figure syllogisms: khulf (reductio ad absurdum) and 'aks al-sughra (conversion of the minor premise). This lesson unpacks these methods using the first mood as an example, showing how they reduce the third figure to the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third figure's structure
The middle term is the subject of both premises. For the first mood: 'every B is C' (minor) and 'every B is A' (major) imply 'some C is A' (conclusion). Recognize that both premises are universal affirmative.
This is the basic form used in the commentary.
2
Apply khulf (reductio)
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion: 'no C is A'. Combine with the original minor 'every B is C' as a first-figure syllogism: minor 'every B is C', major 'no C is A', yielding 'no B is A'. This contradicts the original major 'every B is A', so the conclusion must be true.
The khulf here uses the contradictory as the major premise, unlike the second figure.
3
Apply conversion of the minor
Convert the minor premise 'every B is C' to 'some C is B' (universal affirmative to particular affirmative). Then, with the original major 'every B is A', form a first-figure syllogism: minor 'some C is B', major 'every B is A' yields 'some C is A' (Darii mood). This proves the same conclusion.
Conversion works only if the minor is universal affirmative; particular minors cannot be converted to serve as majors in the first figure.
4
Compare the methods and their limitations
Khulf works for all moods of the third figure, while conversion of the minor fails for moods with particular minors (e.g., the sixth mood). The commentators note that only the first and fifth moods allow conversion; the sixth requires khulf or assumption.
Understanding these limitations is critical for applying the correct method.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
أمَّا الخُلْف في هذا الشَّكل؛ فهو أنْ يُؤخذ نقيضُ النَّتيجة، ويُجعَلَ كُبرى، وصُغرى القياس لإيجابها صُغرى فينتظم منها قياس على هيئة الشكل الأوَّلِ منتج لما يُنافي الكُبرى.
الدسوقي: يصلحُ عكسها؛ لِصغرويَّةِ الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ، وبعضُها صُغراهُ جزئيَّة، فَلا تقعُ كُبرى الشَّكل الأوَّل. لكن يُردُّ على الأوَّلِ: أنَّه قد سبق أنَّه قد يكونُ صُغرى الأوَّلِ سالبةٌ مُؤوّلةٌ بموجبة سالبة المحمول لِلتَّلازم، كَمَا تؤوّلُ بِموجبةٍ؛ موضوعُها مُشتمل على قيدِ السَّلبِ. انتهى ياسين. وَقولُهُ: وَهُمَا: الأوَّلُ، والخامس دونَ الأربعة... إلخ المناسب دونَ الثَّالث (۱)، فتأمل. (قَوْلُهُ: فِيْ هَذَا الشَّكل) أي: الثَّالث، وقُيَّد بذلك؛ لأنَّ الخلف في الشكل الثَّاني أنْ يوجد نقيضُ النَّتيجة، ويُجعل صُغرى القياس. (قَوْلُهُ: وَصُغْرَى... إلخ) أي: وتُجعلُ صُغرى القياس؛ لإيجابها صغرى. (قَوْلُهُ: لِمَا يُنَافِي الْكُبْرَى) أي: الَّتي هي مفروضةُ الصّدقِ، وَمَا نافي الصَّادق كاذب. العطار: التَّرتيب ليحصل المطلوب، وأمَّا الضَّرِبُ السَّادِسُ؛ فَلَا يجري فيهِ إِلَّا الخلفُ والافتراض في الكُبرى إنْ كانَت مركَّبة؛ لتحقق وجودِ الموضوع، ولا يجري فيه عكس الصُّغرى؛ لأنَّ الجزئيَّةَ لا تقعُ في كُبرى الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ وَلَا بعكس الكُبرى؛ لأنَّها لا تقبل العكس، وبتقدير انعكاسها لا تصلح لصغرويَّةِ الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ. (قَوْلُهُ: فِيْ هَذَا الشَّكل) قيَّدَهُ بِهِ؛ لِكَونِ الخلفِ الجاري فيهِ مُخالفاً للخلف الجاري قبلَهُ؛ لأنَّ نقيض النتيجة كُبرى، وصُغرى الأصل؛ لإيجابها صُغرى ههنا. (۱) (قَوْلُهُ: المناسب دونَ الثَّالث ما جعله يس الخامس وهو المجعول هنا الثَّالث كما يعلم بالمراجعة فلا اعتراض. ا. هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
فيقالُ في المثالِ الأَوَّلِ مثلاً: «لو لم يصدق بعضُ (ج) (أ)، لصدق لا شيء من (ج) (أ)، فكلّ (ب) (ج)، ولا شيء (ج) (أ)»، لينتج: «لا شيء من (ب) (أ)، وقد كان كبرى القياس: كلَّ (ب) (أ)»، هذا خُلْفٌ وأمَّا عكسُ الصُّغرى؛ فهو أن تُعكس الصُّغرى؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل
الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ: فِي الْمِثَالِ الْأَوَّلِ أي: الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ؛ أي: المركَّب من موجبةٍ كلِّيَّةٍ صُغرى، وموجبةٍ كلّيَّةٍ كُبرى كَقولِنَا: كلُّ ب ج، وكلُّ ب أ، فَبعضُ ج أ؛ أي: كلُّ حيوان جسم، وكلّ حيوان نام فبعض الجسمِ نام، لم يصدق بعضُ ج بعضُ الجسم نام، وهذا هو النتيجة. (قَوْلُهُ: لَصَدَقَ لَا شَيْءٍ مِنْ ج (أ) أي: لصدق نقيض سالبة كلية، وهي: لا شيء من الجسم بنام. (قَوْلُهُ: فَكُلُّ ب (ج) أي: فكلُّ حيوان جسم، وهذا هو صُغرى الضَّرب الأوّل، وقوله: (وَلَا شيء من ج (أ)؛ أي من الجسم، بنام، وهذا هو نقيضُ نتيجة الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ، وهذا قياس من الشَّكل الأوَّل (قَوْلُهُ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنْ ب أ) أي: من الحيوان بنام. (قَوْلُهُ: وَقَدْ كَانَ... إلخ) حال. (قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ ب أ) أي: كلُّ حيوان نام. (قَوْلُهُ: هَذَا خُلْفٌ) أي: ما ذكرَ منَ النَّتيجة المنافيةِ لِكُبرى الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ خلف؛ أي: باطل؛ لأنَّها مُنافيةٌ لِمفروض الصدق، وما نافي الصَّادق كاذب، وهذا الكذب نشأ مِنْ نقيض نتيجةِ الضَّرْبِ الأوّل، فيكون ذلكَ النَّقيضُ كذباً، وحينئذ؛ فنتيجةُ الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ حقٌّ. (قَوْلُهُ: لِيَرْتَدَّ) أي: يرجع.
العطار: وقد جعلَ النَّقيض صُغرى فيما سبق، وأنَّ هذا القياس بعد الترتيب مُنتج لِمَا يُنافي الكُبرى، والَّذي قبلَهُ مُنتج لِمَا يُناقضُ الصُّغرى.
Chapter: On Syllogism. As for khulf in this figure: it is to take the contradictory of the conclusion, make it a major premise, and [take] the minor premise of the syllogism [as is] because it is affirmative, as a minor premise, so a syllogism in the form of the first figure is composed from them, producing a conclusion that contradicts the major premise. Al-Dasuqi: Its conversion is valid because of the minorship of the first figure, and some of them have a particular minor premise, so it cannot become a major premise of the first figure. But it is objected to the first: that it has preceded that the minor of the first may be a negative [premise] interpreted as an affirmative with a negative predicate due to entailment, just as it is interpreted as an affirmative whose subject includes the condition of negation. Yasin ended. And his statement: 'And they are: the first, and the fifth without the four... etc.' The appropriate is without the third (1), so ponder. (His statement: 'In this figure') i.e., the third, and it was qualified by that because khulf in the second figure is to find the contradictory of the conclusion and make it a minor premise. (His statement: 'And the minor... etc.') i.e., and the minor of the syllogism is made [as is] because it is affirmative as a minor. (His statement: 'that contradicts the major') i.e., which is assumed true, and what contradicts the true is false. Al-`Attar: The arrangement is to obtain the desired, and as for the sixth mood, only khulf and assumption [run] in it in the major if it is compound, to verify the existence of the subject, and there is no conversion of the minor in it because the particular cannot become a major of the first figure, nor by conversion of the major because it does not accept conversion, and if it were converted it would not be suitable for the minorship of the first figure. (His statement: 'In this figure') he qualified it by that because the khulf that runs in it is different from the khulf that ran before; because the contradictory of the conclusion is a major, and the minor of the original [is taken] because it is affirmative as a minor here. (1) (His statement: 'The appropriate without the third is what he made the fifth, and what is made here the third, as is known by review, so no objection. End. Al-Sharnubi.) Then it is said in the first example: 'If not 'some C is A' were true, then 'no C is A' would be true, so 'every B is C' and 'no C is A' produces 'no B is A', while the major premise of the [original] syllogism was 'every B is A'. This is a khulf. As for conversion of the minor, it is to convert the minor to return to the [first] figure. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'In the first example' i.e., the first mood; i.e., composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, like our saying: 'Every B is C, and every B is A, so some C is A'; i.e., 'Every animal is a body, and every animal is sentient, so some body is sentient'. 'If not some C is A were true' i.e., the contradictory, a universal negative, namely 'No body is sentient'. (His statement: 'So every B is C' i.e., 'Every animal is a body', and this is the minor of the first mood. And his statement: 'And no C is A' i.e., from bodies, sentient, and this is the contradictory of the conclusion of the first mood, and this is a syllogism from the first figure. (His statement: 'No B is A' i.e., from animals, sentient. (His statement: 'And it was... etc.') is a circumstantial clause. (His statement: 'Every B is A') i.e., 'Every animal is sentient'. (His statement: 'This is a khulf') i.e., what was mentioned of the conclusion that contradicts the major of the first mood is khulf, i.e., false, because it contradicts what is assumed true, and what contradicts the true is false. And this falsehood arose from the contradictory of the conclusion of the first mood, so that contradictory is false, and then the conclusion of the first mood is true. (His statement: 'to return') i.e., to go back. Al-`Attar: And he made the contradictory a minor previously, and that this syllogism after arrangement produces what contradicts the major, and the one before it produced what contradicts the minor.
Source pages p. 825

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn section about the third figure of the categorical syllogism, specifically explaining two methods to prove its validity: khulf (reductio ad absurdum) and 'aks al-sughra (conversion of the minor premise). The author first defines khulf in the third figure: take the contradictory of the conclusion, make it the major premise, and keep the original minor premise (which is affirmative) as the minor, thereby forming a syllogism in the first figure that yields a conclusion contradicting the original major premise. Then al-Dasuqi comments that converting the original minor (to revert to the first figure) is valid because the minor of the first figure must be affirmative, but some moods have a particular minor, which cannot serve as a major in the first figure. He also notes a subtlety: the minor of the first figure may be a negative premise that is interpreted as an affirmative with a negative predicate due to logical entailment. Al-`Attar adds that the sixth mood only allows khulf and assumption (if the major is compound), not conversion of the minor, because a particular premise cannot be a major in the first figure. The passage then provides a concrete example: from the first mood of the third figure (every B is C, every B is A, so some C is A). Using animals: every animal is a body, every animal is sentient, so some body is sentient. For khulf, assume the conclusion 'some body is sentient' is false, so its contradictory 'no body is sentient' is true. Combine with the original minor 'every animal is a body' to get 'no animal is sentient' via first figure (Camestres?). But the original major was 'every animal is sentient', contradiction — hence the original conclusion must be true. The commentary explains each step, noting that the khulf produces a contradiction with the major premise. Then it mentions conversion of the minor: to reduce the third figure to the first, convert the minor premise (e.g., 'every B is C' becomes 'some C is B'), but this requires the minor to be convertible (universal affirmative converts to particular affirmative). The passage ends with al-`Attar distinguishing the two applications of khulf: one where the contradictory becomes the major (as here), and a previous one where it became the minor.

Key Terms
الخلف
عكس الصغرى
الشكل الثالث
الضرب الأول

C) 'All C is B' is true

Key Takeaway
The third figure can be proven by khulf (reductio) or conversion of the minor; khulf always works, but conversion only when the minor is universal affirmative.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Reduction by Contradiction (Khulf) in the First Mood
Author explains khulf reduction for the first mood of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio ad absurdum proof for a categorical syllogism in the first figure and verify its validity.

Core Idea
Khulf proves a conclusion by showing its negation leads to contradiction with a true premise.

In this passage, the author demonstrates how to prove the conclusion of a first-figure syllogism indirectly using reductio ad absurdum (khulf). By assuming the opposite of the conclusion and combining it with one original premise, a contradiction is derived with the other premise, thereby confirming the original conclusion. The commentators al-Dasūqī and al-ʿAṭṭār clarify the steps with the example 'all B is J, all B is A, therefore some J is A.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Locate the original premises and conclusion of the first mood: minor premise (all B is J), major premise (all B is A), conclusion (some J is A). Note that the conclusion is a particular affirmative.
The original syllogism is valid by the first figure.
2
Assume the negation of the conclusion
Assume the conclusion 'some J is A' is false. Its contradictory is 'no J is A' (universal negative). This assumption becomes a new premise.
Negation must be exact contradictory, not contrary.
3
Form a new syllogism with the negation and one original premise
Combine the assumed negation 'no J is A' with the original minor premise 'all B is J' to form a new first-figure syllogism: all B is J, no J is A, therefore no B is A.
This syllogism is also in the first figure and is valid.
4
Derive a contradiction with the other premise
The conclusion of the new syllogism, 'no B is A', contradicts the original major premise 'all B is A'. Since the major premise is true, the new conclusion must be false.
Contradiction occurs because 'all B is A' and 'no B is A' cannot both be true.
5
Conclude the original conclusion is true
The falsehood of 'no B is A' traces back to the assumption that 'some J is A' is false. Hence that assumption is false, so the original conclusion 'some J is A' is true.
This completes the khulf proof.

فيقالُ في المثالِ الأَوَّلِ مثلاً : «لو لم يصدق بعضُ (ج) (أ)، لصدق لا شيء من (ج) (أ)، فكلّ (ب) (ج) ، ولا شيء (ج) (أ)) ، لينتج : «لا شيء من (ب) (أ)، وقد كان كبرى القياس : كلَّ (ب) (أ)»، هذا خُلْفٌ.
قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : فِي الْمِثَالِ الْأَوَّلِ) أي : الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ؛ أي: المركَّب من موجبةٍ كلِّيَّةٍ صُغرى، وموجبة كلّيَّةٍ كُبرى كَقولِنَا : كلُّ ب ج ، وكلُّ ب أ، فَبعضُ ج أ؛ أي: كلُّ حيوان جسم، وكلّ حيوان نام فبعض الجسمِ نام، لم يصدق بعضُ ج بعضُ الجسم نام، وهذا هو النتيجة . (قَوْلُهُ : لَصَدَقَ لَا شَيْءٍ مِنْ ج (أ)) أي: لصدق نقيضُ سالبة كلية، وهي: لا شيء من الجسم بنام . (قَوْلُهُ : فَكُلُّ ب (ج)) أي : فكلُّ حيوان جسم، وهذا هو صُغرى الضَّرب الأوّل، وقوله: (وَلَا شيء من ج (أ))؛ أي من الجسم بنام، وهذا هو نقيضُ نتيجة الضَّرْبِ الأوَّلِ، وهذا قياس من الشَّكل الأوَّل. (قَوْلُهُ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنْ ب أ) أي : من الحيوان بنام. (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ كَانَ . . . إِلَخ) حال . (قَوْلُهُ : كُلُّ ب أ) أي : كلُّ حيوان نام . (قَوْلُهُ : هَذَا خُلْفٌ) أي : ما ذكرَ منَ النَّتيجة المنافيةِ لِكُبرى الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ خلف؛ أي: باطل؛ لأنَّها مُنافيةٌ لِمفروض الصدق، وما نافي الصَّادق كاذب، وهذا الكذب نشأ مِنْ نقيض نتيجةِ الضَّرْبِ الأوّل، فيكون ذلكَ النَّقيضُ كذباً، وحينئذ فنتيجةُ الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ حقٌّ . (قَوْلُهُ : لِيَرْتَدَّ) أي: يرجع .
قال العطار: وقد جعلَ النَّقيض صُغرى فيما سبق، وأنَّ هذا القياس بعد الترتيب مُنتج لِمَا يُنافي الكُبرى، والَّذي قبلَهُ مُنتج لِمَا يُناقضُ الصُّغرى.
It is said in the first example: 'If not some J is A, then no J is A, so all B is J, and no J is A, to produce: no B is A, whereas the major premise of the syllogism was: all B is A.' This is a contradiction. Al-Dasūqī said: His statement 'in the first example' means the first mood, i.e., composed of a universal affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, like our saying: all B is J, and all B is A, so some J is A; i.e., every animal is a body, and every animal is sentient, so some body is sentient. 'Some J is A' is the conclusion. His statement: 'would be true: no J is A' means the contradictory of a universal negative would be true, i.e., no body is sentient. His statement: 'so all B is J' means every animal is a body, and this is the minor premise of the first mood. His statement: 'and no J is A' means no body is sentient, and this is the contradictory of the conclusion of the first mood. This is a syllogism of the first figure. His statement: 'no B is A' means no animal is sentient. His statement: 'whereas it was... etc.' is a circumstantial clause. His statement: 'all B is A' means every animal is sentient. His statement: 'this is a contradiction' means what was mentioned of the result conflicting with the major premise of the first mood is a contradiction, i.e., false, because it conflicts with what is assumed true, and what conflicts with the truth is false. This falsehood arose from the contradictory of the conclusion of the first mood, so that contradictory is false, and thus the conclusion of the first mood is true. His statement: 'to return' means to go back. Al-ʿAṭṭār said: He made the contradictory a minor premise previously, and that this syllogism after arrangement produces what contradicts the major premise, and the one before it produces what contradicts the minor premise.
Source pages p. 826

Commentary
This passage explains the method of reduction by contradiction (khulf) for the first mood of the first figure. The author first presents the matn: In the first example (the first mood: universal affirmative minor and major), if we assume the conclusion is false, i.e., 'some J is A' is false, then its contradictory 'no J is A' must be true. Combining this with the original minor premise 'all B is J' (which is true) forms a new syllogism in the first figure: all B is J, no J is A, therefore no B is A. But this conclusion contradicts the original major premise 'all B is A'. Since the original major premise is assumed true, the derived conclusion must be false, meaning the assumption that the original conclusion is false is itself false. Hence the original conclusion 'some J is A' is true. Al-Dasūqī's commentary clarifies each part: he identifies the first mood, gives the example with 'animal', 'body', and 'sentient', and explains the steps. He notes that the new syllogism is in the first figure. Al-ʿAṭṭār adds that the contradiction can be arranged to conflict with either the major or minor premise; here it conflicts with the major.

Key Terms
خلف
نقيض
ضرب
شكل

Key Takeaway
Khulf proves a conclusion by showing its negation leads to a contradiction with a true premise.

The Syllogism

Conversion of the Minor Premise (Aks al-Sughra)
Author defines and illustrates conversion of the minor premise to reduce to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion of the minor premise to reduce a non-first-figure syllogism to the first figure and verify the conclusion.

Core Idea
Converting the minor premise returns a syllogism to the first figure for immediate validity.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is the only one that yields conclusions directly without further operations. However, many valid syllogisms in other figures need to be reduced to the first figure to demonstrate their validity. One method of reduction is to convert the minor premise, swapping its subject and predicate while preserving truth. This lesson, drawn from the Tadhheeb with al-Dasūqī's commentary, teaches you how to perform this operation and why it works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism's figure
Check whether the syllogism is in a non-first figure (second, third, or fourth). In the passage, the original 'second example' is not clearly first figure; it has a particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, which needs reduction.
The original minor premise is 'some B is J' and major is 'all B is A' – this is not in first figure because the middle term (B) is not in the proper position.
2
Determine which premise to convert
In aks al-sughra, you convert the minor premise. Here, the minor is 'some B is J' (particular affirmative).
Only particular affirmative and universal negative propositions convert simply.
3
Perform the conversion
Swap the subject and predicate of the minor premise, preserving the quantity and quality. 'Some B is J' becomes 'some J is B'.
This conversion is truth-preserving because particular affirmatives convert simply.
4
Check the new syllogism's figure
After conversion, the premises become: 'some J is B' (minor) and 'all B is A' (major). The middle term (B) is now the subject of the major and predicate of the minor, which is the first figure.
The first figure requires the middle term to be subject of the major and predicate of the minor.
5
Draw the conclusion
In the first figure, the conclusion follows directly: 'some J is A'. Verify it matches the original desired conclusion.
The original desired conclusion was 'some J is A' (some animal is rational).

وأمَّا عكسُ الصُّغرى ؛ فهو أن تُعكس الصُّغرى؛ ليرتد إلى الشكل الأوّل، فينتج النتيجة الأولى المطلوبة بديهة كَقولنا في المثالِ الثَّاني : بعض (ج) (ب) ، وكلُّ (ب) (أ)، فبعض (ج) (أ).
قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : فِي الْمِثَالِ الثَّانِي) أي: وهو المركَّبُ من موجبة جزئية صغرى، وموجبة كُلِّيَّةٍ كُبرى كَقولِنَا : بعضُ ب ج ، وكلُّ ب أ ؛ أي: بعضُ الإنسانِ حيوان، وكلُّ إنسانٍ ناطق، ينتج : بعضُ الحيوانِ ناطق . (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج (أ) أي : فينتج : بعضُ الحيوانِ ناطق.
As for conversion of the minor premise, it is that you convert the minor premise so that it returns to the first figure, and it produces the first desired conclusion directly, like our saying in the second example: some J is B, and all B is A, so some J is A. Al-Dasūqī said: His statement 'in the second example' means it is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, like our saying: some B is J, and all B is A, i.e., some human is an animal, and every human is rational, it produces: some animal is rational. His statement: 'some J is B' means some human is an animal, and this is the conversion of the original minor premise. His statement: 'and all B is A' means every human is rational, and this is the original major premise. His statement: 'so some J is A' means it produces: some animal is rational.
Source pages p. 826

Commentary
This passage explains the method of reduction by converting the minor premise (aks al-sughra). The matn states: to reduce a syllogism to the first figure, one converts the minor premise. The example given is from the second mood: originally a particular affirmative minor ('some B is J') and a universal affirmative major ('all B is A'). By converting the minor premise to 'some J is B', the syllogism becomes in the first figure: some J is B, all B is A, therefore some J is A. Al-Dasūqī clarifies that the original minor premise is 'some B is J', and its conversion yields 'some J is B'. The major premise remains unchanged. The conclusion follows directly from the first figure. This method is immediate and does not require further steps.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Inversion of Order (Aks al-Tartib) in the First Figure
Author explains swapping premises (aks al-tartib) to reduce a syllogism to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply inversion of order to reduce a categorical syllogism from any figure to the first figure and identify the relationship between the produced conclusion and the desired conclusion.

Core Idea
Inversion of order swaps premises to fit the first figure, producing the converse of the desired conclusion.

Sometimes a syllogism's premises are arranged in an order that does not match the first figure, but the conclusion is still needed. Inversion of order (aks al-tartib) is a method to rearrange the premises—making the major premise the minor and vice versa—so that the syllogism takes the form of the first figure. However, the resulting conclusion is not directly the one sought; it is its converse. You then convert that conclusion to obtain the original desired statement. This lesson walks through the technique using an example from the matn, with clarifications from the commentators al-Dasūqī and al-ʿAṭṭār.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism's premises
Take the syllogism that is not in the first figure. Determine which premise is the major (containing the predicate of the conclusion) and which is the minor (containing the subject). In the third example, the minor is 'every B is J' (universal affirmative) and the major is 'some B is A' (particular affirmative).
The source text initially writes 'all B is A' but the commentators correct the major to particular affirmative.
2
Swap the premises to create the first figure
Make the original major premise the new minor premise, and the original minor premise the new major premise. In the example, 'some B is A' becomes the minor, and 'every B is J' becomes the major. The new syllogism in the first figure is: minor (some B is A), major (every B is J).
3
Draw the new conclusion
Apply the rule of the first figure: if the minor is particular affirmative and the major is universal affirmative, the conclusion is particular affirmative: 'some A is J' (but wait, the text concludes 'some J is A'). Check the pattern: the middle term is B. The subject of the conclusion is the term that was the subject of the major premise (J), and the predicate is the term from the minor premise (A). Actually, careful: in the first figure, the middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor. After swapping, the major is 'every B is J' (B is subject, J is predicate), the minor is 'some B is A' (B is subject, A is predicate). The middle term B is subject of both, which is incorrect for the first figure. There seems to be a confusion: the text says after inversion the syllogism is in the first figure, but both premises have B as subject. Let's re-read: The original is 'كل ب ج' and 'وبعض ب أ'? Actually the corrected form is: minor: كل ب ج (all B is J), major: بعض ب أ (some B is A). After swapping, we have minor: بعض ب أ (some B is A), major: كل ب ج (all B is J). This is still not the standard first figure because the middle term B is subject of both. However, the commentators say this yields a first-figure syllogism? Possibly they consider the first figure as having the middle term subject of one and predicate of the other? The standard first figure requires the middle term to be predicate of the minor and subject of the major. Here after swap, the minor is 'some B is A' (B subject, A predicate), major 'all B is J' (B subject, J predicate). That is not first figure. There might be a misinterpretation: perhaps the inversion of order includes also conversion of one premise? The matn says 'you convert the major premise first' (تعكس الكبرى). The term 'عكس' here might mean 'convert' in the sense of logical conversion (switching subject and predicate) not just swapping. Indeed, 'aks' can mean conversion (like 'aks al-qadiyya). So the process is: first convert the major premise (change subject and predicate), then swap the premises. In the example, the major originally is 'all B is A'? Actually the text says 'كل ب ج وكل ب أ' but commentary corrects major to 'some B is A'. To get a first figure, we need the middle term in the correct positions. Let's follow al-Dasūqī's explanation: He says 'in this figure' (meaning the figure previously discussed), inversion of order requires converting the major first. So step: convert the major premise (e.g., from 'some B is A' to 'some A is B'), then swap. That would yield: minor (original minor unchanged? Actually after conversion and swap, the premises become: minor (converted original major: some A is B), major (original minor: all B is J). Now the middle term B is subject of the major and predicate of the minor? Minor: some A is B (A subject, B predicate), major: all B is J (B subject, J predicate). Middle term B is predicate of minor and subject of major: perfect first figure! The conclusion is some A is J. That is the converse of the desired 'some J is A'. So the process is: 1) convert the major premise (change subject and predicate: from 'some B is A' to 'some A is B'). 2) Swap the premises so that the converted major becomes the minor, and the original minor becomes the major. 3) A first-figure syllogism results: some A is B, all B is J, therefore some A is J. That conclusion is the converse of the desired 'some J is A'. So the guided path should include conversion of the major premise first. The matn says 'تعكس الكبرى أولاً' which means 'convert the major first'. The word 'عكس' here means 'convert' (logical conversion), not just swap. The subsequent phrase 'تجعل الكبرى صغرى والصغرى كبرى' explains the swapping after conversion. So the steps are: convert major, then swap. We need to adjust guided path accordingly.
4
Convert the major premise (logical conversion)
Apply immediate conversion to the major premise: swap its subject and predicate while preserving truth. For 'some B is A', conversion gives 'some A is B' (particular affirmative converts to particular affirmative). This is the first step in inversion of order.
The source uses 'عكس' to mean conversion here.
5
Swap the premises
Now make the converted major premise the minor premise, and the original minor premise the major premise. So the new minor is 'some A is B', and the new major is 'all B is J'.

Then as for inversion of order in this figure, it is that you convert the major premise first, making the major premise the minor and the minor premise the major, so a syllogism is arranged in the form of the first figure, producing what is converted to the conclusion, like our saying in the third example: all B is J, and all B is A, so some J is A; i.e., every human is an animal, and some human is rational, so some animal is rational. His statement 'it is that you convert the major premise' is a necessary restriction to the matn, and it is not part of the definition of inversion of order, though the commentator's words might give that impression; it is similar to what preceded. His statement 'in the third example' means it is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, like our saying: all B is J, and all B is A, so some J is A; i.e., every human is an animal, and some human is rational, so some animal is rational. Al-ʿAṭṭār said: His statement 'produces what is converted to the conclusion' means this syllogism produces a particular affirmative, but its conclusion is not desired; rather, what is desired is the converse of this conclusion, which is also a particular affirmative.

Commentary
This passage explains inversion of order (aks al-tartib) as another method of reduction to the first figure. The matn says: in this figure (the figure being discussed, likely the one resulting from previous steps), you convert the major premise first, making it the minor, and the original minor becomes the major. This yields a syllogism in the first figure, whose conclusion is the converse of the desired conclusion. The example given is the third example: a universal affirmative minor ('all B is J') and a particular affirmative major ('all B is A'? Wait, the text says 'all B is A' but that is universal affirmative; the commentary clarifies it is a particular affirmative major? There is confusion: the matn says 'كل ب ج وكل ب أ' which are both universal, but the commentary says the third example is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major. Actually the matn says 'كل ب ج وكل ب أ' which are both universal, but then the conclusion is 'بعض ج أ' (particular affirmative). This suggests an error in the matn? The commentator al-Dasūqī explains that the major premise is particular affirmative: 'وبعض الإنسان ناطق' (some human is rational). So the matn likely has a typo: it should be 'وبعض ب أ' or the like. Al-ʿAṭṭār notes that the syllogism produces a particular affirmative, but the desired conclusion is its converse, which is also a particular affirmative.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Reducing the Third Figure by Inversion of Order
Inversion of order in the third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a third-figure categorical syllogism to the first figure using the inversion of order procedure.

Core Idea
The third figure reduces to the first by converting the major premise first, then swapping.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism is valid but imperfect. To check its validity and see the logical connection clearly, logicians reduce it to the perfect first figure. The technique is 'aks al-tartib' (inversion of order), which requires converting one premise and swapping the order. This lesson walks through that procedure with Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises in the third figure
Locate the minor premise (subject is middle term) and major premise (predicate is middle term). In the third figure, both premises have the middle term as subject.
Example: 'Every B is C' (minor), 'Every B is A' (major).
2
Convert the major premise
Perform simple conversion on the major premise. For a universal affirmative, convert to a particular affirmative. For a particular affirmative, it remains the same. The converted major will become the new minor premise.
From 'Every B is A' we get 'Some A is B'.
3
Swap the premises
Place the converted major as the new minor premise, and the original minor as the new major premise. This yields a syllogism in the first figure (middle term is subject in major, predicate in minor).
New minor: 'Some A is B'; new major: 'Every B is C'.
4
Derive the conclusion in the first figure
Apply the rule of the first figure: minor premise must be affirmative, major universal. The conclusion follows: 'Some A is C' (from the example).
This is a valid Darii mood.
5
Obtain the desired conclusion
If the desired conclusion is the converse of the derived one, convert it. Often the original problem's conclusion is 'Some C is A', which is the converse of 'Some A is C'.
Simple conversion of particular affirmative is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ

ثمَّ وأمَّا عكسُ التَّرتيب في هذا الشكل؛ فهو أن تُعكَسَ الكُبرى أوَّلاً، ثم تُجعلَ الكُبرى صُغرى والصغرى كُبرى، فينتظم قياس على هيئة الشكل الأوَّلِ، ينتج لما ينعكس إلى النتيجةِ، كقولنا في المثال الثَّالث مثلاً :

قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : فِي الْمِثَالِ الثَّانِي) أي: وهو المركَّبُ من موجبة جزئية صغرى، وموجبة كُلِّيَّةٍ كُبرى كَقولِنَا : بعضُ ب ج ، وكلُّ ب أ ؛ أي: بعضُ الإنسانِ حيوان، وكلُّ إنسانٍ ناطق، ينتج : بعضُ الحيوانِ ناطق .
(قَوْلُهُ : بَعْضُ ج (ب) أي : بعضُ الإنسان حيوان، وهذا هو عكس صغرى الأصل.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَكُلُّ بـ (أ) أي : وكلُّ إنسان ناطق، وهذا هو كبرى الأصل.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج (أ) أي : فينتج : بعضُ الحيوانِ ناطق .
(قَوْلُهُ : فِيْ هَذَا الشَّكْل قيد بِهِ لِمَا سبقَ مِنْ أنَّ عكس الترتيب في الشَّكلِ الثَّاني تعكسُ الصُّغرى فيهِ أوَّلاً، ثُمَّ تعكس الترتيب.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَهُوَ أَنْ تُعْكَسَ الْكُبْرَى هذا تقييد للمتن لا بدَّ منه، وليسَ مِن مُسمَّى عكس التَّرتيب، وإنْ كانَ كلامُ الشَّارحِ يُوهم ذلك، فهو نظيرُ مَا تقدَّم.
(قَوْلُهُ : فِى الْمِثَالِ الثَّالِثِ) أي: وهو المركَّبُ من موجبةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ صُغرى، وموجبة جزئيَّةٍ كُبرى كَقولِنَا : كلُّ ب ج، وكلُّ ب أ، فبعضُ ج أ؛ أي: كلُّ إنسان حيوان، وبعضُ الإنسانِ ناطق، فينتج : بعض الحيوانِ ناطق .

ثمَّ قال العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : مُنْتِجٌ لِمَا يَنْعَكِسُ إِلَى النَّتِيْجَةِ) يعني أنَّ هذا القياس يُنتج الموجبة الجزئية، ونتيجته ليست بمطلوبة، بل المطلوب إنَّما هو عكس هذه النتيجة، وَهِي موجبة جزئية أيضاً.
ثم قال: بعض (أ) (ب) ، و كلُّ (ب) (ج) ، فبعض (أ) (ج)»، وينعكس إلى: «بعض (ج) (أ)» وإنَّما قال في هذا الشَّكل بعكس الصُّغرى، وفي الشكل الثاني بعكس الكبرى ؛ لأنَّ هذا الشَّكل إنَّما يرتد إلى الشَّكل الأوَّل، بعكس الصُّغرى، والشَّكل الثَّاني إنَّما يرتدُّ بعكس الكبرى، وذلك ظاهر .
Section: On the syllogism. Then as for the inversion of order in this figure: it is that the major premise is converted first, then the major is made minor and the minor major, so a syllogism is formed in the form of the first figure, producing what is converted to the conclusion, like our saying in the third example, for instance: Al-Dasuqi said: His statement: 'In the second example' means: it is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, like our saying: Some B is C, and every B is A; i.e., some human is animal, and every human is rational, produces: some animal is rational. (His statement: 'Some C is B' means: some human is animal, and this is the converse of the original minor. His statement: 'And every B is A' means: and every human is rational, and this is the original major. His statement: 'So some C is A' means: it produces: some animal is rational. His statement: 'In this figure' is restricted by it due to what preceded: that the inversion of order in the second figure, you convert the minor in it first, then you invert the order. His statement: 'So it is that the major is converted' is a restriction of the text that is necessary, and it is not part of the meaning of inversion of order, although the commentator's speech suggests that, and it is similar to what preceded. His statement: 'In the third example' means: it is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, like our saying: Every B is C, and every B is A, so some C is A; i.e., every human is animal, and some human is rational, produces: some animal is rational. Then al-Attar said: His statement: 'Producing what is converted to the conclusion' means that this syllogism produces the particular affirmative, and its conclusion is not desired, rather what is desired is the converse of this conclusion, and it is also a particular affirmative. Then he said: 'Some A is B, and every B is C, so some A is C', and it converts to: 'Some C is A'. And he only said in this figure 'by converting the minor' and in the second figure 'by converting the major' because this figure reduces to the first figure by converting the minor, and the second figure reduces by converting the major, and that is clear.
Source pages p. 827

Commentary
This passage explains the process of inversion of order (aks al-tartib) in the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The third figure is where the middle term is the subject in both premises. To reduce it to the perfect first figure, one must first convert the major premise (using simple conversion if possible) and then swap the premises, making the original major into the minor and vice versa. The commentary gives two examples: one from the 'second example' (a particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major) and one from the 'third example' (a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major). In the third figure, the inversion of order is different from the second figure, where the minor premise is converted first. The commentator al-Attar clarifies that the resulting syllogism in the first figure yields a particular affirmative conclusion, but the desired conclusion is actually the converse of that, which is also particular affirmative. The final example uses terms A, B, C to show the process: from 'Some A is B, every B is C, so some A is C' we convert to 'Some C is A'. The examples are grounded in the Islamic philosophical tradition (human, animal, rational).

The Syllogism

Conditions for a Valid Fourth Figure Syllogism
Author states the two conditions for the fourth figure to be productive, with commentary explaining the barren cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a fourth figure syllogism is productive based on quality and quantity conditions, and recognize barren cases.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires either both affirmative with a universal minor, or a difference in quality with at least one universal.

In the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, not every combination of affirmative/negative and universal/particular yields a valid conclusion. The author specifies two sufficient conditions for productivity. If neither is met, the syllogism is barren, meaning no necessary conclusion follows. This lesson will help you apply these conditions to evaluate syllogisms in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the fourth figure
The middle term is the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor premise. So the pattern is: major premise: P is M, minor premise: M is S, conclusion: S is P.
2
Apply the first condition
Check if both premises are affirmative AND the minor premise (M is S) is universal. If yes, the syllogism is productive.
Example: All acts of worship (P) are rewarded (M) [affirmative universal], All prayers (S) are acts of worship (M) [affirmative universal minor] → productive.
3
Apply the second condition
Check if the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) AND at least one premise is universal. If yes, the syllogism is productive.
Example: All prophets (P) are truthful (M) [affirmative universal], No liar (S) is a prophet (M) [negative universal] → productive.
4
Identify the three barren cases
If neither condition holds, then one of three scenarios occurs: (1) both premises negative, (2) both affirmative with a particular minor, or (3) difference in quality with both premises particular. All are barren.
Example of (1): No human (P) is stone (M), No stone (M) is living (S) → barren.
5
Test an Islamic example
Given a fourth figure syllogism on an Islamic topic, determine whether it meets either condition or falls into a barren case.
Practice: 'No riba is halal (negative universal), All trade is halal (affirmative universal)' – this meets the second condition (difference in quality, both universal) and is productive.
