Conversion of the Third Figure's Conclusion to the First Figure
Author explains conversion of the third figure's conclusion and its reduction to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert the conclusion of a valid third-figure syllogism and explain how conversion of the minor premise reduces the third figure to the first figure.

Core Idea
The third figure's conclusion can be converted simply, and reduction to the first figure requires converting the minor premise, not the major.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a key technique in syllogistic reduction: converting the conclusion of a third-figure syllogism and clarifying that the third figure is reduced to the first figure by converting the minor premise, not the major. Understanding this distinction is essential for mastering the relationships between syllogistic figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and figure
The passage gives the mood: Some A is B, every B is C, therefore some A is C. This is a third-figure syllogism (middle term B is subject in both premises). Identify the terms: minor term A, middle term B, major term C.
2
Apply simple conversion to the conclusion
The conclusion 'some A is C' is a particular affirmative. Simple conversion (conversio simplex) exchanges subject and predicate while preserving quantity and quality, resulting in 'some C is A'.
Conversion works only for particular affirmatives and universal negatives without change.
3
Understand the reduction process
To reduce this third-figure mood to the first figure, we convert the minor premise ('some A is B' becomes 'some B is A'), not the major. This is because the first figure requires the middle term to be the subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor. Work through the reduction: after converting the minor, we get: some B is A, every B is C, therefore some A is C. Now the middle term B is subject of the major premise (second premise) – but wait, we need the middle to be subject of the first premise in first figure. Actually, careful: The first figure's standard order is minor premise first (subject is minor term), then major premise (subject is middle term). After converting the minor, we have minor premise 'some B is A' (subject B, predicate A) and major 'every B is C' (subject B, predicate C). Here B is subject in both, but that is not first figure. Actually we need to reorder premises? The author says 'returns to the first figure by converting the minor premise'. This likely means that by converting the minor, we get a mood where the middle term is the subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor, which is characteristic of first figure when the premises are ordered correctly. Let's see: original minor: some A is B (A subject, B predicate). After conversion: some B is A (B subject, A predicate). Original major: every B is C (B subject, C predicate). Now the premises are: minor: some B is A, major: every B is C. Here B is subject in both, not first figure. So we need to reorder: major premise should be the one where the middle term is subject, and minor premise where middle term is predicate. Actually in first figure, the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor. So we need to swap? Perhaps the reduction involves converting the minor and then swapping premises? The passage does not detail swapping, but it says 'returns to the first figure by converting the minor premise'. Traditional logic: To reduce third figure moods to first figure, one often uses conversion (of either premise) and sometimes premise exchange (transposition). For example, mood Disamis (some P is M, all M is S, therefore some P is S) reduces to Darii by converting the minor premise and exchanging premises. So the step here is to note that conversion of the minor premise yields a first-figure structure after possible reordering. The guided path should acknowledge this. But the passage says 'and that is obvious', so we can keep it simple: converting the minor premise enables the syllogism to fit the first figure's pattern. For pedagogical clarity, we can say: 'Convert the minor premise to get a first-figure arrangement.'
In practice, you may also need to swap the premises after conversion to match the first figure's standard order (minor premise first).
4
Contrast with second figure reduction
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. To reduce to the first figure, one converts the major premise (not the minor). Understanding this contrast helps internalize the rule: the premise to convert depends on the position of the middle term.

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion of the conclusion in the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating the mood: Some A is B, every B is C, therefore some A is C. This conclusion 'some A is C' can be converted simply to 'some C is A'. He then notes that in this third figure, the return to the first figure is done by converting the minor premise (not the major), whereas in the second figure it is done by converting the major premise. The reasoning is that the third figure requires the minor premise to be converted to become the major of the first figure, and similarly for the second figure. This is a technical point about how to reduce moods of other figures to the first figure through conversion.

Conditions and Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Author states the two conditions for the fourth figure and enumerates its eight productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given categorical syllogism is in the fourth figure and verify if its premises satisfy the conditions for productivity, then list the eight valid moods.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's eight moods derive from two simple conditions on quality and quantity of premises.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism is the most restricted in terms of valid combinations. The author lays down exactly two conditions that yield eight productive moods. Understanding these conditions allows you to quickly test any fourth-figure syllogism for validity. We will walk through each condition and enumerate the eight moods with an Islamic example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The passage states two mutually exclusive conditions for productivity: (1) both premises affirmative with a universal minor, or (2) premises differ in quality with at least one universal. Memorize these as the gateway conditions.
Condition (1) yields four moods; condition (2) yields the other four.
2
Enumerate moods from condition (1)
With a universal affirmative minor, the major can be any of the four types (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative). This gives four moods.
The minor must be universal affirmative; the major may vary.
3
Enumerate moods from condition (2)
When premises differ in quality, the possible productive combinations are: particular affirmative minor with universal negative major; universal negative minor with universal affirmative major; particular negative minor with universal affirmative major; and both negative minors (universal and particular) with particular affirmative major. That accounts for the remaining four moods.
In all these, at least one premise is universal.
4
Test a concrete example
Consider the syllogism: 'Every khamr is intoxicating; No intoxicating thing is permissible; Therefore no khamr is permissible.' The minor is universal affirmative, major is universal negative – different quality. This falls under condition (2). It is a valid fourth-figure mood.
The conclusion is a universal negative; the middle term 'intoxicating' is predicate in the minor and subject in the major.

And it is required in the fourth figure, with respect to quality and quantity, one of two things: 1. Either both of them affirmative, i.e., the minor and major are affirmative, with the minor universal. And a section: On the syllogism 2. Or their difference in quality (conjunction on his saying: 'both affirmative'); i.e., the condition of the fourth figure with respect to quality and quantity is one of two things: either both premises affirmative with universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them. So that the universal affirmative minor (produces) with the four major premises, and the particular affirmative minor produces with the universal negative major, and the two negative minors (i.e., universal and particular) produce with the universal affirmative major, and both of them (i.e., the two negative minors, universal and particular) produce with the particular affirmative major.
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage is a matn listing the conditions for the fourth figure and its eight productive moods. The author first gives two possible conditions: (1) both premises affirmative with a universal minor; (2) premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) with at least one universal. Then he enumerates the productive combinations: with a universal affirmative minor, it works with all four major premises (affirmative universal, affirmative particular, negative universal, negative particular) – that gives four moods. With a particular affirmative minor, it only works with a universal negative major – one mood. The two negative minors (universal and particular) work with a universal affirmative major – two moods. And both negative minors also work with a particular affirmative major – one mood. Total 4+1+2+1=8 moods. This matches the standard eight moods of the fourth figure.

Conditions for the Fourth Figure and Its Sterile Cases
Al-'Attar expands on conditions for the fourth figure and gives examples of sterile combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the valid conditions for a fourth-figure categorical syllogism from the three types of invalid (sterile) combinations.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires either both affirmatives with a universal minor, or mixed quality with one universal; otherwise the syllogism is sterile.

Al-'Attar sharpens our understanding of the fourth figure by explaining not only what conditions make it productive but also what happens when they are absent. By dissecting the three sterile cases, you will learn to recognize and avoid inferential dead-ends in your own arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two valid conditions
The first condition is both premises affirmative with the minor premise universal (e.g. 'Every A is B, Every B is C'). The second condition is that the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and at least one premise is universal.
These conditions govern quality and quantity; modality conditions are omitted per the author.
2
Understand the consequence when both conditions are absent
If neither condition is met, three possibilities arise, all sterile (unproductive). Al-'Attar enumerates them: both negative, both affirmative with a particular minor, or differing in quality with both particular.
Sterile means no necessary conclusion follows from the premises.
3
Examine the three sterile cases with examples
Case 1: both negative (e.g., 'No A is B, No C is A'). Case 2: both affirmative with particular minor (e.g., 'Some A is B, Every C is A'). Case 3: mixed quality with both particular (e.g., 'Some A is B, Some C is not A' or 'Some A is not B, Some C is A').
The examples use standard terms like man, horse, donkey, animal, speaker (natiq).
4
Note the omission of modality conditions
Al-'Attar remarks that the author did not mention the five conditions regarding modality (modality of premises) due to their subtlety and length.
This reminds us that fourth-figure conditions are more complex when considering necessary, possible, or impossible premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيُشْتَرَطُ فِي الشَّكْلِ الرَّابع ... إِلَخ أي : يُشترط في إنتاجِ الشَّكلِ الرَّابِعِ بحسب الكيفيَّةِ والكَمِّيَّةِ : إمَّا إيجابُ المقدِّمتَيْن مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ الصُّغرى، وإمَّا اختلافُهُمَا في الكيف معَ كُلِّيَّةِ إحداهما ، إذ لو لم يتحقق أحد الأمرَيْنِ، بل انتَفَيا جميعاً؛ لزمَ أحد الأمورِ الثَّلاثةِ : إِمَّا سلب المقدِّمتَيْنِ ، وإمَّا إيجابُهُما معَ جزئيَّةِ الصُّغرى، وإمَّا اختلافُهُما في الكيف مع كونهما جزئيتين، والكُلُّ عقيم، أمَّا الأَوَّلُ فَكَقَولِنَا : لَا شيءٌ مِنَ الإنسان بفرس، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الحمار أو الصَّاهل بإنسان، وأمَّا الثَّاني فَكَقَولِنَا : بعضُ الحيوان إنسان، وكُلُّ ناطق أو كُلُّ فرسٍ حيوان، وأَمَّا الثَّالث فَكَقَولِنَا في إيجاب الصُّغرى : بعضُ النَّاطِقِ إنسان وبعضُ الحيوان أو بعضُ الفرس ليس بناطق، وفي إيجابِ الكُبرى : بعضُ الإنسان ليس بفرس وبعضُ الحيوان أو بعضُ النَّاطِقِ إنسان، وإنّما لم يذكر شرط الشَّكلِ الرَّابع بحسب الجهة، وهو أمورٌ خمسةٌ؛ لِخَفائها وطول الكلام عليها .
And it is required in the fourth figure... i.e., it is required for the production of the fourth figure, in terms of quality and quantity: either both premises affirmative with universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them. For if neither of the two conditions is realized, but both are absent, then one of three things results: either both premises negative, or both affirmative with the minor particular, or they differ in quality while both are particular, and all are sterile. As for the first (both negative), like our saying: Nothing of man is a horse, and nothing of donkey or neighing is man. As for the second (both affirmative with particular minor), like our saying: Some animal is man, and every speaker or every horse is animal. As for the third (difference with both particular), like our saying in the minor affirmative: Some speaker is man, and some animal or some horse is not speaker; and in the major affirmative: Some man is not a horse, and some animal or some speaker is man. And he only did not mention the condition of the fourth figure regarding modality, which is five things, due to their subtlety and the length of discussion.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by al-'Attar explaining why the two conditions are necessary and giving examples of the three cases that are sterile (non-productive). He first restates the conditions, then says if both are absent, three cases arise: (1) both premises negative, (2) both affirmative with particular minor, (3) differing in quality but both particular. He gives examples for each using terms like 'human', 'horse', 'donkey', 'neighing', 'animal', 'speaker' (natiq). These are standard logical terms. He concludes by noting that the author omitted conditions regarding modality (modality of premises) because they are complex.

Valid Moods of the Fourth Figure in Syllogism
Commentary explains conditions for valid moods in the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which combinations of quality and quantity in minor and major premises produce valid conclusions in the fourth figure.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires either both premises affirmative with a universal minor, or differing in quality with at least one universal.

In the fourth figure, the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor. Not every premise pair works; specific conditions on quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) must be met. This lesson examines those conditions as laid out in the commentary, enabling you to distinguish valid from invalid moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main conditions
The passage states that for the fourth figure, the condition regarding quality and quantity is one of two: either both premises are affirmative and the minor is universal, or the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and at least one of them is universal.
These conditions guarantee that the middle term connects properly.
2
List the valid premise combinations
From the condition, the passage enumerates four valid patterns: (1) universal affirmative minor with any major; (2) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major; (3) universal negative minor with universal affirmative major; (4) particular negative minor with universal affirmative major? Actually check: the text says 'the two negative minors produce with the universal affirmative major' – but then also with particular affirmative major? The error clarifies that only the universal negative minor works with particular affirmative major.
The error and correction refine this list.
3
Verify the invalid combination
The commentary explicitly identifies the combination 'particular negative minor with particular affirmative major' as invalid because the premises differ in quality but neither is universal, violating the second condition.
This would produce a ninth mood, but only eight are accepted.
4
Check an example from the text
The passage provides examples like 'some animal is a man, every rational is an animal' which yields a valid conclusion. Use the conditions to confirm that this pair (particular affirmative minor, universal affirmative major) satisfies the first condition (both affirmative, minor is particular? Actually minor is particular affirmative, major is universal affirmative. Both affirmative, but minor is not universal. So first condition fails. Then second condition: do they differ in quality? No, both affirmative. So this example is not from the fourth figure? Wait – the example is actually for the third figure? The text says 'as for the second, it is like our saying: some animal is a man, and every rational or every horse is an animal' – this is a valid mood of the third figure (Disamis). So careful: the text is giving examples for earlier discussion. For the fourth figure, the conditions are as stated. So I should not use that example. Instead, use the valid fourth figure mood: 'Universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major' gives a particular affirmative conclusion (Bramantip). For example: 'Every jurist is a scholar; every scholar is knowledgeable; therefore some knowledgeable person is a jurist.' This is valid.
This example is not from the text but illustrates the condition.

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ أَوِ اخْتِلَافُهُما بالرَّفع؛ عطفٌ على قوله: «إيجابهما»؛ أي: شرطُ الشَّكلِ الرَّابع بحسب الكيفية والكمية أحد الأمرين : إما إيجاب الصُّغرى والكبرى مع كلِّيَّة الصُّغرى. وإما اختلافهما في الكيف مَعَ كُلِّيَّةِ إِحْدَاهُمَا؛ لِيُنْتِجَ الصُّغرى الْمُوجِبَةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ مَعَ الكبريات الأَرْبَعِ، ولينتج الصُّغرى الموجبة الجُزْئِيَّةُ مَعَ الكبرى السَّالِبَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، ولينتج الصُّغريان السَّالِبتَانِ؛ أي: الكلية والجزئية مَعَ الكبرى الْمُوجَبَةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ، وَ لينتج كِلْتَاهُمَا؛ أي: الصُّغريانِ السَّالبتان الكلية، والجزئية مَعَ الكبرى الْمُوجَبَةِ الجُزْثيَّة. وفي قوله: «كلتاهما غلط فاحش؛ لأنَّ الصُّغرى السالبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية غيرُ مُعتَبَرٍ؛ لاختلافِ مُقدِّمتيه مع عدم كلِّيَّةِ إحداهما، فَلَا يوجدُ فيهِ ما اشتُرِطَ في هذا الشكل من إيجاب المقدمتين. الدسوقي: قَوْلُهُ : الْمُوْجِبَةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ وصفانِ لِلصُّغرى، والصُّغرى فاعلُ ينتج. قَوْلُهُ : أَي : الْكُلِّيَّةُ ... إلخ بالرفع: تفسير للشالبتين. قَوْلُهُ : غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَر أي : معتبر للإنتاج، وأيضاً يلزمُ عليهِ أنْ تكونَ الضُّروبُ المنتجة تسعة مع أنَّها ثمانية. العطار: شيءٌ مِنَ الإنسان بفرس، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الحمار أو الصَّاهل بإنسان، وأمَّا الثَّاني فَكَقَولِنَا: بعضُ الحيوان إنسان، وكُلُّ ناطق أو كُلُّ فرسٍ حيوان، وأَمَّا الثَّالث فَكَقَولِنَا في إيجاب الصُّغرى: بعضُ النَّاطِقِ إنسان وبعضُ الحيوان أو بعضُ الفرس ليس بناطق، وفي إيجابِ الكُبرى: بعضُ الإنسان ليس بفرس وبعضُ الحيوان أو بعضُ النَّاطِقِ إنسان، وإنّما لم يذكر شرط الشَّكلِ الرَّابع بحسب الجهة، وهو أمورٌ خمسةٌ؛ لِخَفائها وطول الكلام عليها. كُلِّيَّة الصُّغرى، أو اختلافهما في الكيف مع كلِّيَّة إحداهما، وأظنُّ أنَّه مع تصحيف. والعبارةُ الصَّحيحة أن يُقالَ: «وكُلِّيَّتهما»؛ أي: وكلّيَّة السَّالبتين مع الموجبة الجزئيَّةِ؛ أي: السَّالبة الكلية مع الموجبة الجزئية. ولعلَّ هذا الغلط نشأ من الناسخ، وإلا؛ فالمصنِّفُ أعظم شأناً من أن يذهب عليه مثلُ السَّهو الصَّريح. مُوجِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ منصوب على أنَّه مفعول لينتج؛ أي: ضروب هذا الشَّكل تنتج موجبةً جزئيةً. الدسوقي: قَوْلُهُ : تَصْحِيْفٌ أي: تحريف. قَوْلُهُ: كُلِّيَّةُ السَّالِبَتَيْن أي: الكُلِّيَّةُ منهما. قَوْلُهُ : هَذَا الْغَلَط أي: لفظ: (كِلْتَاهُما). العطار: قَوْلُهُ : وَالْعِبَارَةُ الصَّحِيحَةُ أَنْ يُقالَ: وَكُلِّيَّتُهُمَا يعني: أنَّ العبارةَ الصَّحيحة وكلّيَّتهما بإرجاع ضميرِ الثَّثنيةِ إلى السَّالبتَيْنِ؛ الكُلِّيَّةِ والجزئية، لكنْ لَمَّا كَانَتِ الصغرى السَّالبةُ الجزئيَّةُ معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الجزئيَّةِ غيرَ مُرادٍ؛ بقيَ مِنَ الاثنين واحدٌ، وهو الصُّغرى السَّالبةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الجزئيّةِ، فَلِذَا؛ فسَّرَ قولَهُ: أي: كُلِّيَّةُ السَّالبتَين معَ الموجبة الجزئية بقولِهِ: أي: السَّالبةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ معَ الموجبة الجزئية، فكانَ قولُ المصنفِ: وَكُلِّيَّتُهُما عاما خصَّ منه البعض، ولا يخفى عليك أنَّ هذا التصحيحَ أيضاً تصحيفٌ، بَل العبارةُ الصَّحيحةُ هَهُنا أن يُقالَ: وَكُلِّيَّتُهَا كَمَا رأيناه كذلكَ في بعض النُّسَخِ الصَّحيحةِ بإفرادِ الضَّميرِ الرَّاجع إلى السالبة فقط؛ أي: كُلِّيَّةِ الصُّغرى السَّالبة معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الجزئية؛ قاله رجب أفندي. قَوْلُهُ: مِثْلُ هَذَا السَّهْوِ الصَّرِيحِ الأَوْلَى: الخطأ الصَّريح؛ لأنَّ السَّهِوَ وَلَو صريحاً؛ لا يُنافي عظم الشَّأْنِ.
Section: On Syllogism. Or their difference in [quality] by nominative; it is an apposition to his statement 'their affirmation'; that is, the condition of the fourth figure with respect to quality and quantity is one of two matters: either affirmation of the minor and major with universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them, so that the universal affirmative minor produces with the four majors, and the particular affirmative minor produces with the universal negative major, and the two negative minors—i.e., the universal and particular—produce with the universal affirmative major, and both of them—i.e., the two negative minors, universal and particular—produce with the particular affirmative major. In his statement 'both of them' there is a gross error, because the particular negative minor with the particular affirmative major is not considered, due to the difference of its two premises together with the absence of universality of either of them, so there does not exist in it what was required in this figure of affirmation of the two premises. Al-Dasuqi: His statement 'the universal affirmative' are two descriptors for the minor, and the minor is the subject of 'produces'. His statement 'that is, the universal...etc.' by nominative is an interpretation of 'the two negatives'. His statement 'not considered' means not considered for production, and also it would necessitate that the productive moods be nine while they are eight. Al-'Attar: Something like 'man is not a horse, and nothing of the donkey or the neighing is a man'; as for the second, it is like our saying 'some animal is a man, and every rational or every horse is an animal'; as for the third, it is like our saying in affirmating the minor: 'some rational is a man and some animal or some horse is not rational', and in affirmating the major: 'some man is not a horse and some animal or some rational is a man'. He only did not mention the condition of the fourth figure with respect to mode—which is five matters—because of their subtlety and length of discourse on them. Universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them, and I think that it is with distortion. The correct expression is that one should say 'and their universality', i.e., the universality of the two negatives with the particular affirmative; i.e., the universal negative [minor] with the particular affirmative major. Perhaps this error arose from the copyist; otherwise, the author is too great to let such an obvious oversight slip by. 'A particular affirmative' is in the accusative as the object of 'produces'; i.e., the moods of this figure produce a particular affirmative. Al-Dasuqi: His statement 'distortion' means alteration. His statement 'universality of the two negatives' means the universal one of them. His statement 'this error' means the word 'both of them'. Al-'Attar: His statement 'and the correct expression is that one should say: and their universality' means that the correct expression is 'and their universality' by referring the dual pronoun to the two negatives—universal and particular—but when the particular negative minor with the particular affirmative major was not intended, only one of the two remained, namely the universal negative minor with the particular affirmative major; therefore he interpreted his statement, i.e., the universality of the two negatives with the particular affirmative, by his statement i.e., the universal negative with the particular affirmative, so the author's statement 'and their universality' was general, from which part was specified. And it is not hidden from you that this correction is also a distortion; rather, the correct expression here is that one should say 'and its universality', as we saw it thus in some correct copies with the pronoun singular referring to the negative only; i.e., the universality of the negative minor with the particular affirmative major; said by Rajab Afandi. His statement 'such an obvious oversight'—the preferable [expression] is 'obvious error', because oversight, even if obvious, does not contradict greatness of stature.
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered commentary on the conditions for the fourth figure of categorical syllogism. It begins by stating that the condition for the fourth figure regarding quality (ījāb/salb) and quantity (kulliyya/juz'iyya) is one of two things: either both premises are affirmative and the minor premise is universal, or the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and at least one of them is universal. The passage then lists which combinations of minor and major premises produce a valid conclusion: a universal affirmative minor with any of the four major types; a particular affirmative minor with a universal negative major; both negative minors (universal and particular) with a universal affirmative major; and both negative minors with a particular affirmative major. The text identifies a gross error in the phrase 'كلتاهما' (both of them) when used for the last case, because a particular negative minor with a particular affirmative major does not meet the condition (difference in quality but neither universal). Al-Dasuqi comments that such a pair is not considered productive, and if it were, there would be nine productive moods instead of eight. Al-'Attar then provides examples for the valid moods, such as 'some animal is a man, every rational is an animal' for the first mood, and explains why the condition regarding mode (jiha) was omitted. The discussion then turns to a textual correction: the author's phrase 'كلتاهما' should be 'وكلّيتهما' meaning the universality of the two negatives with the particular affirmative, but since only the universal negative minor is valid, the correct wording is 'وكلّيتها' (its universality) referring to the negative minor only, as found in some manuscripts. This is a critical refinement typical of a hāshiya.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which combination of premises is invalid in the fourth figure?
A) Universal affirmative minor + Universal affirmative major
B) Particular affirmative minor + Universal negative major
C) Particular negative minor + Particular affirmative major
D) Universal negative minor + Particular affirmative major

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure requires either both premises affirmative with a universal minor, or differing in quality with at least one universal premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the premises 'Some witness is truthful' (particular affirmative) and 'Every truthful person is trustworthy' (universal affirmative). Determine if they form a valid fourth figure syllogism. If so, state the conclusion; if not, explain which condition is violated.
?
Consider the pair 'No animal is rational' (universal negative) and 'Some rational being is a human' (particular affirmative). Check if this satisfies the fourth figure conditions. If not, explain why.

The Syllogism

Textual Error in the Fourth Figure Conditions
Commentary corrects a textual error in the conditions for the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the textual error in the phrase 'كلتاهما' and explain the correct reading 'وكلّيتها' based on logical requirements.

Core Idea
A single word error in the conditions would admit an invalid mood, increasing the number of valid moods from eight to nine.

Even authoritative logical texts can contain scribal errors. Here, the commentators correct a mistake in the phrasing of the conditions for the fourth figure. The word 'كلتاهما' (both of them) incorrectly suggests that both the universal and particular negative minors can combine with a particular affirmative major, when only the universal negative minor works. This lesson traces the error and its correction across multiple layers of commentary.

Guided Path
1
Locate the erroneous phrase in the Arabic
In the passage, the author writes 'و لينتج كِلْتَاهُمَا' meaning 'and both of them produce'. The commentators point out that this is a gross error (غلط فاحش) because it includes the invalid combination of particular negative minor with particular affirmative major.
The error is in the word 'كلتاهما' which should be corrected.
2
Understand why the combination is invalid
The condition for valid fourth figure moods when premises differ in quality requires that at least one premise be universal. The pair 'particular negative minor + particular affirmative major' violates this because both are particular. Thus it should not be listed as productive.
If it were valid, there would be nine moods instead of eight.
3
Trace the correction through the commentaries
Al-Dasuqi first notes that 'كلتاهما' causes the number of moods to become nine. Al-'Attar then suggests replacing it with 'وكلّيتهما' (their universality), but that still implies both negatives are universal. Finally, Rajab Afandi recommends 'وكلّيتها' (its universality) with a singular pronoun referring only to the negative minor that is actually universal.
The final correct wording restricts the condition to the universal negative minor.
4
Explain the logical reason for the correction
Only the universal negative minor (with the particular affirmative major) satisfies the condition that when premises differ in quality, one must be universal. The particular negative minor does not. Therefore, the phrase must refer to the single universal negative minor, not both.
This shows how logical scrutiny can reveal textual errors.

الموجبة الجزئية، فكانَ قولُ المصنفِ: وَكُلِّيَّتُهُما عاما خصَّ منه البعض، ولا يخفى عليك أنَّ هذا التصحيحَ أيضاً تصحيفٌ، بَل العبارةُ الصَّحيحةُ هَهُنا أن يُقالَ: وَكُلِّيَّتُهَا كَمَا رأيناه كذلكَ في بعض النُّسَخِ الصَّحيحةِ بإفرادِ الضَّميرِ الرَّاجع إلى السالبة فقط؛ أي: كُلِّيَّةِ الصُّغرى السَّالبة معَ الكُبرى الموجبة الجزئية؛ قاله رجب أفندي. قَوْلُهُ: مِثْلُ هَذَا السَّهْوِ الصَّرِيحِ الأَوْلَى: الخطأ الصَّريح؛ لأنَّ السَّهِوَ وَلَو صريحاً؛ لا يُنافي عظم الشَّأْنِ.
Section: On Syllogism. Or their difference in [quality] by nominative; it is an apposition to his statement 'their affirmation'; that is, the condition of the fourth figure with respect to quality and quantity is one of two matters: either affirmation of the minor and major with universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them, so that the universal affirmative minor produces with the four majors, and the particular affirmative minor produces with the universal negative major, and the two negative minors—i.e., the universal and particular—produce with the universal affirmative major, and both of them—i.e., the two negative minors, universal and particular—produce with the particular affirmative major. In his statement 'both of them' there is a gross error, because the particular negative minor with the particular affirmative major is not considered, due to the difference of its two premises together with the absence of universality of either of them, so there does not exist in it what was required in this figure of affirmation of the two premises. Al-Dasuqi: His statement 'the universal affirmative' are two descriptors for the minor, and the minor is the subject of 'produces'. His statement 'that is, the universal...etc.' by nominative is an interpretation of 'the two negatives'. His statement 'not considered' means not considered for production, and also it would necessitate that the productive moods be nine while they are eight. Al-'Attar: Something like 'man is not a horse, and nothing of the donkey or the neighing is a man'; as for the second, it is like our saying 'some animal is a man, and every rational or every horse is an animal'; as for the third, it is like our saying in affirmating the minor: 'some rational is a man and some animal or some horse is not rational', and in affirmating the major: 'some man is not a horse and some animal or some rational is a man'. He only did not mention the condition of the fourth figure with respect to mode—which is five matters—because of their subtlety and length of discourse on them. Universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with universality of one of them, and I think that it is with distortion. The correct expression is that one should say 'and their universality', i.e., the universality of the two negatives with the particular affirmative; i.e., the universal negative [minor] with the particular affirmative major. Perhaps this error arose from the copyist; otherwise, the author is too great to let such an obvious oversight slip by. 'A particular affirmative' is in the accusative as the object of 'produces'; i.e., the moods of this figure produce a particular affirmative. Al-Dasuqi: His statement 'distortion' means alteration. His statement 'universality of the two negatives' means the universal one of them. His statement 'this error' means the word 'both of them'. Al-'Attar: His statement 'and the correct expression is that one should say: and their universality' means that the correct expression is 'and their universality' by referring the dual pronoun to the two negatives—universal and particular—but when the particular negative minor with the particular affirmative major was not intended, only one of the two remained, namely the universal negative minor with the particular affirmative major; therefore he interpreted his statement, i.e., the universality of the two negatives with the particular affirmative, by his statement i.e., the universal negative with the particular affirmative, so the author's statement 'and their universality' was general, from which part was specified. And it is not hidden from you that this correction is also a distortion; rather, the correct expression here is that one should say 'and its universality', as we saw it thus in some correct copies with the pronoun singular referring to the negative only; i.e., the universality of the negative minor with the particular affirmative major; said by Rajab Afandi. His statement 'such an obvious oversight'—the preferable [expression] is 'obvious error', because oversight, even if obvious, does not contradict greatness of stature.
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered commentary on the conditions for the fourth figure of categorical syllogism. It begins by stating that the condition for the fourth figure regarding quality (ījāb/salb) and quantity (kulliyya/juz'iyya) is one of two things: either both premises are affirmative and the minor premise is universal, or the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and at least one of them is universal. The passage then lists which combinations of minor and major premises produce a valid conclusion: a universal affirmative minor with any of the four major types; a particular affirmative minor with a universal negative major; both negative minors (universal and particular) with a universal affirmative major; and both negative minors with a particular affirmative major. The text identifies a gross error in the phrase 'كلتاهما' (both of them) when used for the last case, because a particular negative minor with a particular affirmative major does not meet the condition (difference in quality but neither universal). Al-Dasuqi comments that such a pair is not considered productive, and if it were, there would be nine productive moods instead of eight. Al-'Attar then provides examples for the valid moods, such as 'some animal is a man, every rational is an animal' for the first mood, and explains why the condition regarding mode (jiha) was omitted. The discussion then turns to a textual correction: the author's phrase 'كلتاهما' should be 'وكلّيتهما' meaning the universality of the two negatives with the particular affirmative, but since only the universal negative minor is valid, the correct wording is 'وكلّيتها' (its universality) referring to the negative minor only, as found in some manuscripts. This is a critical refinement typical of a hāshiya.

The Syllogism

The Fourth Figure: Conditions and Productive Moods
Al-Dasuqi explains the conditions for the fourth figure and counts eight productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the eight productive moods of the fourth figure and explain why it never produces a universal affirmative conclusion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's productivity requires the minor to be universal and one premise universal; it never yields a universal affirmative.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar lay out the conditions for valid fourth-figure syllogisms. Understanding these conditions allows you to quickly determine which mood combinations yield a conclusion and which do not. This is essential for verifying syllogistic arguments in Islamic logic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Al-Dasuqi states that the minor premise must be universal, and at least one premise must be universal. If both premises are negative, no conclusion follows.
These conditions are summarized as 'minor universal' and 'one premise universal'.
2
Test minor premise types
For each of the four possible minors (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative), check which majors satisfy the conditions. Al-'Attar provides the detailed breakdown.
For example, a universal affirmative minor produces with any major; a particular affirmative minor only produces with a universal negative major.
3
Count productive combinations
Out of 16 possible mood pairs (4 minors × 4 majors), exactly eight satisfy the conditions and are productive. Verify using al-'Attar's enumeration.
The eight productive moods include all combinations where the conditions hold.
4
Understand the missing universal affirmative
The fourth figure never produces a universal affirmative conclusion. The example 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational is a human' shows that the conclusion cannot be universal because the minor term is more specific than the major.
Al-Harawi's example illustrates why the conclusion is particular, not universal.
5
Note the historical disagreement
Earlier scholars (e.g., author of al-Sullam) counted only five productive moods; later scholars count eight. The difference arises from stricter conditions in the earlier count.
This passage reconciles the two views by clarifying the conditions.

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ (إِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ) في المقدِّمتين (سَلْبٌ). (وَإِلَّا ) أي : وإن كان في المقدمتين سَلْبٌ (فَسَالبَةٌ)؛ أي: ينتج سالبة. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِلَّا ؛ فَسَالِبَةٌ) اعلمْ أنَّ الحاصل : أَنَّ ضروبَ الشَّكلِ الرَّابِعِ ثمانية؛ مأخوذة من كلامه على الصَّحيح، والغير مُنتجةٍ : ثمانية . وبيان ذلكَ أنَّنا اشترطنا في الأمر الأوّل: كُلَّيَّة الصُّغرى، فلو لم تكن الصُّغرى كُلِّيَّةٌ بأنْ كانَت جزئيّةً مُوجبةً ؛ لا تنتج ؛ سواء كانت الكُبرى مُوجبةٌ كُلِّيَّةً أو جزئية . واشترطنا في الأمرِ الثَّاني : أن تكون إحداهما كُلِّيَّةٌ، فلو كانتا مختلفتين في الكيف، ولم تكن إحداهما كُلِّيَّةٌ بأن كانت الأولى مُوجبةٌ جزئيَّة، والثانية سالبة جزئية أو العكس؛ فلا تنتج في هاتين الصُّورتين. مجموع الأمرين، قلتُ فيهما : إمَّا موجبتان أو مختلفتان في الكيف، فلو لم يكن كذلك بأن كانتا سالبتين؛ فلا ينتج ؛ سواء كانتا كُلِّيَّتين أو جزئيتين، أو الأولى سالبةً كُلّيَّة، والثانية سالبة جزئيةً أو العكس، فلا ينتج في هذه الأربعة. فالحاصلُ : أنَّ المنتج ثمانية، وغيرَهُ ثمانية، وَمَا في يس فاسد. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ سَلْبُ وَإِلَّا ؛ فَسَالِبَةٌ) محصِّلُهُ أنَّهُ ينتج ما عدا الإيجابَ الكُلِّيَّ، قالَ الهروي : وإِنَّما لم ينتجه لجواز أنْ يكونَ الأصغرُ أعمَّ مِنَ الأكبرِ وامتناعِ حَمْلِ الأخصّ على كُلِّ أفرادِ الأعمّ كَقَولِنَا : كُلّ إنسانٍ حيوانٌ وَكُلَّ ناطقٍ إنسانٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : وما في يس... إلخ) لا يخفى ما في عبارته أيضاً، فالأولى أن يقال: إن الصغرى إن كانت موجبة كلية أنتجت مع الكبريات الأربع لتحقق أحد الشرطين، وإن كانت موجبة جزئية أنتجت مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية فقط لتحقق الشرط الثاني، ولا تنتج مع الثلاثة الباقية لتخلف الشرطين معاً وإن كانت سالبة كلية أنتجت مع الكبرى الموجبة بقسميها لتحقق الشرط الثاني ولا تنتج مع الكبرى السالبة بقسميها لتخلف الشرطين وإن كانت سالبة جزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية فقط لتحقق الشرط الثاني ولا تنتج مع الثلاثة الباقية لتخلف الشرطين معاً وبذلك يتضح جلياً أن المنتج ثمانية وغير المنتج ثمانية وهذا عند المتأخرين، وعند المتقدمين المنتج خمسة فقط، وعليها صاحب السلم حيث يقول : ورابع بخمسة قد أنتجـا ورغبة في الاختصار نكلك إلى العطار لينكشف لك موجب هذا الخلاف . أنتجت وغير ما ذكرته لن ينتجـا
Section on syllogism: (If there is no) negation in the two premises. (And if there is) i.e., if there is negation in the two premises, (then it is negative) i.e., it produces a negative. al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'And if there is, then negative' know that the result is that the moods of the fourth figure are eight, taken from his speech correctly, and the non-productive are eight. Explanation: We stipulated in the first condition: universality of the minor. So if the minor is not universal but particular affirmative, it does not produce, whether the major is universal affirmative or particular. And we stipulated in the second condition: that one of them is universal. So if they differ in quality and neither is universal, e.g., first particular affirmative and second particular negative or vice versa, it does not produce in these two forms. The sum of the two conditions: either both affirmative or differing in quality. So if they are both negative, it does not produce, whether both universal or both particular, or first universal negative and second particular negative or vice versa, so it does not produce in these four. So the productive are eight, and the non-productive are eight. And what is in [the verse] 'Ya Sin' is corrupt. al-'Attar: His statement: 'If there is no negation... and if not, then negative' summary: It produces everything except the universal affirmative. al-Harawi said: It does not produce it because the minor may be more general than the major and it is impossible to predicate the more specific to all individuals of the more general, as in our saying: Every human is an animal and every rational is a human. (His statement: 'And what is in Ya Sin... etc.') It is not hidden that there is also corruption in his expression. Rather, it should be said: If the minor is universal affirmative, it produces with all four majors because one of the two conditions is fulfilled. If it is particular affirmative, it produces only with the universal negative major because the second condition is fulfilled, and does not produce with the remaining three due to both conditions being absent. If it is universal negative, it produces with both affirmative majors because the second condition is fulfilled, and does not produce with both negative majors due to both conditions being absent. If it is particular negative, it produces only with the universal affirmative major because the second condition is fulfilled, and does not produce with the remaining three due to both conditions being absent. By this it is clearly evident that the productive are eight and the non-productive are eight. This is according to the later scholars. According to the earlier scholars, the productive are only five, and upon this is the author of al-Sullam when he says: 'And the fourth has produced five...' Desiring conciseness, we refer you to al-'Attar to uncover the reason for this disagreement. It produced, and what I have mentioned will not produce.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the rules for the fourth figure of the syllogism. Al-Dasuqi begins by stating the two conditions for productivity: (1) the minor premise must be universal, and (2) at least one premise must be universal. He then enumerates the combinations that fail these conditions, concluding that eight moods are productive and eight are non-productive. Al-'Attar adds further clarification, noting that the fourth figure never produces a universal affirmative conclusion. He provides a detailed breakdown of which minor premise types produce with which major premises, again confirming eight productive moods. He also notes a disagreement between later and earlier scholars: the earlier scholars recognized only five productive moods, as reflected in al-Sullam's versification. The passage uses an example from al-Harawi: 'Every human is an animal and every rational is a human' to illustrate why the universal affirmative is not produced (as the minor 'rational' is more specific than the major 'animal'). This example, though philosophical, is rooted in the Islamic tradition of logic.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Fourth Figure Conditions and Productive Moods
Explains conclusion rule and enumerates productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given fourth-figure categorical syllogism is productive and identify its valid conclusion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure always yields a particular conclusion and requires specific premise conditions.

In this passage, the author outlines the general rule for conclusions in the fourth figure and identifies which moods are productive. You will learn to apply the conditions of universality and quality to quickly evaluate any fourth-figure syllogism. This skill is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Check that the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. This is the definition of the fourth figure.
Example: Minor: 'All intoxicants are forbidden' – middle term 'intoxicants' is predicate. Major: 'All alcoholic beverages are intoxicants' – middle term is subject.
2
Verify the minor premise is universal
The author states the first condition: the minor premise must be universal (affirmative or negative). If it is particular, the syllogism is not productive.
Check whether the minor premise begins with 'All' or 'No' (universal).
3
Apply the quality conditions
The premises cannot both be negative. Also, if they differ in quality, at least one must be universal. These conditions ensure a valid conclusion.
Remember: both negative = no production; both particular and differing quality = no production.
4
Determine the conclusion type
If neither premise is negative, the conclusion is affirmative; otherwise, it is negative. The conclusion is always particular (beginning with 'Some').
Even with two universal premises, the conclusion is particular due to the arrangement of terms.
5
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion has the major term as subject and the minor term as predicate. Write it as 'Some [major term] is/is not [minor term]'.
From the example: 'All intoxicants are forbidden' (minor) and 'All alcoholic beverages are intoxicants' (major) yields 'Some forbidden things are alcoholic beverages'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ (إِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ) في المقدِّمتين (سَلْبٌ). (وَإِلَّا ) أي : وإن كان في المقدمتين سَلْبٌ (فَسَالبَةٌ)؛ أي: ينتج سالبة. اعلم أنَّ الحاصل : أَنَّ ضروبَ الشَّكلِ الرَّابِعِ ثمانية؛ مأخوذة من كلامه على الصَّحيح، والغير مُنتجةٍ : ثمانية . وبيان ذلكَ أنَّنا اشترطنا في الأمر الأوّل: كُلَّيَّة الصُّغرى، فلو لم تكن الصُّغرى كُلِّيَّةٌ بأنْ كانَت جزئيّةً مُوجبةً ؛ لا تنتج ؛ سواء كانت الكُبرى مُوجبةٌ كُلِّيَّةً أو جزئية . واشترطنا في الأمرِ الثَّاني : أن تكون إحداهما كُلِّيَّةٌ، فلو كانتا مختلفتين في الكيف، ولم تكن إحداهما كُلِّيَّةٌ بأن كانت الأولى مُوجبةٌ جزئيَّة، والثانية سالبة جزئية أو العكس؛ فلا تنتج في هاتين الصُّورتين مجموع الأمرين، قلتُ فيهما : إمَّا موجبتان أو مختلفتان في الكيف، فلو لم يكن كذلك بأن كانتا سالبتين؛ فلا ينتج ؛ سواء كانتا كُلِّيَّتين أو جزئيتين، أو الأولى سالبةً كُلّيَّة، والثانية سالبة جزئيةً أو العكس، فلا ينتج في هذه الأربعة. فالحاصلُ : أنَّ المنتج ثمانية، وغيرَهُ ثمانية. ثم الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة ينتج موجبةٌ جزئيَّةٌ، كَقولنا : كلُّ (ب) (ج)، وكلُّ (أ) (ب)، فبعض (ج) (أ). والصُّغرى الموجبة الكلّيَّة مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية، تنتج موجبةٌ جزئيَّةً ، كقولنا : «كلّ (ب) (ج) ، وبعض (أ) (ب)، فبعض (ج) (أ)» . والصغرى الموجبة الكلّيَّة مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية، تنتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا : كلُّ (ب) (ج) ، ولا شيء من (أ) (ب)، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)».
Chapter: On Syllogism. If there is no negation in the two premises, then affirmative; otherwise, negative, i.e., it produces a negative. Know that the summary is that the moods of the fourth figure are eight, taken from his statement correctly, and the non-productive are eight. The explanation of that is that we stipulated in the first condition: universality of the minor premise; if the minor premise is not universal, being particular affirmative, it does not produce, whether the major is universal affirmative or particular. And we stipulated in the second condition: that one of them be universal; if they differ in quality and neither is universal, such that the first is particular affirmative and the second particular negative or vice versa, then there is no production in these two forms. I said regarding them: either two affirmatives or differing in quality. If it is not so, such that both are negative, then no production, whether both universal or particular, or the first universal negative and the second particular negative or vice versa, so no production in these four. Thus the summary: the productive are eight, and the non-productive are eight. Then the minor universal affirmative with the major affirmative produces a particular affirmative, as we say: All B is C, and all A is B, so some C is A. And the minor universal affirmative with the major particular affirmative produces a particular affirmative, as we say: All B is C, and some A is B, so some C is A. And the minor universal affirmative with the major universal negative produces a particular negative, as we say: All B is C, and no A is B, so some C is not A.
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This passage deals with the syllogism of the fourth figure. It first states a general rule: if there is no negative premise in the two premises, the conclusion is affirmative; if there is a negative premise, the conclusion is negative. Then it explains that the moods of the fourth figure are sixteen in total, eight productive and eight non-productive. The conditions for productiveness are two: first, the minor premise must be universal; second, the two premises must not both be negative and must not both be particular when they differ in quality. The author then enumerates three of the productive moods as examples: (1) minor universal affirmative with major universal affirmative, producing a particular affirmative (e.g., all humans are animals, all rational beings are humans, therefore some animals are rational); (2) minor universal affirmative with major particular affirmative, also producing a particular affirmative (e.g., all humans are animals, some rational beings are humans, therefore some animals are rational); (3) minor universal affirmative with major universal negative, producing a particular negative (e.g., all humans are animals, no stone is a human, therefore some animals are not stone). This shows how the fourth figure always yields a particular conclusion, never universal, because the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major.

Key Terms
شكل رابع (shakl raabi'): The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. [Note: Later logicians recognized more productive moods in the fourth figure than earlier ones; the text mentions this dispute.]
منتج (muntij): A syllogistic mood that yields a valid conclusion necessarily.
صغرى (sughraa): The minor premise of a categorical syllogism, which contains the minor term (subject of the conclusion).
كبرى (kubraa): The major premise of a categorical syllogism, which contains the major term (predicate of the conclusion).
موجبة جزئية (muujiba juz'iyya): A particular affirmative proposition: 'Some S is P'.
سالبة جزئية (saaliba juz'iyya): A particular negative proposition: 'Some S is not P'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fourth figure, if the minor premise is 'All intoxicants are forbidden' (universal affirmative) and the major premise is 'No alcoholic beverage is an intoxicant' (universal negative), what type of conclusion is produced?
A) All forbidden things are not alcoholic beverages (universal negative).
B) Some forbidden things are not alcoholic beverages (particular negative).
C) No forbidden things are alcoholic beverages (universal negative).
D) Some alcoholic beverages are not forbidden (particular negative).

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure always gives a particular conclusion, and its productivity depends on the minor premise being universal and the premises not both being negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Four Valid Moods of the First Figure (Part 1)
Author enumerates first four valid moods with examples and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four valid moods of the first figure categorical syllogism and determine their conclusions based on premise types.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative minor in the first figure always yields a particular conclusion.

This passage from the commentary on al-Akhdari's logical primer enumerates the four valid moods of the first figure, where the minor premise is universal affirmative and the major varies. It demonstrates that the conclusion must be particular due to material considerations, even when both premises are universal. Understanding these moods is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed minor premise
In all four moods, the minor premise is universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every B is J' or 'Every human is an animal'). This is the stable element across the moods.
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion.
2
Observe the variations in the major premise
The major premise can be universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative. Each combination defines a distinct mood.
The major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion.
3
Determine the conclusion for each combination
Regardless of the major premise's quantity and quality, the conclusion is always particular: either particular affirmative or particular negative. The author emphasizes that the conclusion cannot be universal because the universal would be false in the given material examples.
For example, with major universal affirmative, the conclusion is particular affirmative ('some J is A') not universal affirmative ('every J is A').
4
Test with concrete examples from the text
The concrete examples (human, animal, rational, stone) illustrate why universal conclusions fail materially. For instance, 'every animal is rational' is false, so the conclusion must be 'some animal is rational'.
The commentator explicitly states: 'because it is not valid in the mentioned example: every animal is rational.'
5
Verify alignment with first figure rules
In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. All four moods satisfy this, confirming their validity as first-figure syllogisms.
The moods also satisfy the general rules of categorical syllogisms (e.g., distributed middle, no illicit process).

(قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ ب ج ، وَبَعْضُ أ لَيْسَ (ب) أي كلُّ إنسان حيوان وبعضُ الحيوان ليس بإنسان . (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ ج لَيْسَ (أ) أي : فبعض الحيوان ليس بحجر
Either universal or particular. The universal affirmative minor with the affirmative (universal) major produces a particular affirmative, as our saying: Every B is J, and every A is B, therefore some J is A. And the universal affirmative minor with the particular affirmative major produces a particular affirmative, as our saying: Every B is J, and some A is B, therefore some J is A. And the universal affirmative minor with the universal negative major produces a particular negative, as our saying: Every B is J, and no A is B, therefore some J is not A. (His saying: 'Either universal') i.e., in one mood, which is composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major. (His saying: 'Every B is J, and every A is B') i.e., every human is an animal, and every rational is a human. (His saying: 'Therefore some J is A') i.e., therefore some animal is rational. (His saying: 'A particular affirmative') i.e., because it is not valid in the mentioned example: every animal is rational. (His saying: 'Therefore some J is A') i.e., therefore some rational is human. (His saying: 'Produces a particular negative') i.e., and it does not produce a universal, because it is not valid in some matters, as when it is said in this example: every human is an animal, and no horse is a human, therefore some animal is not a horse, and it is not valid: every animal is not a horse. (His saying: 'Every B is J, and no A is B') i.e., every human is an animal, and no stone is a human. (His saying: 'Therefore some J is not A') i.e., therefore some animal is not a stone.

Chapter on Syllogism
And the universal affirmative minor with the particular negative major produces a particular negative, as our saying: Every B is J, and some A is not B, therefore some J is not A. So these are four moods understood from his saying: 'That which the universal affirmative produces with the four.' (His saying: 'Every B is J, and some A is not B') i.e., every human is an animal and some animal is not human. (His saying: 'Therefore some J is not A') i.e., therefore some animal is not stone.
Source pages p. 832

Commentary
This passage defines the first four valid moods of the first figure of categorical syllogism. The author enumerates each mood with a symbolic example using variables (B, J, A) and then a concrete example from everyday observation. The first mood: universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major gives a particular affirmative conclusion. The example: every human is an animal, every rational being is a human, therefore some animal is rational. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that the conclusion is particular because the universal 'every animal is rational' is false in reality. The second mood: universal affirmative minor with particular affirmative major gives particular affirmative. The example: every human is an animal, some rational being is human, therefore some animal is rational. The third mood: universal affirmative minor with universal negative major gives particular negative. The example: every human is an animal, no stone is human, therefore some animal is not stone. The fourth mood: universal affirmative minor with particular negative major gives particular negative. The example: every human is an animal, some stone is not human, therefore some animal is not stone. The commentator clarifies that the conclusions cannot be universal due to material considerations. This shows a practical awareness that logical form alone does not guarantee universal conclusions when the matter (subject matter) does not support it. All examples use familiar objects (human, animal, rational, stone, horse) to illustrate the logical relations.

Key Terms
صغرى (sughra): The minor premise in a syllogism, containing the subject of the conclusion. [Note: Stable; no shift.]
كبرى (kubra): The major premise in a syllogism, containing the predicate of the conclusion. [Note: Stable; no shift.]
موجبة (mujiba): An affirmative proposition (subject-predicate relationship affirmed). [Note: Stable; no shift.]
سالبة (saliba): A negative proposition (subject-predicate relationship denied). [Note: Stable; no shift.]
كلية (kulliya): A universal proposition referring to all individuals of the subject term. [Note: Stable; no shift.]
جزئية (juz'iya): A particular proposition referring to some individuals of the subject term. [Note: Stable; no shift.]
ضرب (darb): A mood of a syllogism, i.e., a specific combination of quantity and quality of premises. [Note: Stable; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first mood example (كل إنسان حيوان، كل ناطق إنسان), why is the conclusion 'some animal is rational' rather than 'every animal is rational'?
A) Because the premises are not both universal.
B) Because the universal conclusion 'every animal is rational' is false in reality.
C) Because the middle term is not distributed.
D) Because the first figure always yields particular conclusions.

Key Takeaway
First figure syllogisms with a universal affirmative minor always produce particular conclusions when the major premise is varied, because universal conclusions may be materially false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Construct a first-figure categorical syllogism using Islamic legal terms: take 'every intoxicant is forbidden' as a major premise, and 'every nabidh is an intoxicant' as a minor premise. Determine the mood and whether the conclusion is universal or particular. Then check if the universal conclusion 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is materially true (note: it is, but the exercise is to see the logical form).
?
2. In the passage, the author uses the example ' every human is an animal, some stone is not human, therefore some animal is not stone.' Explain why the conclusion cannot be 'every animal is not stone' (i.e., no animal is stone). Use the commentator's reasoning about material falsehood.
